Hedging and Boosting in Media Discourse: a Comparative Analysis of the Two   

Features in English and Arabic Newspaper Editorials 

Master’s Dissertation 

Field of Study: 45.04.02 “Linguistics”

Statement  

The researcher clearly declares that all the parts of research were written and designed by 

the researcher himself. Therefore, anyone who wants to cite or reference any part  or the whole 

research  should  follow  citation  rules  and  conventions.  Moreover,  the  researcher  used  some 

artificial intelligence (AI) software applications such as DeepSeek and ChatGPT to paraphrase and 

summarize some difficult texts and long paragraphs that originated in other resources especially 

to write the introduction, review of the literature, and conclusion chapters. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
Abstract 

Hedging and boosting, as two rhetorical, communicative, and metadiscursive features, have been 

demonstrated to play an effective persuasive and argumentative role in the genre of newspaper 

editorials (NEs). The purpose of the present research is to compare and contrast the use of such 

features in media discourse, namely in English newspaper editorials (ENEs) and Arabic newspaper 

editorials (ANEs) and consider the similarities and differences in the frequency, form, and function 

of such features in the two corpora. Theoretically, the research aims to examine whether the factor 

sociocultural context where these NEs were written affect the use of such features in the present 

two  corpora.  To  this  end,  40  NEs  (20  ENEs  and  20  ANEs)  were  randomly  selected  from  the 

electronic sections of the leading international and national newspapers published in the Anglo-

American and the Arab World in the period between 1 January and 1 August, 2021. Depending on 

Gee’s  theoretical  cultural  model  of  linguistic  features  (2011,  2012),  Fraser’s  (2010)  analytical 

model, Hyland’s (1996) definition of English hedging, and Hyland’s (2005) definition of boosting, 

these NEs were thoroughly read in order to identify quantitatively and qualitatively the linguistic 

structures that are considered as hedging or boosting features  with the help of corpus linguistic 

methodology. The findings of descriptive statistical analysis showed that the English newspaper 

editors  (ENErs)  used  hedging  significantly  more  frequently  than  the  Arab  newspaper  editors 

(ANErs).  On  average,  the  ENErs  used  48.94  tokens  of  hedging  PTW  (per  a  thousand  word), 

whereas the ANErs used 34.08 tokens PTW. In fact, the ENErs, in the present research, hedged 

their  propositions  approximately  twice  more  often  than  their  Arab  counterparts.  Moreover,  the 

ENErs  used  totally  169  tokens  of  boosting.  On  average,  the  ENEs  contained  10.57  tokens  of 

boosting  PTW.  However,  the  ANErs  used  182  tokens  of  boosting,  i.e.  ANEs  contained  12.95 

tokens of boosting PTW. In other words, the ANErs boosted their propositions more often than 

their English counterparts. The functional analysis of the use of hedging features showed that the 

ENErs and ANErs utilized hedging categories to modify their claims, indicate imprecision, avoid 

categorical commitment, achieve politeness, and indicate modesty. Similarly, both the ENErs and 

ANErs  seemed  to  use  hedging  to  establish  journalistic  reputation,  to  avoid  direct  criticism,  to 

interact with the reader, to present their claims as opinions rather than facts, and finally, to develop 

argumentation  and  achieve  persuasion.  The  findings  also  showed  that  the  ENErs  and  ANErs 

seemed to use boosting to develop argumentation and achieving Persuasion, to establish authority 

and power, to engage the reader, to establish conviction, and to obey cultural norms and models. 

 
 
One major reason of the similarities and differences between the ENErs and ANErs in terms of 

usage of hedging and boosting provided by discourse analysts was the different pattern of cultural 

models which each group of newspaper editors (NErs) adopted in their socio-cultural contexts to 

achieve  various  rhetorical,  persuasive,  social,  or  ideological  purposes.  The  findings  of  present 

research was aimed to contribute to such areas as designing syllabi, materials development, and 

ESP (English for Specific Purposes, EAP (English for Academic Purposes), or even AAP (Arabic 

for Academic Purposes) instruction. The findings was argued to increase the awareness of press 

and  media  students,  translators,  and  ESP  (English  for  Specific  Purposes),  EAP  (English  for 

Academic Purposes), AAP (Arabic for Academic Purposes) instructors about the socio-cultural, 

rhetorical,  communicative,  and  metadiscursive  functions  and  characteristics  of  hedging  and 

boosting in newspaper discourse in general and in ENEs or ANEs in particular.

     Introduction 

This  chapter  presents  the  introduction  of  the  research,  which  includes  the  preview  and 

theoretical background that locate the present research in the relevant literature. Afterwards, the 

chapter presents the hypothesis the research assumes; the problem it addresses, and its objectives. 

Following this, the significance of the research to justify its importance and the research questions 

are provided. Finally, the chapter concludes with the structure of the research.   

1.1 Research Background 

1.1.1 Research Preview 

Writing is one channel of communication, and journalistic writing as one form of written 

discourse involves an interpersonal relationship between the writer and the reader in academic or 

similar-to-academic  discourse  governed  by  certain  conventions  (Dafouz-Milne,  2008).  Thus, 

writers or speakers attempt to attract their hearer(s) or reader(s) attention in a dialogic interaction 

and participation in the text. To achieve this objective, writers or speakers should use the most 

appropriate linguistic and rhetorical strategies or features (e.g., hedging and boosting). Discourse 

analysts distinguish between two levels of language: Discourse and metadiscourse. According to 

Hyland (2005a), metadiscourse refers to the way writers or speakers project themselves in their 

texts to interact with their receivers. It is a concept which is based on a view of writing or speaking 

as a social engagement. Metadiscourse is “discourse about discourse” in which writers or speakers 

demonstrate their interaction with their receivers in the text through using multiple metadiscoursal 

features  (Crismore,  Markkanen,  &  Steffensen,  1993).  Similarly,  discourse  analysts  distinguish 

between  two  main  categories  of  metadiscourse  in  written  texts:  Interactive  metadiscourse  and 

interactional (interpersonal) metadiscourse. Interactive metadiscourse features “primarily involve 

the management of the flow of information and thus serve to guide readers through the content of 

the  text”  (Hyland,  2005b,  P.59).  Interactional  metadiscourse  features  “are  aspects  that  aim  to 

involve readers in the argument or ethos of the text” (Hyland, 2005b, P.59). 

At  present,  media  has  become  influential  powers  in  modern  society,  since  it  has  a 

persuasive  ability  to  influence  the  public  opinion.  Newspapers,  as  an  integral  part  of  media 

discourse,  in  addition  to  reporting  facts  and  neutral  information,  are  tools  used  to  convey 

ideologies, cultures, politics, societies, and negotiations by using different pragmalingual features 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                                     
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                      
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
15 

such as metadiscoursal  resources  (Salahshoor, Najjary, & Tofigh, 2014).  According to  McNair 

(2011),  “the  most  important  voice  of  a  newspaper  is  its  editorial,  which  embodies  its  political 

identity”  (p.  70).  Newspaper  editorials  (NEs),  featured  in  newspapers  or  magazines,  serve  as 

persuasive texts that blend factual information with argumentative content to affect public opinion 

on social, political, and economic matters (van Dijk, 2006). NEs as leading articles are a typical 

example  of  a  discourse  that  combines  arguing,  narrating,  and  exposing  to  influence  the  public 

opinion (Khalil, 2000, p. 23). NEs are usually written by an editor and represent the views and the 

opinions of the newspaper. They generally reflect the newspaper’s analysis, discussion, opinion, 

or judgement on the issues of the day (Bhatia, 2013, p. 170). According to as van Dijk (2006), as 

the voice of the newspaper, NEs are “public discourse that communicate with a mass audience and 

play on obvious role in the determining and shifting of public opinion” (p. 41). 

Hedging, characterized by tentative language (e.g., may, perhaps) to express uncertainty, 

and boosting, which employs assertive terms (e.g., absolutely, certainly) to convey confidence, are 

key rhetorical strategies used to persuade readers and shape their perspectives on the text’s subject 

matter  (Crismore  &  Vande  Kopple,  1997;  Dafouz-Milne,  2008).  Hyland  (2005b)  states  that 

hedging and boosting are essential rhetorical strategies in media discourse, particularly in  NEs, 

where they function to maintain a balance between objectivity and persuasiveness. These linguistic 

features play a significant role in shaping the credibility of arguments, as they convey the writer’s 

stance and affect public perception (Hyland, 2005a; Salager-Meyer, 1994). Skelton (1988, p.38) 

commenting on the importance of hedging and boosting in language states that: “Without hedging 

and boosting, the world is purely propositional, a rigid place where things either are the case or 

are not. With a hedging and boosting system, language is rendered more flexible and the  world 

more subtle.” In his research on metadiscourse, Hyland found that “hedges and boosters accounted 

for 83 per cent of all metadiscourse (…), with hedges being four times more frequent” (2005a, 

p.67).  Hedging 

softens 

assertions 

by 

signaling 

tentativeness 

or 

likelihood 

(e.g., might, possibly, suggests),  while  boosting  reinforces  certainty  in  statements,  conveying 

confidence and assertiveness (e.g., clearly, undoubtedly, always) (Hyland, 1996). 

According  to  Farrokhi  and  Emami  (2008),  in  English-language  newspaper  discourse, 

hedging  is  frequently  used  to  maintain  journalistic  neutrality  and  acknowledge  alternative 

perspectives.  While  hedging  is  a  part  of  Arabic  discourse,  its  function  may  differ.  In  Arabic 

discourse,  hedging  serves  as  a  politeness  strategy  or  as  a  means  of  acknowledging  religious, 

 
 
 
 
16 

cultural values, and societal norms (Al-Ghamdi & Alrefaee, 2020). Hedging and boosting are two 

crucial interactional metadiscourse features used by writers to convey their epistemic stance and 

position  related  to  the  writer–reader  interaction.  Hyland  (2004)  demonstrates  that  through 

presenting information as an opinion rather than a fact, hedging features emphasize the subjectivity 

of a position and therefore open that position to negotiation. However, boosting features function 

to indicate the writer’s strong confidence when presenting a claim (Holmes, 1982) and affirmation 

of that claim confidently (Abdi, Rizi, & Tavakoli, 2010). In contrast to hedging, boosting expresses 

the author’s confidence in their assertions, demonstrating engagement with the subject matter and 

alignment  with  the  audience’s  perspective.  In  other  words,  boosting  increases  the  force  of  the 

statement  (Jalilifar  &  Seidian,  2016)  and  allows  the  writer  who  has  various  positions  in 

argumentative writing to narrow these alternative positions  and confront  choices by expressing 

their ideas with certainty and confidence (Hyland, 2005b). According to Dafouz-Milne (2008), a 

persuasive text seems to consist of a combination of tentative features (i.e., hedging) and assertive 

ones  (i.e.,  boosting)  in  order  to  produce  a  discourse  that  balances  assertiveness  with  clarity, 

avoiding extremes of forcefulness or ambiguity. 

Hedging and boosting, as distinct interactional metadiscourse feature, are supposed to vary 

in frequency, form, and function across languages and discourse types (spoken or written). This 

variation  arises  because  different  languages  employ  unique  rhetorical-pragmatic  norms  and 

linguistic  systems  (semantic,  syntactic,  etc.),  shaping  how  they  deploy  textual  markers  like 

hedging  and  boosting  to  achieve  communicative  goals.  Research  comparing  ENEs  and  ANEs 

shows that they employ hedging and boosting regularly. However, their usage varies in frequency, 

form, and purpose, influenced by socio-cultural and linguistic contexts. (Al-Khatib & Salem, 2011; 

Hyland,  2005b).  According  to  Alghazo,  Al-Anbar,  Rabab’ah,  Abusalim,  and  Rayyan,  (2024), 

English  academic  and  journalistic  discourse  tends  to  adopt  tentative  claims  and  open-ended 

argumentation,  while  Arabic  academic  and  journal  discourse  historically  tends  to  adopt 

assertiveness and expressive persuasion. Similarly, Farrokhi and Emami (2008) argue that ENEs 

frequently employ modal verbs and epistemic markers to soften assertions and foster critical reader 

engagement  through  dialogic  interaction.  In  contrast,  Arabic  discourse  relies  more  on  forceful 

language, predominantly utilizing boosting features to assert positions emphatically (Al-Khatib & 

Salem,  2011;  Abdel  Salam,  2011).  Williams  (1981)  argues  that  NEs,  as  argumentative  and 

persuasive texts, use interactional metadiscourse to strengthen the relationship between the writer 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
17 

and the reader.  In other  words,  as important  interactional  metadiscursive  features, hedging and 

boosting are used by the writer/speaker to indicate their epistemic position related to the writer–

reader interaction or to the claim they present. That is to say, whenever a writer hedges his/her 

propositions or claims, he/she also aims to strengthen his/her relationship with his/her readers since 

he/she avoids imposing claims or views on them. 

The  researcher  in  the  present  research  specifically  examines  the  use  of  two  important 

interactional metadiscoursal features, namely hedging and boosting. Despite the fact that NEs have 

attracted a very wide readership and much research (e.g., Dafouz-Milne, 2008; Al-Ghoweri &Al-

Kayed, 2019), a little research has been done to explore the use of hedging and boosting features 

in  ENEs  and  ANEs.  Connor  (1996)  argues  that  “research  on  editorials  cross-culturally  is 

significant” and that “little is known cross-culturally about the genre” (p.144). For instance, Al-

Ghoweri and Al-Kayed (2019) studied English and Jordanian Arabic economic newspaper articles 

(i.e.  NEs)  to  compare  similarities  and  differences  in  terms  of  frequency  of  use  of  hedging  and 

boosting. To this purpose, the researchers selected 60 NEs published in both Anglo-American and 

Arabic newspapers (30 for each type of NEs) to investigate the use of such two features in these 

articles  based  on  Salager-Meyer’s  (1997)  and  Hyland’s  (2005a)  framework  of  hedging  and 

boosting features. The findings of research showed that the ENEs used a more frequent number of 

hedging than their Arabic counterparts. Findings also revealed that the socio-cultural (i.e. language 

factor) determined the frequency and the function of these features. The findings also showed that 

modal verbs and approximators were the most frequent hedging  categories in the ENEs, while 

approximators  and  lexical  verbs  were  the  most  frequent  hedging  categories  in  the  ANEs. 

Emphatics  and  amplifiers  were  very  frequently  used  boosting  features  by  both  the  ENEs  and 

ANEs. Hinkel (2005) and Dafouz-Milne (2008) have analyzed the used of hedging and boosting 

in media texts. However, their findings were limited to Western discourse. Previous research has 

primarily focused on hedging and boosting in academic writing (Hyland, 1998; Salager-Meyer, 

1994)  or  within  single-language  contexts  (Mauranen,  1997;  Alqam,  2020),  leaving  a  gap  in 

understanding  their  cross-linguistic  and  cross-cultural  characteristics  in  media  and  newspaper 

discourse.   

1.1.2 Theoretical Framework: Gee’s Theory 

The present research uses Gee’s (2011, 2012) theoretical framework of cultural models of 

linguistic  features.  Analyzing  hedging  and  boosting  features  involves  necessarily  studying  the 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
18 

socio-cultural context where these features occur. Socio-cultural context is the social surrounding 

or  society  in  which  the  language  is  used  as  a  means  of  communication.  As  interactional 

metadiscourse features, investigating hedging and boosting provides us with critical insights into 

how  cultural  contexts  shape  their  frequency,  purpose,  and  linguistic  realizations.  However, 

scholars  argue  over  the  extent  to  which  these  contexts,  embedded  within  specific  discourse 

communities, influence the deployment of such features (Sanjaya, 2013). Research suggests that 

an author’s cultural background, disciplinary norms, and genre conventions significantly shape the 

function, form, frequency of these features (Gee, 2012; Sanjaya, 2013, 2015). Gee (2012) defines 

cultural  contexts  as  cultural  models  that  guide  individuals’  linguistic  and  behavioral  patterns 

within a given discourse community. Cultural models may affect the pattern of use of rhetorical 

structures in general and metadiscursive features such as hedging and boosting in particular (Gee, 

2012;  Sanjaya,  2013,  2015).  One  important  aspect  of  Gee’s  linguistic  theory  is  the  notion  of 

situated meaning, cultural model, and discourse. The situated meaning of an expression is that 

meaning determined by the cultural specific context where the expression is used, and this context 

is relevant to a specific cultural model followed by the language producer. Discourse comprises 

both linguistic and non-linguistic resources employed by a speaker/writer to construct a particular 

kind of person (i.e. the person’s identity). For instance, by using hedging and boosting in different 

discourses, writers may construct their desired identity, which may or may not be recognized by 

their readers.    

Gee (2012) argues that his theory constitutes a social and cultural approach to language 

based on the claim that the best way to understand language is “to displace it from the center of 

our attention and to foreground society, culture, and values” (p.1). Sanjaya (2013) states that Gee’s 

theory of language can be considered as socio-cognitive, rather than purely social and cultural. 

Gee (2012) characterizes hedging and boosting as socio-cognitive phenomena, as their use and 

interpretation  inherently  engage  cognitive  processes  that  link  linguistic  forms  to  intended 

meanings.  In  essence,  all  language  use,  whether  constructing  or  interpreting  texts,  involves 

cognitive engagement. However, when writers employ hedging or boosting to modify the certainty 

of their claims, cognitive processing constitutes only one dimension of their multifaceted rhetorical 

strategy. According to Gee (2012), the mitigation (hedging) and amplification (boosting) of the 

statement’s  truth  value  by  the  writer  can  be  cognitively  planned  to  build  some  sort  of  social 

relationship with the reader, and this can be achieved by using such features. Gee’s (2012) socio-

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
19 

cognitive  framework  posits  that  language  is  not  an  isolated  system  but  is  deeply  shaped  by  its 

socio-cultural environment, including cognition, societal norms, and cultural values. Accordingly, 

understanding  language  requires  analyzing  how  specific  communities  employ  it  within  their 

unique social and cultural contexts (Gee, 2011, 2012). 

Since  language  cannot  be  separated  from  the  social  and  cultural  contexts  in  which  it 

functions  as  a  communicative  tool,  the  meaning  and  function  of  linguistic  elements  are  not 

universal, but they are associated with the specific contexts of their use. Gee (1999) emphasizes 

that meanings are collectively constructed through the cultural models shared by social groups and 

internalized  by  individuals,  rather  than  existing  as  fixed  or  independent  entities.  The  multiple 

meanings  created  and  adapted  to  specific  contexts  of  use  are  what  Gee  referred  to  as  situated 

meanings in socio-cultural settings (Gee, 1999, p. 40, as cited in Sanjaya, 2013). Although hedging 

and boosting features have referential meanings or utterance-type meanings in a particular context, 

they might be interpreted differently when they are used in different socio-cultural context as they 

might have different ideological structures. The fact that hedging and boosting features are used 

differently (in terms of form, function, and frequency) by writers/speakers from different cultural 

contexts means that these rhetorical features are associated with different cultural models adopted 

by  those  different  writers/speakers  (Gee,  2012;  Sanjaya,  2015).  The  writer’s  cultural  models 

include:  The  writer/speaker’s  intrapersonal  factors,  low  or  high  power  distance  between  the 

writer/speaker and the reader/listener, the speaker/writer’s degree of individualism vs. collectivism, 

the writers’ educational backgrounds or journalistic experiences, and the speaker/writer’s degree 

of high or low uncertainty avoidance. All these aspects are a part of the concept of cultural models 

or  belief  systems  proposed  by  Gee  (2011,  2012)  which  are  determined  by  the  socio-cultural 

circumstances of the writer (Sanjaya, 2013, 2015). 

Furthermore, empirical research examining linguistic and cultural variations presented in 

the review of literature chapter, demonstrates that socio-cultural contexts profoundly influence the 

use of hedging and boosting strategies. According to Sanjaya’s (2013, 2015) findings, RAs written 

in two or more different languages did not contain hedging and boosting at comparable rates. In 

other words, the socio-cultural context factors seemed, to some extent, to determine the rhetorical 

characteristics of the RAs (i.e. use of hedging and boosting).  The referential meaning of hedging 

involves the use of linguistic features (e.g., perhaps, suggest) to signal the writer’s cautious stance, 

avoiding absolute certainty about the validity of a claim or statement in the text. The referential 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
20 

meaning of boosting is the use of expressions, such as strongly, certainly, to indicate that the writer 

is committed fully toward the truth value of the presented proposition (Hyland, 2005a). However, 

there  are  other  meanings  and  functions  for  such  features,  which  are  the  situated  or  cultural 

meanings or functions. Gee (2012, p.87) states that “one and the same speaking and acting can 

count  as  different  things  in  different  contexts.”  Hedging  and  boosting  can  mean  and  function 

differently in different socio-cultural contexts (where a language and genre occur) and can be used 

at  different  frequencies  in  different  discourses  or  genres  written  in  different  languages  (i.e.  in 

different socio-cultural contexts). To put it differently, hedging and boosting can have multiple 

situated  meanings  or  functions  depending  on  the  various  contexts  where  they  occur.  In  other 

words,  different  cultures  or  languages  may  adopt  different  ideological  structures  or  rhetorical 

features  such  as  hedging  and  boosting  in  their  discourse.  The  ideological  structures  mean  the 

cultural values or models prevailing in  specific societies,  which determine  the use of words or 

expressions  (Gee,  2011,  2012).  Moreover,  Hyland  (2005a)  argues  that  the  use  of  hedging  and 

boosting  features  is  deeply  associated  with  the  structural  dynamics  of  a  discourse  community. 

Specifically,  the  community’s  conventions  and  its  members’  adherence  to  these  shared  norms 

critically influence how and when such rhetorical features are used. Accordingly, in the present 

research, the socio-cultural context of the ENEs is the English-speaking countries such as the U.S, 

Britain, Canada, Australia, New Zealand and  the journalistic discourse community where these 

articles are written. Similarly, the socio-cultural context of the ANEs is the Arab world and the 

journalistic discourse community where these articles are written. 

1.2 Research Hypotheses  

Because of the linguistic and cultural differences between English and Arabic discourse 

particularly in media writing, the use of hedging and boosting (i.e., frequency, form, and function) 

by English newspaper editors (ENErs) and Arabic newspaper editors (ANErs) is assumed to vary 

considerably.  Depending  on  prior  research  examining  how  hedging  and  boosting  function  as 

persuasive  and  argumentative  features  within  media  discourse,  the  following  hypotheses  are 

proposed for testing in this comparative analysis of ENEs and ANEs: 

1.  There are some noticeable differences in terms of frequency, and structure of hedging and 

boosting features used in the ENEs and the ANEs.  

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
21 

2.  Linguistic  and  cultural  variations  are  believed  to  influence  how  the  two  editor  groups 

employ hedging and boosting. In other words, the use of hedging and boosting in the ENEs 

and ANEs is supposed to be language or culture sensitive.    

3.  The frequency of hedging is higher in the ENEs than in the ANEs, whereas the frequency 

of  boosting  is  higher  in  the  ANEs  than  in  the  ENEs.  Moreover,  it  is  assumed  that  the 

linguistic  structure  of  hedging  and  boosting  differ  significantly  between  the  ENEs  and 

ANEs. Previous research suggests that the Anglo-American journalistic tradition tend to 

use  objective  and cautious language through the use of hedging  features such as  modal 

verbs (may, might), epistemic adverbs (probably, possibly), and tentative verbs (suggests, 

appears) (Hyland, 1996; Farrokhi & Emami, 2008). However, Mejdell (2006) demonstrates 

that because of different cultural conventions regarding academic discourse, Arabic writers 

may  seem  to  prefer  more  direct  and  assertive  argumentation  through  using  boosting 

features than their English counterparts (i.e. hedge their texts less frequently). Arabic media 

discourse  tends  to  prefer  assertiveness  and  persuasive  rhetoric  and  use  a  very  frequent 

number  of  boosting  features  such  as  emphatic  adverbs  (definitely,  certainly),  emphatic 

structures  (possibly,  probably),  repetition,  evaluative  adjectives  (undeniable,  absolute), 

and rhetorical questions to emphasize the author’s conviction and authority (Al-Khatib & 

Salem, 2011; Abdel Salam, 2011).  

4.  There  are some  significant  differences and similarities  between  the ENEs and ANEs in 

terms of functions of hedging and boosting features.  

1.3 Research Problem 

One important issue the present research tackles is cross-cultural differences, which can 

lead to different realization of hedging and boosting features. Different realization of such features, 

consequently, may result in cross-linguistic or cross-cultural miscommunication between Anglo-

American and Arab readers, writers, or translators of Arabic or English language. In other words, 

a  breakdown  or  failure  in  communication  is  likely  to  happen  due  to  “cross-culturally  different 

perceptions of what constitutes appropriate linguistic behavior” (Fraser, 2010, p.13). The primary 

cause  of  this  breakdown  is  that  these  metadiscursive  features  are  specific  to  each  language  or 

culture.  That  is  to  say,  (Thomas,  1983;  Hyland,  1995).  To  put  it  differently,  the  frequency, 

function,  and  form  of  hedging  and  boosting  differ  across  discourse  communities  and  cultures, 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
22 

which can lead to misunderstandings in intercultural exchanges (Thomas, 1983; Hyland, 1995). 

To put it differently, when speakers or writers use hedging or boosting inappropriately, his 

or her claims can be considered as too assertive or too tentative according to the cultural models 

of each language group (i.e. English or Arabic). The present research also investigates whether 

ENErs and ANErs employ hedging and boosting in comparable ways or if their usage is shaped 

by cultural and linguistic factors. Cross-cultural findings suggest that English discourse tends to 

feature  hedging  more  frequently  and  with  a  greater  variety  of  tentative  constructions,  whereas 

Arabic  rhetoric  and  discourse  typically  adopts  a  more  forceful,  expressive  style,  marked  by 

frequent use boosting features (Najjar, 1990; Al-Khatib & Salem, 2011). These differences point 

to distinct hedging and boosting patterns across the two discourse traditions. 

1.4 Research Objectives 

One important objective the present research aims to achieve is to contrastively analyze 

and compare the form, frequency, and function of hedging and boosting features in the genre of 

NEs  as  a  distinctive  genre  in  journalistic  discourse.  In  other  words,  the  present  research 

investigates the effect of the independent variable (i.e. language or cross-cultural context) on the 

dependent  variable  (i.e.  the  use  of  hedging  and  boosting  in  the  English  and  Arabic  corpora). 

Accordingly, the present research aims to achieve the following objectives:  

1.  To analyze  the frequency  of hedging  and boosting  used in  both  the English and Arabic 

corpora (ENEs & ANEs).  

2.  To  compare  the  frequency  and  distribution  of  various  categories  or  subcategories  of 

hedging and boosting used in the ENEs and ANEs.   

3.  To  identify  what  linguistic  grammatical  and  lexical  forms  or  categories  functioning  as 

hedging and boosting features used by writers with different cultural backgrounds in the 

genre of NEs and  how NErs mitigate or amplify their claims (Dafouz-Milne, 2008; Hinkel, 

2005). 

4.  To identify what pragmatic functions hedging and boosting features can achieve in the two 

corpora.  

1.5 Research Significance 

Despite the importance of cross-linguistic differences in the use of hedging and boosting 

in media discourse, the research done in this area is limited particularly between ENEs and ANEs. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                                                                                   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
23 

There has been a great deal of research on hedging and boosting across languages, disciplines, and 

genres (e.g., Vassileva, 2001; Abbas, 2011; Andrusenko, 2015; Al-Ghoweri &   Al-Kayed, 2019; 

Zarza, 2018; Lafuente-Millán, 2008; Sanjaya, 2013; Amirrostami, 2019). However, to the best of 

the researcher’s knowledge, no comparative research has been carried out to examine hedging and 

boosting in English and Arabic newspaper discourse particularly in ENE and ANEs except for Al-

Ghoweri  and  Al-Kayed  (2019)  (cf.  Abbadi,  2014).  The  limited  amount  of  research  that  is 

conducted in this area has shown that there are some noticeable variations in terms of use of such 

features cross languages and across genres. The findings of the past comparative research on the 

use  of  such  features  in  English  and  other  languages  (e.g.,  Spanish,  Persian,  &  Arabic)  are 

inconclusive or incomprehensive, and they have offered scant insights into how these features vary 

in use. Consequently, this research examines hedging and boosting cross-culturally in ENEs and 

ANEs within a non-Western European language specifically Modern Standard Arabic (MSA) and 

English as a European language to see if there are any differences or similarities in their frequency, 

form and function. The present research has significant theoretical and practical implications for 

understanding  hedging  and  boosting  in  media  discourse,  particularly  in  ENEs  and  ANEs.  The 

present research can be particularly beneficial to nonnative editors, readers, translators, and second 

language learners, for it provides insights and information about how to deal with two different 

metadiscusive  systems  of  using  such  features.  Furthermore,  the  investigation  of  hedging  and 

boosting realizations in two different cultures is an important area, as it allows us to explore the 

impact of linguistic and cultural variations on cross-cultural communication and the use of such 

rhetorical  features.  By  comparing the frequency, form,  and function of such  rhetorical  features 

across two linguistically and culturally distinct contexts, this research contributes to the growing 

field  of  contrastive  rhetoric  (Connor,  2002)  and  media  linguistics  (Bednarek  &  Caple,  2017).  

Investigating how the ENErs and ANErs use these features can provide deeper insights into how 

persuasion and argumentation are linguistically structured across languages (Al-Khatib & Salem, 

2011;  Najjar,  1990).    Practically,  the  findings  of  this  research  have  important  implications  for 

journalism,  cross-cultural  communication,  and  translation  studies.  Understanding  the  rhetorical 

strategies used in two different languages can help journalists, translators, and discourse analysts 

to  develop  more  effective  strategies  to  writing  and  understand  NEs  in  multilingual  and 

multicultural contexts (Biber & Finegan, 1989).  

 
24 

1.6 Research Questions 

This research aims to investigate the use of hedging and boosting in the ENEs and ANEs 

by addressing the following research questions: 

1.  What  is  the  frequency  of  occurrence  of  hedging  and  boosting  in  the  ENEs  (English 

corpus)?   

2.  What is the frequency of occurrence of hedging and boosting in the ANEs (Arabic corpus)?   

3.  What  are  the  linguistic  forms  or  categories/subcategories  of  such  features  used  in  the 

English and Arabic corpora?  

4.  What  are  the  pragmatic  and  socio-cultural  functions  of  these  features  in  the  ENEs  and 

ANEs?  

5.  What socio-cultural and rhetorical factors influence the use of such features in the ENEs 

and ANEs?  

6.  What are reasons of differences or preferences in terms of usage of such features (if there 

are any) between the ENEs and ANEs? 

7.  Do the ENEs tend to use a more frequent number of tentative features and an indirect style 

(hedging features) and the ANEs tend to use a more assertive and expressive style (boosting 

features), as suggested by previous research (Najjar, 1990; Alqam, 2020)?  

1.7 Structure of the Research  

The  present  research  is  divided  into  five  chapters:  Chapter  one  presents  the  theoretical 

background of the research. It deals primarily with the position of hedging and boosting within 

body of literature as two rhetorical linguistic features of metadiscourse. It combines the theoretical 

framework,  the  hypothesis,  the  problem,  the  significance  of  this  research,  and  its  research 

questions. Chapter two presents the methodology the research applies, the analytical framework 

and  taxonomy  the  research  adopts,  the  description  of  the  two  corpora,  and  the  descriptive  and 

inferential  procedures  of  data  collection.  Chapter  three  presents  the  review  of  literature  which 

provides a theoretical account of evolution of hedging  and boosting concepts, scholars’ several 

views  on  these  two  linguistic  rhetorical  features,  their  definitions,  categories,  models,  and  the 

position  of  hedging  and  boosting  in  the  body  of  both  theoretical  and  empirical  literature.  The 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
25 

researcher supports this theoretical account with some empirical research conducted in this area. 

Chapter four introduces the analysis of the data, findings and discussion of the findings. Chapter 

five provides some limitations the research encountered and presents some recommendations for 

future  researchers.  It  concludes  with  providing  some  contributions  the  research  achieved  and 

implications for teachers, translators, leaners, and editors, and it summarizes the major procedures, 

methodology, and findings of the present research. Moreover, a list of references and appendixes 

containing  the  frequency  and  distribution  of  hedging  and  boosting  in  each  ENE  and  ANE  are 

presented in the last section of the research. 

1.8 Summary  

The present chapter introduced the theoretical basis of this research. It proceeded to present 

the hypothesis, the problem, the objectives, and the significance of the present research. Identifying 

the previous components helped in building the research questions, which were also provided in 

this chapter. The final part of chapter one involved the structure of the research.  

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
26 

Chapter Two  

    Methodology 

After establishing the theoretical background of the current research, this chapter aims to 

describe the analytical framework the research adopts to identify hedging and boosting features in 

the two corpora. The chapter also presents the nature of the current research, the size of the two 

corpora  the  researcher  analyzes,  the  research  method,  and  the  procedures  that  are  applied  to 

conduct statistical analysis and collect the data. 

2.1 Identification of Hedging and Boosting in the two Corpora (ENEs & ANEs) 

Since  hedging  and  boosting  features  can  be  derived  from  various  lexico-grammatical 

categories  and  realized  in  various  strategies,  there  has  been  no  predetermined  definite  lexico-

grammatical taxonomy of hedging and boosting feasible to all types of discourse (Hyland, 1996). 

That is to say, hedging and boosting features are  open functional and formal classes. A further 

methodological  challenge  is  that  a  single  structure  may  be  classified  as  either  a  hedging  or  a 

boosting feature, depending on its contextual use. For example, in the sentence “Further research 

is obviously necessary in this direction”, the adverb obviously could function as a hedge or booster 

depending  on  the  context  or  the  author’s  intention.  Accordingly,  the  researcher  conducts  a 

thorough manual contextual examination to identify hedging and boosting occurrences along with 

their functions in their contextual use. This analysis draws on Fraser’s (2010, pp. 23–24) analytical 

framework,  Hyland’s  (1996)  definition  of  hedging,  and  Hyland’s  (2005a)  concept  of 

boosting.According to Hyland (2005a), “A hedge is … any linguistic means used to indicate either 

(a) a lack of complete commitment to the truth of a proposition, or (b) a desire not to express that 

commitment categorically” (p. 251). According to Hyland (2005a), “Boosters … allow writers to 

express conviction and assert a proposition with confidence, representing a strong claim about a 

state  of  affairs.”    Moreover,  the  researcher  incorporates  other  categories  that  are  not  found  in 

Fraser’s  model  for  both  English  and  Arabic  corpora.  As  for  Arabic,  and  to  the  best  of  the 

researcher’s knowledge, there is no definite classification of Arabic hedging and boosting features 

of any type; therefore, Fraser’s model (2010, pp.23-24) of English hedging features is adopted as 

main guidance to identify occurrences of hedging and boosting features in the Arabic corpus. The 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
researcher adopts and modifies Fraser’s (2010) analytical model to identify and categorize the two

2.2 The Present Research  

The  present  research  can  be  described  as  a  quantitative,  qualitative,  comparative,  and 

corpus-based research. Since the present research compares two linguistic features, which exist in 

both  English  and  Arabic  languages,  a  comparative  corpus-based  approach  can  be  ideal  to  this 

purpose. Adel and Mauranen (2010) argue that scholars comparing languages or genres typically 

rely on corpus-based methods that use a fixed taxonomy of lexical items or features (for example, 

“however” as a connective, possible as a hedge, definitely as a booster, and so forth). Moreover, a 

contrastive or comparative research can be an important method for understanding cultural specific 

features as well as linguistic universals (Adel & Mauranen, 2010). To put it simply, contrastive 

research  provides  an  essential  means  of  gaining  detailed  descriptive  insights  into  particular 

languages and cultures, as well as a deeper understanding of language-mediated communication 

(Pery-Woodley,  1990,  cited  in  Connor,  1996,  p.  6).  Moreover,  because  of  its  theoretical  and 

practical strengths, the present research adopts a corpus-based approach, utilizing the instruments 

and techniques of corpus linguistics.   

By applying these tools and techniques to compare hedging and boosting patterns in ENE 

and  ANE  newspaper  discourse,  the  present  research  can  shed  some  light  on  differences  and 

similarities in terms of use of such features in this discourse. According to McEnery and Hardie 

(2011), using corpus tools refers to “the actual text collections and software packages for corpus 

access” (p. 206). Corpus methods, on the other hand, refer to “the analytic techniques that are used 

when we work with corpus data” (McEnery & Hardie, 2011, p. 206). In terms of tools, the research 

investigates  two  collections  of  texts  as  described  above  depending  on  a  mixture  of  manual 

contextual  and  computer-based  analysis.  The  present  research  also  employs  corpus  methods—

such as generating concordances, calculating frequency lists, and producing word sketches—to to 

identify hedging and boosting caqtegories. As Conrad (1999) demonstrates, corpus-based research 

uses computer tools to analyze bodies of authentic texts, enabling the investigation of linguistic 

features and usage patterns of language that have been extremely difficult to investigate without 

the  use  of  computers.  A  corpus-based  research  is  very  useful  when  the  purpose  is  to  derive 

occurrences of a linguistic feature in a large amount of texts. Furthermore, corpus-based analysis 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                                   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
29 

is vital for researchers because corpora provide a rich repository of data not only on word and 

phrase  frequencies  but  also  on  empirical  inquiries  into  discourse  community.  Beyond  simple 

frequency counts, these large text collections allow for systematic, data-driven exploration of how 

discourse  structures  unfold  in  authentic  language  use.  Such  empirical  insight  into  discourse 

organization underscores the value of corpus methods in both descriptive and functional studies of 

language (Biber, Connor, & Upton, 2007). 

 2.3 Research Method  

A research method can usually be described as either quantitative or qualitative and mixed 

methods when these are combined. It is argued that a quantitative research method is extremely 

effective  and  sufficient  for  obtaining  accurate  results  (Adel  &  Mauranen,  2010).  Qualitative 

research typically involves an in-depth analysis of a small number of texts, meaning that achieving 

broad generalizability or representativeness of the results is not its goal. However, a quantitative 

research  method  is  “important  for  generalizable  results”  (Biber,  2009,  p.  1287).  Adel  and 

Mauranen  (2010)  compare  their  quantitative  method  “unfavourably  with  their  own  qualitative 

approach, which also seems to involve counting features but which sees the metadiscusive unit as 

larger than the search term” (p.4). A quantitative method involves “data collection procedures that 

result  primarily  in  numerical  data  which  is  then  analyzed  primarily  by  statistical  methods” 

(Dornyei,  2007,  p.  24).  The  results  are  often  statistically  analyzed  and  then  used  to  generalize 

findings. A qualitative method involves “data collection procedures that result primarily in  open-

ended, non-numerical data which is then analyzed primarily by non-statistical methods” (Dornyei, 

2007,  p.24).  This  approach  typically  produces  textual  insights  rather  than  numerical  data. 

Moreover, in qualitative research, “the information is subjectively interpreted by the researcher 

with the goal of providing greater understanding of a range of phenomena” (Dornyei, 2007, pp.37-

38).  Quantitative  and  qualitative  approaches  may  be  integrated  within  a  framework  known  as 

mixed-method research (Dörnyei, 2007, p. 24). 

The present research employs both quantitative and qualitative data collection techniques, 

aligning with what Dörnyei (2007, p. 24) refers to as a mixed-methods approach. In other words, 

both methods are used to answer the research questions and achieve the objectives of the research. 

The qualitative method is used in the present research to interpret the metadiscoursal and pragmatic 

functions of hedging and boosting features used by the two groups of NErs and to provide details 

about  forms  of  such  features.  The  quantitative  aspect  of  this  research  involves  analyzing  NEs 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
30 

written by both the ENErs and ANErs to determine the frequency of categories of hedging and 

boosting features. Aijmer (2002) argues that sometimes a manual analysis is necessary to avoid 

disambiguation. A computer-based analysis may not consider the linguistic context surrounding 

each feature, making it likely inadequate for this research, as understanding contextual usage is 

particularly important because computer-based analysis faces the risk of assuming some linguistic 

features  (when  they  are  not)  as  hedging  or  boosting  features  and  can  damage  the  validity  of 

research  (Abbas,  2011).  In  other  words,  the  current  research  incorporates  both  manual  and 

computer-assisted  analyses.  To  put  it  differently,  identifying  hedging  and  boosting  is  done 

manually using Microsoft Word Office as well as Adobe Acrobat Professional X program, whereas 

frequencies and percentages of hedging expressions over the total number of running words are 

computed, identified, and tabulated quantitatively. 

2.4 Procedures of Data Collection   

The data collection in the current research includes retrieving 20 ENEs and 20 ANEs from 

the  electronic  version  of  influential,  national,  and  international  Anglo-American  and  Arabic 

newspapers available on the internet. The NEs are electronically retrieved, labeled, and stored as 

TXT files to create a database for the two corpora. Data collection is done in March 2025, and the 

collected NEs are published in the period between 1 January and 1 August 2021. The texts are 

carefully examined to count instances of hedging and boosting, as well as to identify the specific 

structures  and  expressions  serving  these  functions.  Then,  the  expressions  or  items  that  are 

considered to be hedging or boosting features are identified, categorized, and subcategorized in 

the texts based on Frazer’s (2010) model and Hyland’s (1996) definition of hedging and Hyland’s 

(2005a)  definition  of  boosting.  Then,  two  independent  lists  of  Arabic  and  English  linguistic 

features of hedging and boosting are generated from these manual readings to compare and contrast 

the two sets  of data:  The  English  list  and  Arabic  list  of hedging and boosting. Afterwards, the 

number of hedging and features boosting is recorded in each text and in each corpus separately to 

calculate the frequencies and percentages of different types of hedging and boosting categories. 

Each  category  and  subcategory  of  hedging  and  boosting  in  the  research  includes  Romanized 

versions of the Arabic examples and features. In the case of the Arabic corpus, examples taken 

from the main text are presented in Romanized Arabic, accompanied by their English translations 

and original Arabic script. The Romanization is in accordance with the transliteration of Arabic in 

ALA-LC Romanization tables adopted by the Library of Congress. Finally,  a  major  concern  in 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
31 

most quantitative research is the process of ensuring random sample selection (Henning, 1986). 

Therefore, the researcher is concerned that all NEs are chosen randomly from their newspaper’s 

websites. Random selection is carried out using a freely available Random Number Generator tool 

found online (www.random.org). However, the researcher also takes into account two key factors 

when  choosing  each  text.  First,  the  corpora  represent  writing  samples  of  different  writers,  and 

second, these samples are comparatively in equal length. In other words, texts in both corpora are 

limited  to  a  maximum  of  1500  words,  as  longer  texts  often  pose  challenges  in  tracking  the 

argumentative  elements  during data  analysis.  Moreover, a preliminary pilot  analysis  is done  to 

select a representative sample of NEs. To achieve this, newspapers with a specific political bias or 

targeted at a particular audience are excluded, while those covering a broader range of topics and 

appealing  to  a  wider  audience  are  chosen.  From  this  selection,  a  group  of  ENEs  and  ANEs  is 

ultimately selected for this research. Translated NEs are not chosen. The construction of the entire 

corpus for analysis in the present research is also based on what Connor and Moreno (2005) refer 

to as “tertium comparationis”, which simply refers to the idea that “we compare elements that can 

in  fact  be  compared”  (p.154).  In  other  words,  prior  to  data  collection,  selection  criteria  are 

established to ensure that both data sets are comparable. The first criterion is that both corpora 

must consist of the same genre. The second criterion is that the field in which the texts are produced 

should also be similar. Third, in order to compare two natural and authentic set of data, the texts 

from  the  two  languages  have  to  be  written  by  the  native  speakers  of  the  respective  languages, 

determined from the name of the author. However, this criterion is not applicable to unsigned and 

unnamed NEs, making it one of the limitations faced in this research. The coding and identification 

of linguistic realizations of hedging  and boosting is done by the researcher alone, because it is 

difficult  to  find  English  and  Arab  native-speaker  expert  volunteers  carry  out  the  coding  or 

identification tasks in the present research. 

2.5 Corpus Description and Size   

The  current  research  analyzes  two  data  sets,  each  consisting  of  40  NEs—20  written  in 

English by Anglo-American ENErs and 20 written in Modern Standard Arabic (MSA) by ANErs. 

The first data is the English corpus, which consists of 20 ENEs often labeled under the title of 

opinion articles  or editorials. The second one is the Arabic  corpus, which comprises 20 ANEs 

often labeled under the headline of  al-ra’ay articles  (يأرلا تلااقم /opinion articles) or under the 

headline of iftitaḥīya (ةيحاتتفا /editorial) (also known as comment columns and opinion columns). 

 
                              
 
 
 
               
              
 
 
 
 
 
                    
 
32 

The whole two corpora, which comprise 40 NEs, 20 ENEs and 20 ANEs, amounts to 29,744 words. 

The English corpus contains 15,692 words, whereas the Arabic Corpus contains 14,052 words. 

Oostdijk (1991, p.50) reports that “a sample size of 20,000 words would yield samples that are 

large enough to be representative of a given variety.” It can be said that the current two corpora 

were sufficient and representative, as the number of words in the two sets of data is over 20,000 

words. The NEs are all published during the period between 1 January and 1 August, 2021 due to 

the assumption that time influences the style and convention of writing. The two corpora are drawn 

from  the  electronic  versions  of  major  international  and  national  daily  Arabic  and  English 

newspapers as table 2.2 illustrates:

These NEs are randomly selected from a pool of leading and influential newspapers in the

global reach, significance in the media industry, and their wide-ranging coverage of diverse topics 

in  both  the  international  Anglo-American  and  Arab  Worlds.  As  table  2.2  shows,  20  ENEs  are

that the selected English newspapers are: The New York Times, the Independent, the Guardian, 

the New York Post, the Washington Post, Los Angeles Times, and the USA Today. The chosen 

Arabic  newspapers  are:  Al-Watan  Syrian  (ةيروسلا  نطولا),  Al-Qūds  (سدقلا),  Al-Shārq  Al-Awsat
