These newspapers are leading daily international newspapers because of their professional 

and  well-known  experienced  journalists  or  editors.  All  these  newspapers  are  printed  and 

distributed simultaneously in Europe, the Middle East, the UK, and the USA, which means that 

they have an extensive market  in  the World. Although there may be minor differences in  their 

 
 
 
                                                                                                   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
33 

styles, these professional writers represent a writing sample following the convention of newspaper 

discourse in the English-speaking and Arab countries. These NEs are written in variable length, 

ranging from a 200-word text to a 1350-word text. The subjects covered in these NEs primarily 

focused  on  political  issues  that  are  significant  to  both  the  local  community  and  the  global 

population  due  to  their  impact  on  people's  lives  (Maynard,  1996;  Van  Dijk,  1998).  The  texts 

analyzed in the present research represent a single genre of journalistic writing, specifically NEs. 

McCarten argues that corpora containing various genres can lead to misleading findings (2010, p. 

414). Thus, it is essential to construct a corpus consisting solely of NEs written by ANErs and 

ENErs to guarantee the data's representativeness and comparability. 

 2.6 Statistical Procedures 

Once the hedging and boosting features in the data are quantified, the frequency of each 

category or subcategory, along with other relevant variables in the two main corpora and their sub-

corpora,  is  input  into  SPSS  (version  22)  software,  a  program  used  to  calculate  statistical 

significance.  SPSS  software  can  be  effective  in  identifying  and  analyzing  the  raw  and  mean 

frequency  of  use  of  hedging  and  boosting  for  the  two  corpora.  Following  the  counting  and 

recording of the total occurrences of hedging and boosting features based on formal categories and 

subcategories, the mean frequency of these features per one thousand words (PTW) is calculated 

for each text group and for each category individually. The relative frequency is calculated per one 

thousand words (PTW) to allow for fair comparison between the two corpora and categories, as 

the sample sizes are inherently unequal (English corpus = 15,692 words; Arabic corpus = 14,052 

words). In fact, the ANEs are comparatively shorter than their English counterparts. Therefore, the 

analysis  is  conducted  on  normalized,  rather  than  absolute  or  raw  occurrences  of  hedging  and 

boosting. According to Biber (2009, p.1299), when examining the counts of features across texts, 

“it is  important  to  make sure that the scores are  comparable.” This  process  is  very common to 

academic researchers, and they name it normalization. Normalization of counts refers to “a way 

to convert raw counts into rates of occurrence, so that the scores from texts of different lengths can 

be compared” (Biber, Conrad, & Reppen, 1998, p.1299). The following normalization formula is 

used to change raw frequencies into normalized mean frequencies for all the values of the two 

corpora:  

      𝑴𝒆𝒂𝒏 𝑭𝒓𝒆𝒒𝒖𝒆𝒏𝒄𝒚 𝑷𝑻𝑾 =

𝑵𝒐.𝒐𝒇 𝒐𝒄𝒄𝒖𝒓𝒓𝒆𝒏𝒄𝒆𝒔 𝒐𝒇 𝒉𝒆𝒅𝒈𝒆𝒔 𝒊𝒏 𝒂 𝒕𝒆𝒙𝒕

𝑵𝒐.  𝒐𝒇 𝒘𝒐𝒓𝒅𝒔 𝒐𝒇 𝒕𝒉𝒆 𝒕𝒆𝒙𝒕

Thus, 1,000 value is used as a base number of normalization. Adopting a higher basis, for 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
34 

example,  10,000  words  would  result  in  artificial  inflation  of  rare  features,  more  particularly  in 

ANEs (Biber, 2009). For example, if a text containing 1,110 words includes 44 hedging features, 

the normalized mean frequency (MF) of these features is 39.63, rounded to two decimal places. 

2.7 Summary   

The  present  chapter  introduced  the  methodology  the  research  applied  to  achieve  its 

objectives and answer research questions. It presented the analytical model adopted to identify the 

use of hedging and boosting features, the nature of present research, the research method, which 

was applied, the procedures of data collection, the corpus description and size, and concluded with 

the  statistical  procedures,  which  were  done  to  obtain  valid  results.  Next  chapter  provides  a 

theoretical and empirical review of the literature in relevance to hedging and boosting features.   

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
35 

    Chapter Three  

                   Review of Literature                                                                          

    This chapter aims to provide a theoretical background that puts the research within the body 

of relevant literature. It reviews the development of such concepts as discourse and metadiscourse 

and concurrent emergence of hedging and phenomena. It also gives an account of the concept of 

hedging and its evolution. Following this, the chapter reviews the literature of boosting concept 

and scholars’ views of the phenomenon. Afterwards, the chapter provides some perspectives and 

approaches  that  investigated  the  feature  of  hedging  and  boosting.  It  also  highlights  some 

characteristics  of  the  genre  of  newspaper  editorials  (NEs)  which  the  current  research  analyzes. 

This  chapter  also  provides  some  approaches  that  investigated  hedging  and  boosting  in  Arabic 

language,  and  it  concludes  with  some  previous  research  that  is  conducted  in  relevance  to  such 

features. 

3.1 Theoretical Framework   

3.1.1 Discourse and Metadiscourse  

Before exploring the literature relevant to hedging and boosting features, it is important to 

clarify;  as  previously  mentioned,  two  main  levels  of  analysis  in  language:  Discourse  and 

metadiscourse.  In  simple  terms,  words  are  grammatically  structured  into  phrases,  clauses  and 

sentences to function meaningfully in various contexts. Similarly, sentences are systematically and 

rhetorically  arranged together to  produce  a  text  or discourse. Discourse is a linguistic unit that 

comprises more than one sentence structured and arranged systematically to function in different 

contexts.  To  understand  any  discourse,  we  should  examine  its  smallest  elements  and  its  socio-

cultural and pragmatic context where it is written. Simply, a linguist carries out a discourse analysis 

to explore those elements and features that define this discourse. Discourse analysis examines the 

forms and functions of linguistic features of language in use and their social and cultural properties. 

Examining discourse features includes also  analyzing what linguists (e.g., Hyland, 1999, 1998; 

Myers, 1989; Salager-Meyer, 1994; Skelton, 1988) categorize as metadiscourse. Researchers and 

linguists  commonly  distinguish  two  core  components  of  coherent  texts:  Propositional  content, 

which  conveys  factual  or  informational  material,  and  metadiscursive  elements,  which  guide 

readers through the text’s structure and aid interpretation of its core ideas. Thus, discourse is the 

 
 
 
 
 
      
     
 
                                 
 
 
 
             
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                          
 
 
 
 
                               
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
36 

propositional materials, whereas metadiscourse is the non-propositional ones. 

Metadiscourse  is  a  prominent  linguistic  feature  frequently  used  in  language  and  has 

attracted  increasing  scholarly  interest  across  diverse  theoretical  and  analytical  frameworks, 

particularly  for  its  role  in  shaping  interactional  features  within  texts  (e.g.,  Atai  &  Sadr,  2008; 

Davoodifard, 2006; Falahati, 2004; Hyland, 1999, 1998a; Myers, 1989; Salager-Meyer, 1994; & 

Skelton, 1988). According to Vande Kopple (1985), “metadiscourse refers to those features which 

writers  include  to  help  readers  decode  their  message,  share  the  writer’s  views  and  reflect  the 

particular  conventions  that  are  followed  in  a  given  culture”  (p.97).  Hyland  (2005b)  defines 

metadiscourse as “the cover term for the self-reflective expressions used to negotiate interactional 

meanings in a text, assist the writer (or speaker) to express a viewpoint and engage with readers as 

members of a particular community” (P.98). In this way, a text does not only consist of information 

but also includes other features that guide the reader and help the writer convey his/her purpose 

and  attitude.  According  to  Vande  Kopple  and  Crismore  (1997),  primary  discourse  is  primarily 

concerned with ideational function, whereas metadiscourse is related to textual and interpersonal 

function of the language. Sultan (2011) states that metadiscourse has been analyzed across diverse 

textual  genres,  including  textbooks,  dissertations,  advertisements,  research  articles,  and  casual 

conversations, with notable emphasis on news discourse (e.g., Hyland, 1998a, 1998b, 2005a, 2017; 

Dafouz-Milne 2008).  Hyland (2000) argues that writers not only construct discourse to convey 

objective  content  but  also  actively  negotiate  the  validity  of  their  positions,  strategically  frame 

arguments,  and  balance  factual  assertions  with  evaluative  commentary  and  certainty  with 

measured caution, which is a dynamic interplay facilitated through metadiscoursal feature. 

According to Hyland (2005a), Zelling Hariss (1959) has been the first scholar to propose 

the concept of metadiscourse, According to Hariss (1959), metadiscourse is introduced “to offer a 

way of understanding language in  use, representing a writer’s or  speakers’ attempts to  guide a 

receiver’s  perception of  a text” (Hyland, 2005a,  p.3). To clarify more, metadiscourse as  a new 

concept  in  the  area  of  discourse  analysis,  refers  to  the  way  speakers  and  writers  address  and 

communicate  with  their  audience.  Linguists  have  been  recently  interested  more  often  in 

metadiscourse analysis than discourse analysis of language (e.g., Schiffrin, 1980; Williams, 1981; 

Van  de  Kopple,  1985;  Crismore  et  al.,  1993;  &  Hyland,  2005a).  Hyland  and  Tse  (2004)  have 

investigated metadiscourse through an interpersonal lens, arguing that all metadiscoursal elements 

inherently function as interpersonal features, as they explicitly address readers’ prior knowledge, 

 
  
 
 
 
37 

textual familiarity, and cognitive demands. Metadiscourse scholars (e.g., Hyland, 2005a; Vande 

Kopple  &  Crismore,  1997;  Crismore  et  al.,  1993)  have  proposed  several  taxonomies  of 

metadiscourse.  Hyland  (2005a,  p.49)  proposes  one  of  the  most  comprehensive  model  of 

metadiscourse.  His  model,  which  was  developed  from  previous  linguists’  models  (i.e.,Vande 

Kopple,  1985  &  Crismore  et  al.,  1993),  consists  of  two  major  categories  of  metadiscourse: 

Interactive  and  interactional.  Interactive  metadiscourse  structures  the  text  to  enhance  its 

coherence,  cohesion,  and  persuasiveness  for  the  audience,  whereas  interactional  metadiscourse 

functions  to  engage  the  reader  by  guiding  him/her  through  the  discourse.  The  interactional 

metadiscours involves the writer’s direct dialogic interaction, such as commenting on or evaluating 

their own claims. Interactive features include organizational strategies such as transitions, frame 

markers,  endophoric  references,  evidentials,  and  code  glosses.  Transitions  create  relations 

between main clauses in the text (e.g., in addition, and, but, thus, and so on). Frame markers refer 

to discourse acts, sequences, or stages of parts of the text (e.g., first, next, finally, to conclude, and 

others). Endophoric  markers  are used to  refer to  information  in  other parts of the text (e.g., as 

noted above, see Fig, in section, and others). Evidentials are used to refer to information from other 

texts  (e.g.  according  to  X,  Z  states,  and  others).  Code  glosses  are  used  to  clarify  propositional 

meanings  (e.g.,  namely,  for  example,  such  as,  in  other  words,  and  others).  Interactional 

metadiscourse  includes  hedging,  boosting,  attitude  markers,  self-mentions,  and  engagement 

markers. Hedging is used to indicate imprecision and establish a dialogue with the reader (e.g., 

might, perhaps, possible, about, and so on). Boosting is used to emphasize precision or close a 

dialogue with the reader (e.g., in fact, definitely, obviously, it is clear that, demonstrate, and so 

on).  Attitude  markers  indicate  the  writer’s  attitude  to  the  proposition  (e.g.,  unfortunately, 

hopefully, surprisingly, I agree, and others). Self-mentions are an explicit reference to the author 

in the text (e.g., I, we, my, me, our, and so on). Engagement markers explicitly build a relationship 

between the writer and the reader (you, your, consider, note, you can see that, and so on) (Hyland, 

2005a, p.49).    

The  researcher  is  precisely  interested  in  exploring  two  categories  of  interactional 

metadiscourse  namely  hedging  and  boosting.  Hedging  and  boosting  considered  by  many 

researchers (e.g., Hyland, 1999, 1998a, 2005a; Myers, 1989; Salager-Meyer, 1994; Skelton, 1988) 

the most typical realization of interactional metadiscourse. Metadiscourse in general and hedging 

and boosting in particular have been studied in journalistic discourse namely NEs and proved to 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
38 

have  persuasive  effects  in  newspaper  discourse  (Dafouz-Milne,  2008;  Buitkienė,  2008; 

Moghadam, 2017). 

3.1.2 Concept of Hedging 

Hedging, a multifaceted component of interpersonal metadiscourse, has drawn extensive 

scholarly inquiry. Its functions have been analyzed through frameworks such as politeness theory 

(Brown  &  Levinson, 1978, 1987;  Leech, 1983),  semantic interpretation  (Lakoff, 1973),  formal 

logic systems (McCawley, 1981; Zadeh, 1972), and pragmatic explorations of its communicative 

functions (Hyland, 1995, 1997; Markkanen & Schröder, 2000; Varttala, 1999, 2001; Vassileva, 

2001). According to Oxford Dictionary (2016), to hedge against something is “to avoid giving a 

direct answer to a question or a promising to support a particular idea, etc.” Similarly, a hedge 

against  something  is  “a  way  of  protecting  yourself  against  the  loss  of  something,  especially 

money” (Oxford University Press, Hedge, 2016). Hedging refers to linguistic features that reduce 

the  force  of  an  assertion  to  indicate  uncertainty,  politeness,  or  subjectivity  (Hyland,  1998a; 

Salager-Meyer, 1994). Therefore, whenever writers or speakers of a language hedge their claims 

or  propositions  (i.e.  main  elements  of  information)  in  their  discourse,  they  defend  themselves 

against something, for instance, criticism or absolute commitment. Functionally, hedging serves 

as a linguistic feature to mitigate the force of statements, functioning as a critical component of 

socially  attuned  communication  by  balancing  directness  with  politeness.  Hedging  functions  to 

mitigate the assertiveness of statements, thereby reducing the potential for face-threatening acts 

(FTAs) in communicative interactions (Al-Ghoweri & Al-Kayed, 2019. Additionally, hedging has 

been interchangeably linked with concepts like mitigation (Holmes, 1984, p. 346; Hyland, 1998b, 

p. 10) and attenuation (Hu & Cao, 2011, p. 2796). Hyland (1998a) defines hedging as “an item of 

language which a speaker uses to explicitly qualify his/her lack of commitment to the truth of a 

proposition he/she utters” (p. 281). According to Hyland (1998a), hedging can be used to indicate 

either: “(a) a lack of complete commitment to the truth value of an accompanying proposition or 

(b) a desire not to express that commitment categorically” (p.1). The following example illustrates 

the use of hedging in English:  

(a) All I know is that smoking is harmful to your health.  

The  hedging  phrase  All  I  know  functions  to  qualify  the  speaker’s  epistemic  certainty, 

signaling that the proposition smoking is harmful is framed not as an absolute truth but as a claim 

depends the speaker’s subjective knowledge and evaluative capacity. According to Fraser (2007, 

 
 
 
 
 
                                                                         
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
39 

p. 201), 

Hedging as a rhetorical strategy is associated with all means that lead to lack of a  full 

commitment.  By  including  a  particular  term,  choosing  a  particular  structure,  or

imposing a specific prosodic form on the utterance, the speaker signals a lack of a full 

commitment  either  to  the  full  category  membership  of  a  term  or  expression  in  the 

utterance (content mitigation), or to the intended illocutionary force of the  utterance 

(force mitigation).  

Hedging is a linguistic feature that can establish an interaction between the writer and the 

reader  in  the  text  and  create  a  relationship  between  the  writer  and  the  message  intended  to  be 

expressed.  That  is  to  say,  whenever  a  writer  hedges  his/her  propositions,  he/she  also  aims  to 

strengthen his/her relationship with his/her readers since he/she avoids imposing claims or views 

on them. Moreover, a speaker can state a proposition as fact by inserting a boosting feature (e.g. 

this medicine will help you recover quickly), or he/she can use a hedging feature to avoid complete 

responsibility when he/she presents a claim (e.g. I believe that this medicine could help you recover 

quickly). 

3.1.3 Concept of Boosting 

Boosting  in  metadiscourse  refers  to  a  linguistic  feature  used  by  writers  or  speakers  to 

express a high level of certainty, commitment, or emphasis in their statements. Unlike hedging, 

which signals doubt or caution, boosting strengthens claims, making them appear more assertive, 

authoritative,  and  persuasive  (Hyland,  2005a).  Boosting  encompasses  linguistic  features  such 

as clearly, obviously, undoubtedly, in  fact,  and of  course,  which  signal  the  author/speaker’s 

conviction in their propositions. Contrary to hedging features, boosting has received comparatively 

less  scholarly  scrutiny  (Vassileva,  2001).However,  both  hedging  and  boosting  are  important 

linguistic features, which writers or speakers use in persuasive texts to indicate both doubt and 

certainty in their propositions. Boosting and hedging features are categorized as epistemic modality 

(Martin, 2000), which demonstrates a writer or speaker’s confidence or lack of confidence in the 

propositional information that he/she presents (Coates, 2003). To persuade the reader into adopting 

a certain view and express a degree of  confidence, the writer uses boosting features such as of 

course, clear, clearly, certainly, definitely, obviously, and so on. According to Hyland (1998a), 

boosting is used to support a writer’s statement or argument. Boosting functions to emphasize the 

writer’s conviction in a proposition and persuade the audience by increasing the argument’s logical 

 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                                                                                                                                             
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
40 

rigor  and  persuasive  impact.  Similarly,  writers  use  boosting  to  comment  impersonally  on  the 

validity of their propositions. “Boosters are emphatic features, which allow writers to close down 

alternatives, avoid conflicting views, and express their certainty in what they say” (Hyland, 2005a, 

p.  52).  As  Hyland  (2005a)  clearly  states  that,  “by  closing  down  possible  alternatives,  boosters 

emphasize certainty and construct rapport by marking involvement with the topic and solidarity 

with an audience, taking a joint position against other voices” (p. 53). 

According to Hyland (1998b), boosters or emphatics are defined through their function as 

the markers that help their users to express opinions, state a suggestion with confidence in their 

knowledge  of  the  topic,  and  reduce  the  likelihood  of  acknowledging  or  pursuing  alternative 

possibilities. Various terminologies, such as certainty markers, emphatics, and modality markers, 

have  been  proposed  to  characterize  the  linguistic  expression  of  confidence  in  a  proposition. 

Accordingly, scholars have conceptualized a continuum of certainty to delineate the spectrum of 

assertiveness  conveyed  by  writers,  ranging  from  complete  certainty  to  tentative  claims,  with 

varying levels in between (Holmes, 1982; Rubin, 2007). Moreover, Hyland (2008) suggests that 

boosters might help bring readers to the text or involve them in building some kind of solidarity. 

Holmes  (1982)  argues  that  NEs,  functioning  as  the  institutional  voice  of  newspapers,  employ 

pragmatic  boosters  to  assertively  articulate  the  publication’s  stance  on  contemporary  issues, 

thereby  reinforcing  its  editorial  authority.  Boosting  features  include:  Modal  verbs  (must,  will, 

cannot).  Adverbs  of  certainty  (undoubtedly,  clearly,  certainly),  emphatic  lexical  verbs 

(demonstrates,  proves,  confirms),  superlatives  and  intensifiers  (the  most  significant,  extremely, 

highly).  

Jalilifar and Alavi-Nai (2012) classifies boosters into: 

(1) Propositional  boosters, which include intensifiers and personal  involvement pronouns (e.g., 

must, will, clearly, undoubtedly, and so on).   

(2) Illocutionary force boosters, which include boosting epistemic commitment (e.g., prove, assert, 

confirm, considerably, of course, and so on).  

(3) Content oriented boosters, which include source tagging and bounding emphatics (the most 

obvious, the most significant, no doubt, and so on).     

(4) Hearer oriented boosters, which aim at seeking solidarity and presupposing verification (it is 

clearly, as we know, strictly speaking, and so on).             

3.1.4 Form and Function of Hedging and Boosting Features 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                                                         
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
             
 
 
 
 
        
 
 
 
 
        
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
41 

Linguistic form and function are inseparably interconnected, a reciprocal relationship that 

has been extensively theorized and analyzed in scholarly inquiry. According to Fraser (2010), “the 

connectedness  between  the  form  and  function  of  a  hedge  is  at  the  heart  of  investigation  in 

pragmatics”  (p.27).  Richards  and  Schmidt  (2010)  state  that  form-function  relation  is  “the 

relationship between the physical characteristics of a thing (i.e. its form) and its role or function” 

(p.208).  For  instance,  a  linguistic  form  (e.g.  modal  verbs)  can  perform  a  variety  of  different 

functions such as hedging, boosting, polite requests, possibility, and so on (Amiryousefi & Rasekh, 

2010).  To  put  it  differently,  every  linguistic  form  is  intrinsically  tied  to  a  distinct  function  or 

meaning within a specific context. Form include lexical and grammatical elements (such as main 

verbs, adjectives, adverbs, nouns, and modal auxiliaries) that serve as fundamental structures in 

most languages, and function refers to the social and pragmatic purpose of using such forms. The 

question  that  always  raised  by  scholars:  Is  there  a  definite  list  of  invariable  forms  that  always 

function  as  hedging  and  boosting  features.  For  several  researchers,  providing  a  definite  list  of 

hedging  or  boosting  features  feasible  to  various  contexts  is  next  to  impossible.  Hedging  and 

boosting  do  not  have  a  one-to-one  correspondence  between  form  and  function,  as  their 

interpretation depends heavily on contextual and discursive variables. A single linguistic structure 

(e.g.,  modal  verbs,  adverbs)  may  serve  as  a  hedge,  a  booster,  or  neither,  depending  on  its 

communicative  context.  This  functional  flexibility  complicates  attempts  to  categorize  lexico-

grammatical forms into fixed pragmatic functions. Furthermore, the diverse discursive contexts in 

which  these  features  operate  necessitate  adaptable  frameworks  to  account  for  their  varied 

realizations across genres and communicative purposes. “Particular items can therefore only be 

understood as expressions of doubt and certainty by attending to the context in which they occur. 

Meanings do not reside in the items themselves but are assigned to utterances which contain them” 

(Fraser, 2010, 223). In other words, since there is no single taxonomy of hedging and boosting that 

is feasible to analyze all types of speech and writing discourse, researchers often conduct a manual 

contextual  analysis  to  approach  such  features  and  create  their  own  taxonomies  to  specifically 

identify  occurrences  of  such  linguistic  features  and  deal  with  their  data  sets.  Researchers  may 

choose to  adopt  a  formal  taxonomy  (e.g.,  Hinkel, 2005;  Salager-Meyer,  1994, 2010;  &  Fraser, 

2010),  where  categorization  is  based  on  the  grammatical  classification  of  each  feature,  or  a 

functional taxonomy (e.g., Prince et al., 1980, Hyland 1998a, 2005a), where categorization is based 

on the function of such features and their contextual use.   

 
 
                                                          
42 

3.1.4.1 Forms of Hedging and Boosting Features   

a) Modality 

The first category of hedging and boosting is modality. Modality refers to a range of lexical 

and grammatical forms such as modal verbs, adjectives, adverbs, and nouns that allow speakers 

and  writers  to  denote  degrees  of  tentativeness,  emphasis,  likelihood,  and  projection  of  future 

events.  These  forms  are  frequently  employed  to  hedge  or  boost  claims,  express  possibility  or 

probability, and convey predictions or obligations within discourse. According to Hyland (1998b), 

modality  is  one  principal  means  by  which  writers  convey  hedging  that  is,  expressions  of 

uncertainty  or  tentative  stance  and  is  likely  the  most  pervasive  hedging  strategy  employed  by 

English users. One of the most universally common realizations of modality across languages is 

the  modal  verb,  which  Zhang  and  Chen  (2020)  observe  to  be  particularly  frequent  in  news 

commentary,  serving  to  denote  the  author’s  stance  toward  their  claims.  Extensive  research  on 

English (Hyland 1998a; 1998b) and cross‐linguistic research have emphasized that modal verbs 

regularly indicate epistemic meanings  such as doubt, possibility, confidence, and certainty thus 

functioning as key features of a speaker’s or writer’s epistemic attitude. Modal verbs such as may, 

might, should, could, would, can, must, and shall function as self-referential linguistic features that 

indicate epistemic modality, allowing speakers or writers to soften, strengthen, or otherwise shape 

the force of their statements. As Varttala (2001) observes, these verbs help authors reinforce their 

stance  within  a  discourse.  Lyons  (1977,  p.797)  further  clarifies  that,  unlike  deontic  modality 

(which  deals  with  obligation  and  permission),  epistemic  modality  specifically  involves  the 

speaker’s  indication  of  how  firmly  they  modify  the  truth  of  the  proposition  they  express.  The 

writer/speaker  uses  epistemic  modality  to  convey  his  or  her  level  of  knowledge  and  beliefs 

regarding the information presented as shown in the following example:  

(a) Beginner readers may not even know the phrase meaning.   

(b) It was not his war (although he did nothing to stop it), and he would like to move on.              

(c) He must act now to help prevent a repetition.  

However,  deontic  modality  occurs  to  convey  obligation,  possibility,  necessity,  and 

permission and reflects the speaker/writer’s “attitude towards the desirability (or non-desirability) 

of certain actions or events” (Lyons, 1977 as cited in Fraser, 2010, p.67). In example (c) above, 

must is used to express deontic modality indicating the necessity of Brown’s action in order to stop 

the war in Iraq. Epistemic modality, therefore, is related to the speaker/writer’s knowledge and 

 
 
 
 
                                 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                     
 
 
                              
 
 
 
 
 
 
43 

beliefs  towards  the  information  he/she  presents  or  related  to  his/her  confidence  or  lack  of 

confidence  towards  the  truth  of  the  proposition  expressed.  However,  deontic  modality,  Lyons 

(1977) states, is related to the necessity or possibility of acts performed by morally responsible 

agents.  Deontic  modality  refers  to  expressions  of  obligation,  duty,  volition,  and  reflects  the 

speaker/writer’s attitude toward the desirability (or nondesirability) of certain actions or events. 

This distinction depends on the function and context within which these modals occur (Al-Ajmi, 

2015, p.31). To put it simply, hedging and boosting are closely related to epistemic modality, as 

they  both  reflect  the  speaker’s  level  of  knowledge  about  the  proposition  or  their  degree  of 

confidence in what they claim. 

Martin-Martin  (2008,  p.29)  refers  to  hedges  and  boosters  by  using  the  term  epistemic 

modalities.  He  states  that  “writers  use  epistemic  modalities  to  communicate  their  academic 

knowledge  in  a  way  which  permits  them  to  get  community  acceptance  of  their  academic 

contributions  excluding  the  risk  of  Face  Threatening  Acts.”  According  to  Palmer  (2006,  p.2), 

“English  uses  a  modal  verb  to  distinguish  a  judgment  about  a  proposition  from  a  categorical 

statement” as the following examples show: (a) Mary is at home (factual proposition) (b) Mary 

may  be  at  home  (epistemic  modal  verb,  low  degree  of  probability)  (c)  Mary  must  be  at  home 

(epistemic modal verb, high degree of probability). These functions are typically epistemic since, 

they denote the speaker’s assessment of a proposition’s truth value , and other deontic functions, 

which involve the meanings of obligation, necessity, ability, or permission of the verbal  event. 

Unlike the Arabic modal system, the system of modal verbs in English is rather complex as most 

of modal verbs are polysemous. In English, modal verbs such as can, shall, will, could, should, 

would, may, and must might serve two main functions or more depending on the context of their 

use.   

Hyland  (1994)  states  that  modal  adjectives  such  as  possible,  probable,  potential,  and 

apparent serve to convey varying degrees of certainty or uncertainty. Hyland (1998a) argues that 

modal adjectives as hedging features are used more frequently than other subcategories of modality 

in RAs because they function to downgrade the writer’s commitment towards the claim presented. 

Varttala (2001, p.125) states that modal adjectives “can be used to indicate tentative, uncertain and 

not quite precise characteristics of nouns or actions.” Modal adverbs such as probably, possibly, 

apparently, likely, unlikely, and others are also used at comparable rates with modal adjectives as 

hedging features to show some degree of tentativeness towards the proposition the writer presents 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                       
 
 
 
 
 
                           
 
44 

in  the  text.  They  are  used  to  affect  the  proposition’s  meaning  because  of  their  function  as 

downgraders (Hyland, 1998a). Modal nouns are nouns that function similarly to modal adjectives 

and  adverbs  as  they  might  modify  epistemically  the  writer’s  proposition  (e.g.,  likelihood, 

assumption, claim, estimate, possibility, suggestion, and so on). 

b) Epistemic Lexical Verbs   

The  second  category  of  hedging  and  boosting  is  epistemic  lexical  verbs.  This  category, 

which includes such verbs as believe, suggest, indicate, assume, tend to, see, assert, confirm, and 

others, indicate various degrees of the writer/speaker’s knowledge or cognition (Hyland, 1998a). 

These  verbs  serve  to  modify  the  speaker’s  or  writer’s  absolute  commitment  to  the  claims  they 

present.  They  can  also  indicate  a  particular  degree  of  doubting  or  uncertainty  presenting  a 

subjective opinion or suggestion rather than a fact (Perkins, 1983, as cited in Hyland, 1994, p.248). 

Carrio-Pastor (2017) argues that writers are more likely to use these verbs to shape their stance on 

a topic, to the amplify claims they make in their research, and to reduce the risk of the reader’s 

opposition. Non-factive reporting verbs (e.g. suggest, argue, state, and others) are an important 

subcategory of epistemic lexical verbs, which are used to report other writer’s results or describe 

the author’s own research. Another subcategory of such verbs is tentative cognition verbs (e.g., 

hope, think, suspect,  realize,  and others), which  indicate the mental  status  of the author whose 

work is being reported. “The information they (i.e. tentative cognition verbs) introduce in one way 

or another based on subjective cognitive activity rather than uncontroversial empirical evidence” 

(Varttala,  2001,  p.122).  In  other  words,  tentative  cognition  verbs  are  used  to  indicate  the 

writer/speaker’s confidant imprecision rather than his/her lack of knowledge.   

c) Introductory Phrases/Verbs 

Introductory phrases or verbs may have a similar function and meaning to epistemic lexical 

verbs (e.g., to  our knowledge, it is  our view that, it is  worth  mentioning,  we confirm  that,  and 

others) in that they can hedge or boost a proposition. This category may indicate writer/speaker’s 

uncertainty, certainty, imprecision, or precision towards the claim he/she presents (Salager-Meyer, 

2010).  As  a  boosting  feature,  it  can  function  to  increase  the  writer  or  speaker’s  confidence  or 

certainty (e.g., it is clear, it is certain, and so on).  

d) Adverbs and Adjectives of Degree, Frequency, Quantity or Time (Approximators) 

Adverbs of degree, frequency, quantity or time (e.g., approximately, roughly, about, often, 

occasionally,  sometimes,  generally,  seldom,  usually,  somewhat,  rarely,  somehow,  frequently, 

 
 
 
 
          
                 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
            
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                      
 
 
45 

never,  completely,  and  others),  might  indicate  the  indefiniteness  or  definiteness  towards  the 

meaning, entity, or number presented by the writer/speaker. Such adverbs allow writers to hedge 

against error or objection particularly when precise data are unavailable (Varttala, 2001, p.122). 

Varttala (2001, p.132) further explains that they soften a statement’s impact by avoiding to provide 

exact figures essentially approximating the verb’s force. These adverbs can appear before a verb 

to lessen its strength (e.g. I almost resigned), can give tentative approximations avoiding to provide 

exact  numerical  information  (e.g.  about  50  percent),  or  pre-modify  pronouns  (e.g.  almost 

everyone).  Hedging  adverbs  primarily  function  to  engage  readers  in  a  dialogue  by  avoiding  to 

provide  rigid  claims  or  total  commitment  (Ibrahim,  Kadir  &  Abbas,  2019).  Other  adverbs  of 

frequency “seek to  express  only part of the potential  force of the item  concerned” (e.g.,  fairly, 

slightly,  considerably,  greatly,  largely,  partly,  mostly,  relatively,  significantly,  completely, 

obviously, and so on) (Ibrahim et al., 2019). Adjectives denoting degree, frequency, quantity, or 

time (e.g., common, frequent, rare, popular, or typical) can likewise indicate an unspecified level, 

frequency, or time.  In other words, they might indicate tentative quantifications where it is not 

exactly necessary, known, or possible to quantify entities (Ibrahim et al., 2019). All these types of 

adjectives help writers “draw attention to the approximate nature of the information presented” 

(Varttala, 2001, p.125). 

e) Conditional Clauses and Clausal Elements 

 Conditional clauses indicate the circumstances that enable a speaker or writer to modify 

claims  tentatively  or  non-definitively  and  avoid  providing  absolute  assertions  and  categorical 

commitments  (Fraser,  2010).  (e.g.  If  there  is  no  solution  to  such  crisis,  this  will  particularly 

threaten  the  lives  of  hundreds  of  people).  Other  forms  of  conditional  clause  include  if  true,  if 

nothing, if anything, and others (e.g. If true, then, the study contradicts the myth that fishing attracts 

the bravest and strongest men). Hyland (1998a, p.141) categorizes conditional clauses functioning 

as hedges as non-lexical hedges, since the clause rather than a word or expression which affects 

the truth condition of the proposition. Other clausal elements functioning as hedges or boosters 

include sentences containing tentative words or concessive conjunctions such as although, though, 

while, whereas, even though, even if, and others (e.g. although he improved his speaking skills, he 

spoke very awkwardly in the meeting).    

f) Rhetorical Questions  

Rhetorical questions convey the speaker’s viewpoint without expecting an answer, serving 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                       
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                                                                   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
46 

instead  to  assert  imprecisely  and  tentatively  a  claim.  Salager-Meyer  (2010)  states  that  these 

questions  functioning  as  hedges  are  the  most  effective  linguistic  features  in  developing 

argumentation and establishing a relationship between the writer and reader (e.g. can the president 

avoid solving the crisis of fuel and housing?).   

g) Compound Hedging and Boosting 

In  addition  to  previous  categories  of  hedging,  Salager-Meyer  (1994)  proposes  another 

hedging  category  labeling  it  as  compound  hedging.  Compound  hedging  refers  to  multiple 

categories  of  hedging  occurring  together  in  a  proposition.  One  of  the  most  frequent  forms  of 

compound hedges and boosters in English includes:   

1- A modal verb combined with a lexical verb functioning as a hedge or booster (e.g., it would 

appear, it will confirm, a double feature). 

2- A lexical verb combined with an adverb or adjective (e.g., it seems probable, it seems likely 

that, it confirms clearly, or double feature). 

3- A lexical  verb followed by an adverb or adjective followed by a lexical  verb (e.g., it seems 

reasonable to assume that, a triple feature). 

4- A modal verb followed by a lexical verb followed by two adverbs or adjectives (e.g., it would 

seem somewhat unlikely that, a quadruple feature).    

3.1.4.2 Functions of Hedging and Boosting 

 Several  linguists  (Hyland,  1994,  1998a,  1998b;  Salager-Meyer;  1994,  2010;  Skelton, 

1988; Fraser 1990, 2010; and others) have identified particular pragmatic functions that hedging 

and  boosting  features  have  in  addition  to  the  semantic  functions  presented  previously.  Their 

classifications  of  these  features  were  based  on  functional  considerations.  As  linguistic  features 

used  in  oral  or  written  texts,  hedging  and  boosting  vary  in  their  function  within  different 

communicative situations. Hyland (2005a) and Hinkel (2009) state that modal verbs can perform 

diverse  pragmatic  and  textual  functions  indicating  aspects  such  as  likelihood,  possibility, 

evidentiality,  politeness,  strategic  vagueness,  as  well  as  uncertainty  and  certainty  in  discourse. 

Similarly, hedging  and boosting  can serve different  functions that can  change according to the 

context of propositional situation itself. A speaker can state a proposition as fact by using a booster 

(e.g.  this  medicine  will  help  you  recover  quickly),  or  he/she  can  use  a  hedge  to  distance 

himself/herself  from  a  claim  (e.g.  I  believe  that  this  medicine  could  help  you  recover  quickly. 

Skelton (1988) argues that hedging might show distance (as cited in Salager-Meyer, 1994). In other 

 
 
                                                                  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
             
 
 
               
                                                                                                   
                                                                                                                                             
 
 
 
 
 
                     
 
 
                  
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
47 

words, hedging allows the writer or speaker to show distance between himself/herself and his/her 

assertions, enabling him/her  their stance especially when there is a possibility their claims might 

face criticism or rejection within their discourse community(Hyland, 1998b). According to Holmes 

(1988, p.22), hedging can be considered as a politeness feature that indicate “deference rather than 

uncertainty”. To put it differently, one important pragmatic function of hedging is to indicate the 

writer/speaker’s  politeness  and  respect  towards  the  reader/listener,  since  by  using  hedging  the 

speaker/writer  avoid  imposing  views  on  the  listener/reader.  Moreover,  according  to  Varttala 

(1999), hedging can function as an interpersonal positive politeness feature, which helps the writer 

build  a  strong  relationship  with  the  reader  and  draw  the  reader  to  participate  in  the  academic 

conversation.  Halliday  and  Hasan  (1985)  argue  that  every  utterance  carries  not  only  ideational 

content but also interpersonal meaning. In other words, hedging is a key interpersonal feature of 

linguistic utterances. Hyland (1998a; 1998b) claims that hedging can function as downtoners and 

can be divided into four categories, namely: (a) Compromiser (e.g., quite, usually, normally, and 

others);  (b)  diminisher  (e.g.,  partially,  slightly,  and  others);  (c)  minimizers  (e.g.,  rarely, 

occasionally, and others); and (d) approximators (e.g., almost, virtually, & relatively). 

Salager-Meyer (1994) provides five main categories of hedging based on their functional 

objectives:  

(a) Shields encompass  modal  verbs  and  semi-auxiliary  verbs 

that  denote  possibility 

(e.g., may, would, must, will, appear, seem),  adverbs  expressing  probability 

(e.g., probably 

, likely), and epistemic verbs (e.g., suggest, speculate).   

(b) Approximators include terms or phrases that qualify quantity, degree, frequency, or time, such 

as approximately, roughly, around, about, somewhat, quite, often, and occasionally. 

(c) Authors’  Insufficiency  and  Doubt involve  phrases  that  explicitly  signal  the  writer’s 

uncertainty or personal perspective, such as I believe, to our knowledge, it is our view that, in our 

opinion, we assume, or to the best of our understanding.       

(d) Emotionally-Charged  Expressions are  evaluative  words  or  phrases  reflecting  the  author’s 

subjective 

reactions 

such 

as extremely 

difficult, surprisingly, of 

particular 

importance,  unexpectedly, or particularly noteworthy).                       

(e) Compound Hedges combine multiple hedging devices such as may suggest, would seem likely 

that, it seems reasonable to assume, or it would appear somewhat improbable that. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                          
 
                 
             
 
                     
                      
48 

Moreover, according to Hyland (1998a), hedging can serve two primary functions: The first 

function  indicates  a  lack  of  commitment  towards  the  truth  of  proposition  on  the  part  of 

speaker/writer. The second one serves as a mitigating feature and politeness strategy to reduce the 

force and the effect of utterances in order to make the listener/reader accept what has claimed in a 

spoken or written language. Fraser (1975) introduces the term hedged performative. He states that 

certain performative verbs such as apologize, promise, and request when preceded by a specific 

modal verb could function as hedging features resulting in a mitigated and modified illocutionary 

force of the speech act as the following example below shows: (a) I should apologize for running 

over your cat. Similarly, according to several linguists (e.g. Hinkel, 1997; Swales, 1990; Swales 

&  Feak,  1994;  Salager-Meyer,  1994,  2010),  in  written  discourse,  different  lexico-grammatical 

categories of hedging are used to decrease the writer’s responsibility towards the truth-value of 

propositions  or  claims,  indicate  hesitation,  uncertainty,  indirectness,  or  politeness,  and  reduce 

imposing claims on the reader. The following two examples were adopted from Varttala’s (2001) 

model of hedging clarify the point:  

1. This drug may help.   

2. Penguins are sort of birds.   

The use of hedging features in these two examples may and sort of, functions to indicate a 

particular  degree  of  imprecision  in  the  utterances  and  that  the  writer/speaker  wants  to  modify 

his/her commitment regarding the accuracy of what is claimed (Varttala 2001, as cited in Tahririan 

& Shahzamani, 2009). Similarly, boosting as a metadiscourse feature serves several functions in 

academic  and  persuasive  writing.  According  to  Hyland  (2005b),  boosters  are  features  that 

emphasize certainty and conviction, helping writers strengthen their claims and persuade readers. 

They  indicate  the  writer’s  complete  commitment  to  a  proposition  and  enhance  credibility  and 

assertiveness  in  arguments.  Boosters  such  as  clearly,  undoubtedly,  and  certainly  express 

confidence,  reducing  potential  ambiguity  in  communication  (Hyland,  2000).  Additionally, 

boosting serves to build a solid relationship with readers by emphasizing common knowledge or 

widely recognized ideas (Crismore & Farnsworth, 1990). Writers or speakers may use boosting 

features to harmonize their perspective with the audience, thereby enhancing the persuasiveness 

of their claims (Hyland, 1998b). 

3.1.5 Genre of Newspaper Editorial (NE)    

According  to  Swales  (1990),  “genre  refers  to  language  use  in  a  conventionalized 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
             
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                    
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                    
 
 
 
 
 
49 

communicative  setting  in  order  to  give  expression  to  a  specific  set  of  communicative  goals” 

(p.122).  In other words,  genre  encompasses established structures or  recurring  elements  within 

texts,  shaped  by  specific  contexts  that  a  discourse  community  employs  to  engage  in  shared 

communication. These are specified frameworks or conventions recognized by the community to 

facilitate purposeful interaction and achieve collective goals (Swales, 1990). In constructing a text, 

the writer is supposed to employ certain features that are conventionally associated with such text 

constituting the genre that he or she is writing in. Genre can be defined as a category of text shaped 

by  the  social  and  cultural  norms  and  conventions  expected  by  its  intended  audience.  In  this 

definition, text types are defined by the communicative purposes these texts achieve (i.e. NEs as a 

genre  have  a  particular  communicative  purpose  such  as  persuasion).  Therefore,  texts  can  be 

categorized  into  specific  genres  according  to  their  primary  communicative  aims  and  stylistic 

characteristics.  Several scholars (e.g., Swales, 1990; Bhatia, 2004; Ansary & Babaii, 2009) argue 

that  genres  inherently  possess  distinct  features  such  as  linguistic,  paralinguistic,  contextual,  or 

pragmatic  elements  that  enable  writers  to  fulfill  the  genre’s  intended  purpose.  These 

characteristics,  however,  may  vary  across  different  discourses  and  text  types,  even  within  or 

between languages. 

Newspapers contain different types of articles or genres that may have a different pattern 

of linguistic and discursive properties. These various types of genre according to Bhatia (1993) 

include: Headlines, news reports, sports reports, newspaper editorials (NEs), news stories, letters, 

advertisements, and others. Scholars define NEs as a genre of written argumentative discourse that 

has  a  persuasive  and  argumentative  purpose,  and  they  are  considered  as  the  most  influential, 

rhetorically charged, and discourse-oriented genre in newspaper contexts (Al-Khatib, Wachsmuth, 

Kiesel, Hagen, & Stein, 2016, p. 3433). In NEs, a writer may state and defend a claim that conveys 

his or her stance on a controversial topic usually related to the public interest (Al-Khatib et al., 

2016). Discourse analysts (e.g., Al-Khatib et al., 2016) identify some general global characteristics 

of  NEs:  First,  these  articles  articulate  the  publication’s  official  stance  on  a  topic  and  are 

consistently  located  in  a  designated  section  of  the  newspaper.  Second,  they  are  typically 

anonymous, with lengths spanning 200 to 1,500 words. Third, newspaper editors (NErs) construct 

structured arguments aimed at convincing readers to adopt their viewpoint. Fourth, NEs aim to 

shape public opinion, stimulate critical thinking, and occasionally prompt action regarding specific 

issues (Al-Khatib et al., 2016). NEs tackle topics that are “considered to be of particular societal 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
50 

importance at the time of publication” (Le, 2004, p.688), and they represent the official stance of 

the newspaper. However, they do not only aim to persuade readers to adopt a particular opinion; 

but they also defend particular ideologies or certain attitudes in the discourse community (Van 

Dijk, 1992). 

Moreover, “there are certain conventions in writing editorials and these conventions might 

be different  cross-culturally”  (Ansary  & Babaii,  2009,  p.2). To put  it differently, the typology, 

structure,  rhetorical  strategies,  and  metadiscoursal  features  of  NEs  may  differ  across  discourse 

communities or languages. Ansary and Babaii (2009) regard NEs as a compelling genre for cross-

cultural analysis, emphasizing their persuasive nature, public accessibility, and role in reflecting 

cultural and ideological frameworks. Similarly, Zarza (2016) states that NEs are valuable sites for 

analyzing  strategic  language  use  in  persuasion  and  argumentation,  particularly  how  linguistic 

features  such  as  hedging  and  boosting  establish  a  writer-reader  interactional  relationship  and 

demonstrate perspectives on certain issues.  The use of metadiscourse features such as hedging 

and  boosting  enables  NEs  to  develop  arguments,  enhance  persuasiveness,  present  claims  with 

precision, and shape ideologies, particularly within socio-cultural contexts (Van Dijk, 1989, 1992).                                            

1.5.1 Hedging and Boosting in Media Discourse: Newspaper Editorials (NEs)  

Within  media  discourse,  hedging  functions  to  harmonize  persuasive  language  with 

journalistic  neutrality  (Dafouz-Milne,  2008).  In  contrast,  boosting  amplifies  assertions  by 

conveying certainty, emphasis, and persuasive force (Hyland, 2005a). Boosting is often used in 

NEs to defend ideological positions, to shape public opinion, and reinforce credibility (Dafouz-

Milne,  2008;  Bednarek  &  Caple,  2017).  Boosting  features  such  as  undoubtedly,  clearly,  and 

without  a  doubt  enhance  the  persuasive  effect  of  media  discourse  (Hyland,  2000).  In  media 

narratives,  boosting  is  employed  to  strengthen  trustworthiness,  elicit  emotional  reactions,  and 

solidify prevailing perspectives (Martin & White, 2005). The strategic and balanced use of these 

metadiscourse  elements  influences  how  audience  interprets  news  and  engages  with  mediated 

realities.  While  hedging  may  be  used  to  maintain  neutrality  and  credibility,  boosting  is  often 

employed to assert dominance, persuade the audience, and frame narratives in a particular direction 

(Bell, 1991). Boosting is particularly prevalent in political and ideological discourse, where media 

seek to persuade readers (Van Dijk, 2006). As previously mentioned, in order to achieve successful 

persuasion  and  argumentation,  writers  or  speakers  should  use  a  reasonable  number  of 

metadiscourse markers including hedging and boosting in addition to other elements of persuasion 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
51 

and  argumentation.  Rodríguez  (2008)  demonstrates  that  the  argumentative,  evaluative,  and 

persuasive  nature  of  opinion  discourse  (e.g.  NEs)  requires  the  use  of  both  facts  as  well  as 

assessments  in  presenting  such  opinions.  In  other  words,  NEs  require  a  frequent  use  of 

metadiscourse features in order to develop arguments, express the writers’ opinion, and persuade 

the reader (Vande Kopple & Crismore, 1997; Dafouz-Milne, 2008; Khabbazi-Oskouei, 2012).  

Hedging and boosting, if used precisely, can support the writer’s opinion and build a strong 

writer-reader relationship. They are argued to be powerful features in persuading the readers and 

in  influencing  their  attitudes  about  the  subject  matter  covered  in  the  text  (Vande  Kopple  & 

Crismore,  1997;  Dafouz-Milne,  2008;  Khabbazi-Oskouei,  2012;  Vold,  2006).  Brooke  (2016) 

argues that “persuasive or argumentative genres should elicit more hedging than other genres, as 

making claims and stating opinions are inherent in formulating an argument” (p.2). In other words, 

NEs  are  supposed  to  contain  a  more  frequent  number  of  hedging  and  boosting  since  they  are 

written to persuade the reader and negotiate the truth of the proposition. Moreover, whenever the 

writer/speaker  employs  hedging  or  boosting,  he/she  presents  his  claims  or  views  precisely  and 

diplomatically, and thus he/she attempts to build a strong relationship with the reader to achieve 

persuasive and argumentative purposes. Similarly, since NEs usually focus on national economic, 

social, and political issues, writers are supposed to be more cautious or precise in presenting their 

claims; therefore, they mainly  can hedge or boost their propositions to avoid any confrontation 

with their readers (Hyland, 1998a, 1999). NErs may threaten the newspaper’s reputation if they 

are not cautious in presenting their claims or views. To mitigate the effects of such claims, attack, 

or persuasion, the use of hedging is therefore important in NEs (Nugroho, 2014).   

Van de Kopple and Crismore (1997), exploring readers’ reactions to hedging, concludes 

that  students  read  hedged  texts  more  evaluatively  than  unhedged  texts.  Dafouz-Milne  (2008) 

argues  that  “the  key  to  an  effectively  persuasive  text  is  the  artful  combination  of  weakening 

expressions (i.e. hedges) and strengthening ones (i.e.certainty markers and/or attitudinal markers) 

with the final intention of producing a discourse that is neither too assertive nor too vague” (p.193). 

Similarly,  when  presenting  information  in  the  text,  the  author  by  using  hedging  presents  this 

information as opinions instead of facts (Hyland, 2005b; Zarza, 2016), and thus he/she avoids any 

confrontation with the reader. Buitkienė (2008), investigating language of newspaper discourse, 

argues  that  NEs  use  hedging  features  more  frequently  than  news  stories.  He  attributes  these 

findings to the communicative function of NEs, which is to persuade the reader, while the function 

 
 
 
52 

of  news  stories  to  inform  him/her.  Similarly,  an  advertisement  may  use  different  hedging  and 

boosting  features  compared  to  a  news  story  article,  since  they  both  have  different  purposes. 

Advertisements  function  to  persuade  readers  to  buy  a  certain  product,  whereas  news  stories 

function  to  provide  information,  facts,  and  events  (Al-Rickaby,  2016;  Zarza,  2018).  To  put  it 

differently, NErs tend to use various categories of rhetorical features (e.g., hedging, boosting, and 

so on) to develop arguments, achieve persuasion, and to influence their readers’ opinions (Zarza, 

2018). In English-language newspapers, according to Dafouz-Milne (2008) hedging is often used 

to: 

a) Avoid absolute claims: It seems that the economic policy may have unintended consequences.    

b) Acknowledge alternative perspectives: Some analysts suggest that this approach could lead to 

instability.  

c) Mitigate risk in legal or political statements: There is reason to believe that the new regulations 

might be effective.  

However, boosting is used to strengthen arguments in NEs, to persuade the reader, and to 

establish authority (Hyland, 2005a). Dafouz-Milne (2008) reports that boosting is more frequently 

used  in  persuasive  and  ideological  texts,  particularly  in  politically  charged  NEs,  which  often 

employ boosting to: 

a) Assert strong opinions: This is undoubtedly the best course of action.      

c) Reject opposing views: There is no question that this policy is flawed.     

c) Emphasize urgency or importance: We must act now to prevent further crisis. 

Hyland  argues  that  hedging  and  boosting  in  newspaper  discourse  “work  to  balance 

objective  information,  subjective  evaluation  and  interpersonal  negotiation,  and  this  can  be  a 

powerful persuasive factor in gaining acceptance for claims” (1998a, p. 353). Moreover, research 

reports that hedging and boosting are used differently in Arabic journalistic discourse determined 

by  cultural  and  rhetorical  traditions  (Al-Khatib  &  Salem,  2011;  Najjar,  1990).  According  to 

Salager-Meyer (1994), the ENEs tend to prefer indirectness and deference to authority. However, 

the ANEs are likely to prefer directness and explicit argumentation. Arabic media often hedge a 

claim or proposition as a politeness strategy to avoid direct confrontation (Al-Khatib & Salem, 

2011) and tend to use stronger rhetorical features and repetition as boosting features to enhance 

persuasion (Najjar, 1990). Unlike English discourse, Arabic discourse often appeal to emotion and 

collective  values,  reinforcing  community-based  rhetoric  (Al-Khatib  &  Salem,  2011).  In  other 

 
                                                                                                                                                    
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                      
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                                     
 
 
                                             
 
                                   
 
                    
                 
 
53 

words, Arabic discourse, as affected by the collectivistic values of Arabic community, tend to use 

more boosting features and less hedging ones. 

3.1.6 Hedging and Boosting in Modern Standard Arabic (MSA)  

3.1.6.1 Approaches and Views  

There is a notable lack of research investigating hedging and boosting features in Arabic, 

particularly  in  Modern  Standard  Arabic  (MSA).  This  is  probably  can  be  explained  that  these 

features have only recently been recognized as phenomena within Arabic linguistics and discourse 

analytic  approaches  (Al-Kholani,  2010;  Al-Semeiri,  2018).  In  other  words,  while  extensive 

research  has  explored  hedging  and  boosting  in  English,  limited  attention  has  been  given  to 

examining their structure, usage, and prevalence in Arabic. Hedging and boosting are significantly 

unexplored  in  previous  research  on  Arabic  rhetoric  and  discourse  analysis.  However,  concepts 

such as particles, connectives, and discourse markers (DMs) have been extensively studied either 

in research in MSA or in other Arabic varieties (Al-Semeiri, 2018). Al-Kholani (2010) states that 

pragmatic  markers  (PMs),  which  constitute  hedging,  boosting,  and  other  markers,  have  been 

approached differently in the literature of Arabic linguistics and discourse analysis approaches. 

However, the Arabic concept hūrūf (particles/فورح) has been used to label discourse markers such 

as hedging, boosting, and other features. Furthermore, hedging and has not been approached in 

Arabic as it has in English, and their structures, use, and understanding are obviously different 

from  English.  There  have  been,  to  the  best  of  the  researcher’s  knowledge,  very  little  research 

focusing on hedging and boosting in Arabic (e.g., Safi, 1988; Khuwaileh, 2006; Al-Kholani, 2010; 

Abbadi, 2014; Al-Semeiri, 2018).           

Research  on  Arabic  discourse  have  predominantly  focused  on  applying  comparative 

frameworks  from  English  hedging  and  boosting  to  analyze  analogous  pragmatic  functions  in 

Arabic structures. According to Abbas’ (2011) findings, hedging occurred in every 77 words in 

English academic discourse while they appeared once every 100 words in Arabic academic one. 

Al-Kholani (2010) states that traditional Arab linguists, such as Safi (1988), Abbadi (2014), and 

Al-Jurjani  (1984),  focused  their  investigations  narrowly  at  the  sentence  level  in  analyzing 

linguistic and metadiscursive elements. In fact, Arab linguists were not interested in the study of 

discourse markers, but they were concerned with the concept of particles, which were the source 

forms of many markers and were studied extensively in their research. Similarly, these scholars 

offered  detailed  accounts  of  such  linguistic  elements  (i.e.,  particles),  classifying  them  as  a 

 
                                      
 
                
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
54 

grammatical  category  operating  strictly  within  sentence-level  parameters.  In  Arabic  grammar, 

particles are usually referred to as hūrūf (i.e. the plural of harf, particle فرح/فورح), which were 

categorized later as hūrūfū al-mā’ani (particles of meanings/

يناعملا فورح   
 (.

Arabic and English have fundamentally distinct linguistic systems, as they originate from 

separate  language  families.  Accordingly,  we  can  find  many  hedging  or  boosting  features  and 

structures exist in Arabic discourse but do not exist in English one or vice versa. While English is 

an Indo-European language, Arabic as a Semitic language is characterized by linguistic features 

and structures different from those of English. For instance, the Arabic linguistic system does not 

have  a  similar  modal  verbal  system  equivalent  to  the  English  one;  and  similarly,  the  English 

linguistic system does not have hedging particles such as qad structure (may/would/دق), similar- 

to-verb particles such as wa ka’anna (seem as if/  نأك), and rubbama particle (may/maybe/ام بر). It 

is supposed that native Arabic speakers often compensate for the lack of a modal verb system in 

Modern  Standard  Arabic  by  employing  particles  and  lexico-grammatical  constructions. 

Conversely, English speakers address gaps in certain structural features through the utilization of 

diverse modal verb subcategories. 

However,  given  the  presence  of  linguistic  universals  across  languages,  it  can  be  found 

noticeable similarities between the two languages in terms of meaning, structure, and function of 

certain features. In the present research, in order to categorize and identify Arabic hedging and 

boosting features, the researcher adopts and modifies an English taxonomy of such features as an 

analytical framework for the Arabic corpus. Since no taxonomy of Arabic hedging and boosting 

has  been  provided,  the  researcher  attempts  to  establish  some  similarities  and  difference  by 

comparing  the  use  of  hedging  and  boosting  in  both  English  and  MSA  in  a  specific  genre.  

According to  several  linguists (e.g., Al-Jurjani, 1984;  Safi, 1988; Al-Kholani,  2010;  & others), 

hedging  and  boosting  structures  of  both  languages  are  obviously  different.  However,  there  are 

some similarities in form and function of such features between Arabic and English (Al-Semeiri, 

2018). Arab linguists specifically categorize hedging and boosting as stance markers or epistemic 

modalities  (e.g.,  Al-Jurjani,1984;  Safi,1988;  Al-Kholani,  2010;  Al-Semeiri,  2018;  and  others). 

Moreover,  although  some  research  on  Arabic  modality  in  general  has  been  done,  very  little 

research  has  been  conducted  on  epistemic  modality  in  Arabic.  For  Arab  linguistists,  hedging, 

boosting,  and  stance  are  primarily  realized  through  epistemic  lexical  verbs,  adverbial  or 

prepositional phrases, conditional clauses, and auxiliary/semi-auxiliary modal constructions (Al-

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
55 

Semeiri,  2018).  Al-Harbi  (2002)  has  sought  to  develop  a  framework  for  epistemic  modality  in 

Modern Standard Arabic (MSA), despite that in MSA there is no modal verb system. Al-Semeiri 

(2018) argues that specific verbs, adverbials, and nominal constructions in MSA parallel English 

modal structures as they indicate possibility, probability, or certainty, thereby serving hedging and 

boosting functions within epistemic contexts. In other words, MSA, much like English, appears to 

depend  extensively  on  epistemic  markers  such  as  modal  or  semi-modal  verbs,  nominal 

constructions, and adverbial phrases to modify propositional force in discourse. 

According to  several  linguists (e.g., Connor, 1996;  Sa’adeddin,  1989;  El-Arousy, 2004; 

and others), Arab writers prefer direct and assertive structures, i.e. use a more frequent number of 

boosting features and use a less frequent number of hedging features. However, English writers 

tend to use tentative structures and hedging features more frequently. For instance, Connor (1996) 

and Sa’adeddin  (1989)  report  a  number of studies that describes  Arabic rhetorical  and stylistic 

structures as amplified and overassertive. According to Holes (2004), Arabic language employs 

diverse grammatical and lexical mechanisms to denote modality, including epistemic distinctions, 

aligning closely with how modal functions are realized in English. However, despite the fact that 

both  Arabic  and  English  tend  to  use  modality  as  an  effective  feature  to  hedge  and  boost  their 

propositions; it seems that the two languages use various different structures to indicate epistemic 

modality. Palmer (2001) argues that “mood and modality are intimately bound” (p. 423) in MSA 

because  modality  can  be  realized  through  inflectional  marking  of  verbs  (i.e.  morphological 

markers). For instance, the prefix sa can be attached to a verb to indicate intention and futurity in 

addition to its hedging or boosting functions. Moreover, when the particle qad precedes a present 

verb,  “it  generally  implies  the  modality  of  doubt,  uncertainty,  [and]  possibility”  (Kinberg  & 

Versteegh, 2001, p. 123). However, while verbs, nouns, and adjectives are regarded as primary 

categories for mitigating or strengthening assertions in Modern Standard Arabic (MSA), standard 

grammatical frameworks of MSA do not formally acknowledge modality as a discrete category 

(El-Hassan, 1990, p. 164). Scholars have nonetheless identified lexical or syntactic constructions 

in MSA including specific verbs, phrases, and grammatical patterns, which function similarly to 

English modal verbs in indicating epistemic or deontic functions (Mitchell & El-Hasan, 1994). 

For instance, Arabic particles such as rubbama, adverbials (prepositional phrases), semi-

verbal particle of la’alla (wish it would/   لعل), particle qad, the modal verb yūmkin (might/نكمي), 

and others can function in their epistemic meanings as hedging or boosting features. The diverse 

 
 
 
 
56 

range of hedging and boosting categories in Modern Standard Arabic (MSA) arises from its lack 

of  a  grammaticalized  modal  system,  contrasting  with  English,  where  modality  is  structured  in 

grammatical forms. Therefore, a variety of lexico-grammatical structures are used to indicate the 

meaning and function of a single English modal auxiliary or other epistemic structures. What is 

important  to  establish  here  is  whether  modality  in  MSA  is  a  lexical  or  grammatical  category. 

According  to  Mejdell  (2006),  MSA  has  less  grammaticalized  structures  of  modal  distinction. 

MSA,  however,  use  a  range  of  lexico-grammatical  particles  and  structural  structures  to  denote 

modality,  functioning  as  a  pragmatic  rather  than  morphosyntactic  feature.  Al-Semeiri  (2018) 

argues  that  modality  in  Arabic  operates  as  a  primarily  semantic  rather  than  grammatically 

structured  category,  with  Arabic  varieties  relying  more  heavily  on  lexical  markers  to  mitigate, 

intensify, or qualify propositions. Conversely, Holes (2004) observes that Arabic modality, like its 

English  counterpart,  is  similar  to  English  in  encompassing  categories  such  as  possibility, 

obligation,  and  ability,  which  are  further  categorized  into  deontic  (obligation/permission)  and 

epistemic  (certainty/probability)  modalities.  Moreover,  Biber  (2004)  and  Biber,  Conrad,  and 

Leech  (2002),  who  explored  modality  in  ENEs  and  ANEs,  classify  modality  into  truth  and 

obligation.  According  to  them,  modals,  which  indicate  necessity  such  as  must  and  should  in 

English, also indicate different levels of certainty in MSA such as the modals yatawajabu (ب  جوتي 

/must/should)  and  yajibu  (بجي/should).  Moreover,  the  morphemes  or  particles  sa  (س/will)  and 

sawfa (will/فوس) indicate prediction or futurity in Arabic as the modals will and would indicate 

prediction and futurity in English.  

Similarly,  Abdalla  (2018)  demonstrates  that  the  verb  yumkin  (may/نكمي)  is  primarily 

employed  to  indicate  qualified  commitment  (non-categorical  assertion)  and  direct  explicitness, 

while qad and rubbama (maybe/ام بر) function to denote partial engagement or indirect claim in 

discourse. That is, they indicate the possibility of biased attitude, which is either intensified takthīr 

(i.e. increased possibility) or downplayed taqlīl (i.e. reduced possibility) depending on the socio-

cultural contexts of their use (p.2003). Similarly, according to El-Hassan (1990, p. 164), the Arabic 

particle rubbama (may be/

  برام ), which consists of two fragments rubba (  بر) + ma (ام) is probably 

the  nearest  equivalent  to  the  English  modal  may  or  might.  However,  its  usage  is  governed  by 

specific syntactic and discourse constraints. Structurally, it may precede verbal clauses (headed by 

a  finite  verb)  or  nominal  clauses  introduced  by  the  complementizer  ann  (  نأ).  The  following 

examples show the use of rubbama:    

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                          
 
 
                        
57 

(a) rubbama yakūn al- mūdīr fi-al-bait (تيبلا يف ريدملا نوكي ا مبر/ the manger maybe is at home). 

(b) rubbama anna al mūdīr fi-al-bait (تيبلا يف ريدملا نوكي ا مبر/ the manger maybe is at home). 

The modal particle qad (دق) can also be equivalent to may or might indicating a particular 

degree of uncertainty or epistemic modality specifically when it precedes a present form of the 

verb, for example:   

(a) qad yakūn al mūdīr fi-al- bait (تيبلا يف ريدملا نوكي دق / the manager may be at home).  

Boosting in  MSA can also be realized by the  modal  particle lā  būdda ‘ann  (  دبلا/wish it 

would) which combines the particle la (لا) for negation and a modal noun būdda followed by an 

embedded sentence (S) usually introduced by the complementizer ann (نأ).This structure is used 

to  indicate  prediction,  uncertainty,  and  expectation  towards  a  particular  action  or  claim  as  the 

following example shows: (a) la būdda annhū sa-yanjaḥ fi-al-imtiḥān (He will pass the exam  دب لا

/ناحتملاا يف حجنيس ه ).  The following table shows some forms of Arabic semi-modal verbs and their
