Table  3.1  shows  some  distinctive  Arabic  semi-modal  verbs  and  their  equivalent 

expressions in English. For instance, modals such as can, could, may and might are similar to the 

modal Arabic equivalent yumkin ‘ann (نأ نكمي /may/might); and they are analogous in meaning and 

function to the these modals in their epistemic meaning of possibility or probability. The semi-

verb or semi- auxiliary structure yumkin (might/may/نكمي) in MSA indicates possibility and ability. 

Similarly, the particle  qad  means  and functions  as  similar as these modals  in  their probability, 

possibility,  and  predictability.  Moreover,  the  Arabic  particles  sawfa  (فوس/will/would)  and  sa 

(س/will/would) when they precede a present verb, they might have the same meaning or function 

as the modal shall, should, will, and would in their epistemic meaning of prediction, probability, 

or  futurity.  It  is  important  to  note  that  not  all  Arabic  expressions  are  verbs.  Some  of  them  are 

particles such as sawfa and qad, which are attached to verbs to modulate a proposition or mitigate 

 
                     
 
 
 
 
 
                                                  
 
                
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
58 

its force act, while others preceding a verb, are categorized as semi-verbs or semi-auxiliary verbs 

like yumkin (might/may/نكمي), lā būdda ‘an (it must/دبلا), yastiṫi’u, (can/عيطتسي), yajibu (must/بجي), 

and  yanbaghi  (should/يغب ني).  In  most  cases,  the  Arabic  semi-modal  verb  is  followed  by  the 

accusative particle ann (نأ). This Arabic particle is always followed by the infinitive form of the 

verb, a structure that is also found in the case of the English grammar:  

modal verb + to + infinitive or modal verb + bare infinitive. 

3.1.6.2 Form and Function of Arabic Hedging and Boosting Features   

One  distinctive  structure  indicating  hedging  is  the  particle  qad  (دق)  followed  by  an 

imperfective present form of the verb, which can denote uncertainty or epistemic modality. The 

particle qad  does not  have a precise meaning by itself, it cannot  stand alone, and it precedes a 

present or past verb. According to Abdalla (2018, p.2004), when qad precedes the past form of the 

verb  (perfective),  qad  meaning  or  function  indicates  taḥqīq  (emphasis/actualization/قيقحت). 

However, when qad precedes the present form of the verb (imperfective); its meaning or function 

indicates  taqrīb  or  taqlīl  (approximation,  reduced  possibility,  and  diminution/بيرقت/ليلقت);  and  it 

functions to reduce the force of the verb. According to Abdalla (2018), Arab writers or speakers 

use qad to lessen the probability of the future threat or to reduce the threat into a warning. In other 

words, Arab writers or speakers tend to use qad to hedge utterances, indicate epistemic uncertainty, 

show  judgment  of  possibility,  reduce  the  possible  threat,  and  proactively  welcome  alternative 

critiques (Badawi, Carter & Gully, 2004; Abdalla, 2018). For instance, the Arabic structure qad 

‘akūn (I might be/نوكأ دق), functioning as a hedge, consists of two segments: Qad (دق) and the present 

verb ‘akūn (نوكأ/ I might be). Mansour (2013) argues that both rubbama (   برام ) and qad can indicate 

a higher degree of possibility or probability than the semi-verb yumkin (نكمي). Exploring the use of 

hedging  and  boosting  in  English  and  Arabic  research  articles,  Al-Otaibi  (2015)  investigated  if 

hedging features function similarly in other languages such as Arabic as they do in English. Al-

Otaibi (2015) provided the following examples:                                       

(a) rubbama naḥnu nantaṭir mūstaqbalan ‘aswa’ (Perhaps, we are awaiting worse future/ رظتنن ام بر

ئوسأ لابقتسم).     

(b) ‘a’taqid ‘annaha ḥaraban laisat ‘adīlah (I believe that this is not a fair war/ ريغ برح اه نأ دقتعأ

ةلداع).            

(c) qad tastāmir al-ḥarb li-mūdat ‘ūsbū’aiīn (The war may last for two weeks/ ةدمل برحلا رمتست دق

نيعوبسأ).   

 
                                                                        
                                                                 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                               
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                       
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
59 

As shown in  examples (a), (b), and (c), inserting the hedging  feature into the sentences 

helps the speaker/writer mitigate his/her claims in the propositions. Al-Otaibi (2015) argues that 

qad  does  not  have  a  similar  equivalent  in  English  and  its  closer  equivalent  is  may  or  might. 

According to Al-Otaibi (2015), while qad was the most frequently used hedging category by the 

Arab  researchers,  the  modal  verb  may  was  the  most  frequently  used  hedging  category  by  the 

English  ones.  Moreover,  according  Al-Otaibi  (2015),  regarding  the  interactional  metadiscourse 

resources, the results showed that English texts were more cautious by using more hedging features 

than  Arabic  ones.  Arabic  discourse  were  more  assertive  by  using  boosting  features.  These 

interactional occurrences were not only less frequent in the Arabic texts but also were less varied. 

For  example,  the  only  hedging  expression  used  in  the  Arabic  texts  was  qad;  while  there  were 

various categories of hedging features used in the English texts (e.g., seem, may, might, could, and 

likely). Al-Otaibi (2015) argues that Arab writers’ reliance on qad as their primary hedging feature 

stems  from  their  conscious  understanding  of  its  function  and  use.  In  MSA, qad is  viewed  as  a 

comprehensive  linguistic  feature,  rendering  other  hedging  elements  unnecessary  or  repetitive. 

Notably, this particle can also amplify the certainty of a proposition, thereby acting as a boosting 

feature.  Al-Otaibi  (2015)  further  emphasizes  that  socio-cultural  norms  inherent  to  a  language 

significantly determine both the form and communicative  function of metadiscourse feature. For 

example,  while  the  Arabic  hedge qad and  the  present  verb  tense  convey  uncertainty,  they 

simultaneously imply stronger authorial commitment compared to the English modal may, which 

denotes  lower  responsibility.  Consequently,  socio-cultural  contexts  play  a  pivotal  role  in 

determining  the  level  of  commitment  conveyed  through  hedging  and  boosting  features  in  both 

languages (Al-Otaibi, 2015).   

Moreover, some features or structures in MSA can indicate a high degree of commitment 

or certainty in a particular context and a low degree of commitment or certainty in other contexts 

(Al-Semeiri,  2018).  Both qad and rubbama,  which  serve  as  counterparts  to  the  English  modal 

may, can express varying levels (low, moderate, or high) of commitment or possibility depending 

on  the  context  or  communicative  purpose.  Similarly, la’lla—whether  expressing  desirable  or 

undesirable possibility may also indicate a spectrum of commitment levels (from strong to weak). 

However, wa kaʾanna (seem as if/   نأكو) only indicates a high degree of certainty or commitment 

(Abdalla, 2018). Moreover, MSA uses sa + the present form of the verb (e.g. sa-yakūn bi-wisī’ina 

(نوكيس انعسوب/we would be able to) to indicate certainty, full commitment, and future possibility. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
60 

Therefore, this structure functions as a boosting feature. In other words, the particle sa (س) when 

it precedes the present form of verb yakūn (نوكي /be); it denotes futurity or prediction similar to 

will construction in English (Al-Semeiri, 2018). In in MSA the particle rubbama (ا مبر /maybe) is 

an  important  hedging  feature,  which  has  been  categorized  by  Arab  linguists  as  a  prepositional 

modal particle of probability. It consists of two fragments: Rubba (a preposition/  بر) and ma (ام), 

and both of these fragments constitute a single particle, which means maybe or perhaps. It is used 

in MSA to mitigate a proposition or avoid full responsibility on the part of the writer/speaker in a 

discourse (Al-Semeiri, 2018). Abdalla (2018) states that rubbama should not necessarily always 

indicate  a  high  possibility  modal.  It  occurs  more  frequently  as  a  reduced  possibility  and  less 

frequently as an increased possibility. In other words, rubbama can indicate a high or low degree 

of commitment or probability depending on the contextual and communicative situations of its 

use. 

In addition to the previous structures used in Arabic discourse, the researcher has observed 

other  hedging  and  boosting  structures  employed  in  Arabic  books  of  linguistics,  grammar,  and 

discourse  analysis.  Other  lexical  or  grammatical  structures  indicating  modality,  hedging,  and 

boosting include epistemic lexical verbs, adverbials or approximators (i.e. prepositional phrases), 

adjectives,  similar-to-verbs  particles,  and  nouns.  The  researcher  categorizes  some  possible 

instances of modality, hedging, and boosting in MSA and their English equivalents. The following 

table clarifies these instances:

As shown in table 2.3, MSA employs the structure of adverbials (prepositional phrases) 

to  hedge  or  to  boost  its propositions.  Adverbials  include  expressions  such  as  min-al-mūḥtamal 

(probably/

لمتحملا

نم 

), min-al-mūmkin (possibly/ 

نكمملا

 نم

), min-al-mūtawāqa’ (expectedly/نم عقوتملا 

), min-al-mūftarḍ (supposedly/نم ضرتفملا ), min-al-wāḍiḥ (clearly/ حضاولا نم) and others which are 

very common structures in MSA and have a similar function to English adverbials. Moreover, like 

English, MSA also employs epistemic lexical verbs to hedge or to boost its propositions. These 

verbs are embedded in a sentence clause usually modifying another sentence clause. These verbs 

include yajūz (could/would/زوجي), ya’taqid (believe/دقتعي), ya’tabir (consider/ربتعي), yaẓinn (think/ 

نظي), yanbaghi (should/must/ غبني), and yabdū, yabdū ka/kaman (seem/seem as if ودبي/كودبي/ودبي), and 

yūāḍiḥ (clarify ح ضوي). The following example shows the use of epistemic lexical verbs in MSA:    

(a) ‘aẓūn ‘annahū lan yā’ti (

يتأي نل

يتأي

 هنأ نظأ

/ I think that he would not come). 

b)  min-al-wāḍiḥ ‘annahū  lan yā’ti  ( يتأي نل ه نأ حضاولا نم/clearly, he will not come). 

Another important hedging structure in MSA is the similar-to-verb particle wa ka’anna or 

wa ka’anna ma (seem/appear/ام نأكو/  نأك), which functions to indicate a high degree of imprecision, 

a  high  degree  of  irresponsibility,  or  al-zānn  (نظلا/believing/guessing)  on  the  part  of  the 

speaker/writer when it is used to hedge a claim. Moreover, la’alla (wish it would/  لعل) is another 

similar-to-verb particle used in MSA to indicate taraji (ي  جرت/wishful uncertainty/wishfulness) or 

weak expectation. According to Abdalla (2018, p.2004), la’alla does not function as similar as the 

particle wa ka’anna but its meaning or function of probability is “permeated with inclination, i.e. 

possibility  that  is  either  desirable  (hoped/loved)  or  undesirable  (feared/hated).”  According  to 

Abdalla (2018, p.2004), these particles are “modal particles of desirable probability and usuality” 

as the following example shows: 

(a) wa ka’annahū mūshikun ala al-sqūṫ (

طوقسلا

 ىلع كشوم

 ه نأكو /It seems that he is about to fall).  

Conditional clauses are also an important structure used in MSA to hedge a proposition. 

Conditional clauses are clauses which consist of one of the following conditional particles: Idha 

(if/اذإ),  law  (if/ول),  lawla  (unless/لاول),  and  inn  (if/نإ).  Another  hedging  structure  in  MSA  is 

rhetorical questions. In MSA, rhetorical questions are questions that start with question words 

such  as  mann  (who/نم),  li-mādha  (why/اذامل),  hal  (do/does/is/له),  ayna  (where/نيأ),  and  others. 

These questions are not posed to be answered but to indicate the writer/speaker’s interrogative and 

tentative opinion. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
               
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
62 

3.2 Previous Research  

3.2.1 Hedging and boosting across Languages, Genres, and Disciplines  

Differences  and  preferences  in  terms  of  use  of  hedging  and  boosting  features  across 

languages  and  cultures  have  been  investigated  by  various  researchers.  Much  research  has 

compared how such rhetorical interpersonal features were used in English and in other languages, 

such as Bulgarian (Vassileva, 2001), Persian (Zarei & Mansoori, 2011), French and Norwegian 

(Vold,  2006),  Spanish  (Martín-Martín,  2008),  Arabic  (Sultan,  2011;  Al-Ghoweri  &  Al-Kayed, 

2019; Abbadi, 2014), German (Kreutz & Harres, 1997), and Chinese (Hu & Cao, 2011). Although 

there  is  much  research  that  investigated  the  use  of  hedging  and  boosting  in  English  and  other 

languages in NEs (e.g., Buitkiene, 2008;  Dafouz-Milne, 2008; Al-Ghoweri & Al-Kayed, 2019; 

Zarza, 2018; Al-Subhi, 2023), this research seems to be limited. The scanty amount of research 

conducted  in  this  area  has  shown  that  there  are  some  noticeable  differences  and  similarities  in 

terms use of such features across languages, genres, and disciplines. 

For  instance,  Zarza  (2018)  investigated  the  pattern  of  rhetorical  moves  and  the  use  of 

hedging and boosting in 120 American ENEs and 120 Malaysian newspaper editorials (MNEs). 

To this end, 240 NEs published in the New York Times (NYT) and New Straits Times (NST) were 

explored in both their macro- and micro-levels. Findings showed that both types of NEs preferred 

the  use  of  hedging  rather  than  boosting  in  their  texts.  Furthermore,  the  findings  showed  that 

hedging in the American ENEs were less frequently used than in their Malaysian counterparts, 

while boosting in the American ENEs were more frequently used than in the MNEs. According to 

Zarza’s (2018) findings, the MNEs were more tentative in presenting their views or claims than 

the  American  ENEs,  who  seemed  to  be  more  assertive  and  certain  in  presenting  their  claims.

Similarly, Al-Ghoweri and Al-Kayed (2019) conducted a comparative analysis of hedging 

and boosting features in English and Jordanian Arabic economic news articles (NEs), aiming to 

identify  cross-linguistic  patterns  in  their  frequency  and  functional  usage.  Drawing  on  Salager-

Meyer’s (1997) and Hyland’s (2005a) frameworks, the researchers compiled a corpus of 60 NEs 

(30 English and 30 Arabic) published between 2016 and 2017 in two Anglo-American and two 

Jordanian  newspapers.  Their  analysis  revealed  that  the  ENEs  employed  hedging  features  (e.g., 

modal verbs, approximators) more frequently, while ANEs tended to use boosting features (e.g., 

emphatics).  These  differences  were  attributed  to  sociocultural  and  linguistic  factors  shaping 

communicative norms. Specifically,  modal verbs  were a very  frequent  hedging category in the 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
63 

ENEs, whereas lexical verbs and approximators were very frequently used in the ANEs. Similarly, 

amplifiers were  a frequent  category of boosting in  the ENEs, while  emphatics  prevailed in  the 

ANEs.  

Dheskali (2017) explored the use of hedging and boosting in Albanian, British, and Italian 

online news articles in Europe (NEs). The aim of this comparative research was to investigate the 

usage of hedging and boosting in Albanian, British and Italian online news articles on the refugee 

crisis. The researcher compared categories and subcategories of author-related and proposition-

related  hedging  (e.g.,  believe,  possibly,  approximately)  and  boosters  (e.g.  show,  certainly, 

completely)  as  well  as  relevant  interrelated  aspects  such  as  their  positioning,  orientation, 

manifestation, and prosody of moralization. The research was based on compatible small corpora 

of  Albanian,  British  and  Italian  online  articles  from  well-known  media  sources  that  included 

genres such as news reports and NEs. The results showed that hedging and boosting have similar 

frequencies in the British and Albanian corpora, but hedging was more frequent than boosting in 

the  Italian  corpus.  The  researcher  concluded  that  journalists  from  Britain  and  Albania  struck  a 

balance  between  outright  affirmation  and  cautious  or  partial  endorsement  of  the  claim,  while 

Italian journalists adopted a more cautious and hesitant tone in their texts.   

Furthermore,  Al-Subhi  (2023)  explored  the  use  of  interactional  metadiscourse  and 

phraseology in NEs during the Russia-Ukraine war in a comparative analysis of the language of 

NEs that were published during the war. The research was based on samples taken from leading 

newspapers in the USA and the UAE. The corpus consists of 24 NEs amounting to 16,673 words 

written  in  English.  Hyland’s  (2005b)  interactional  model  of  metadiscourse  was  used  as  an 

analytical framework. Data analysis revealed significant similarities between editorials from the 

USA and the UAE regarding the use of multiple linguistic features. Additionally, stance markers 

were found to substantially outnumber engagement markers in both sets of texts. The researcher 

further highlighted diverse usage of collocations, modal language, and idiomatic expressions. It 

concluded  that  NEs  function  as  argumentative  discourse,  characterized  by  persuasive  elements 

such as hedging, boosting, attitude markers, and similar rhetorical features. 

Sanjaya  (2013)  analyzed  a  group  of  English  and  Indonesian  research  articles  in  two 

disciplines (i.e. applied linguistics and chemistry) in order to explore the frequency of usage of 

hedging and boosting. To this purpose, a corpus of 104 research articles from the two languages 

and disciplines were investigated quantitatively with the help of corpus-linguistic methods. The 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
64 

findings of the research revealed that the overall English research articles were more tentative than 

the Indonesian articles as the English research articles used a more frequent number of hedging, 

and the Indonesian research articles used a more frequent number of boosting. 

Yazdania,  Sharifib,  and  Elyassic  (2014)  studied  the  use  of  hedging  and  boosting  in  11 

September English Front Page News Articles (i.e. NEs). The research aimed to find the function 

of  interpersonal  metadiscourse  markers  such  as  hedging  and  boosting  in  these  articles. 

Accordingly, 27 front pages of widely read newspapers in the US were selected randomly from 

around 50 news about 9/11 events. Quantitative and qualitative methods were applied to answer 

the research questions and to identify the use of hedging and boosting features in the corpus. The 

findings showed that hedging was the most frequently used metadiscourse marker in English front 

page  news  articles.  Probability  adverbs  were  the  most  frequently  used  hedging  subcategory, 

whereas verbs were the most frequent subcategory of boosting used to describe writers’ certainty. 

The researchers concluded that journalists preferred to be conservative by using indirect strategies 

(i.e. hedging features) like adverbs and adjective on controversial issues like 9/11 events.                                                          

3.3 Summary  

This chapter provided a theoretical background for the concepts of hedging and boosting, 

their evolution,  their  emergence,  their forms,  and functions. The present  chapter also  reviewed 

some  important  approaches  and  perspectives  that  tackled  hedging  and  boosting  features. 

Moreover, the present chapter gave a brief account of the concept of genre defining how generic 

approaches  dealt  with  hedging  and  boosting  and  different  categories  of  texts.  In  addition,  this 

chapter  presented  some  perspectives  and  approaches  of  the  two  features  in  MSA.  Finally,  it 

provided some empirical previous research, and how it investigated hedging and boosting across 

languages,  discourses,  and  genres.  Next  chapter  presents  the  data  analysis,  the  descriptive  and 

inferential statistical findings, and the discussion of findings of the present research.   

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
65 

Chapter Four   

Data Analysis and Findings    

4.1 Introduction  

This  chapter  presents  the  findings  of  the  quantitative  and  qualitative  analysis,  and  a 

discussion related to each research question is provided in details. The first section of the chapter 

presents the descriptive findings in relation to the frequency of hedging and boosting features in 

the English and Arabic corpora. Afterwards, a qualitative functional analysis and interpretations 

of functions of such features in the English corpus as compared to the Arabic one is presented. 

Following this, referential findings drawn from the previous descriptive findings are  given. The 

chapter concludes with a discussion of how these quantitative and qualitative analyses relate to the 

theoretical framework and the research questions that were posed in chapter one.    

4.2 Data Analysis 

 As mentioned earlier in the introduction, the main objective of the present research was to 

determine whether the socio-cultural context (language) where the current NEs situated affected 

the frequency, form, and function of hedging and boosting in these NEs. To achieve this objective, 

two sets of NEs written in two different languages (Modern Standard English (MSE) & Modern 

Standard Arabic (MSA)) were compared in terms of their use of hedging and boosting features. In 

the  present  research,  there  was  one  independent  variable  and  one  dependent  variable.  The 

independent variable was the linguistic or socio-cultural context where the NEs were written. This 

independent  variable  was  supposed  to  affect  the  dependent  variable,  which  was  the  form, 

frequency, function of hedging and boosting features in the two sets of corpora. Table 4.1 shows 

the size of the two entire corpora: 

Table 4.1 Distribution of Length of the NEs in words by Language

As previously mentioned, the whole two corpora (English and Arabic), comprising 40 NEs, 

20 ANEs and 40 ENEs, amounted to 29,744 words. The English corpus contained 15,692 words, 

whereas the Arabic corpus contained 14,052 words. As shown in table 4.1 above, the mean length 

(ML)  and  standard  deviation  (SD)  of  the  lengths  of  both  the  ANEs  and  ENEs  in  the  two  sub-

corpora in  the present  research  were as follows:  The  ENEs (ML  = 784.6 words, Max. = 1,360 

words, Min. = 498 words, SD = 272.67 words) and the ANEs (ML = 702.6 words, Max. = 1,045 

words, Min. = 406 words, SD = 182.62 words). Thus, on average the ENEs were longer than the 

ANEs. Moreover, it is noteworthy that both the ANErs and ENErs deviated from the mean length 

(i.e. 272.67 words in the ENEs and 182.62 in the ANEs). The total standard deviation of lengths 

of both the ENEs and ANEs was 233.13 words. In fact, ENErs wrote longer articles compared to 

their Arab counterparts.      

4.3 Descriptive Findings  

To  reiterate,  the  analysis  of  the  selected  NEs  was  based  on  Fraser’s  (2010,  pp.23-24) 

analytical model and Hyland’s (1996) definition of hedging and Hyland’s (2005a) definition of 

boosting.  Therefore,  occurrences  of  hedging  and  boosting  in  both  corpora  were  classified  and 

coded within these two models. In addition to these identified categories, other categories; which 

did  not  exist  in  Fraser’s  classification;  were  found  and  incorporated  to  hedging  and  boosting 

categories in both the Arabic and English corpora. By presenting table 4.2 and 4.3, the researcher 

attempts to answer the research questions: What is the frequency of occurrence of hedging and 

boosting  in  the  ENEs  (English  corpus)?  And  what  is  the  overall  frequency  of  occurrence  of 

hedging  and  boosting  in  the  ANEs  (Arabic  corpus)?  The  descriptive  statistics  of  usage  of  the 

hedging and boosting features in the two corpora illustrated in table 4.2 and 4.3:

As table 4.2 shows, the ENErs used totally 768 hedging features, while the ANErs used 

479 hedging features. On average, the ENErs used 48.94 tokens of hedging PTW, whereas the 

ANErs used 34.08 tokens of the feature PTW. Apparently, these findings demonstrate that hedging 

were used more frequently in the ENEs compared to the ANEs. These findings are similar to Al-

Ghoweri  and  Al-Kayed’s  (2019)  findings  of  the  use  of  hedging  in  ENEs  and  ANEs.  These 

researchers  found  that  the  ENEs  were  more  heavily  hedged  than  the  Arabic  ones.  In  fact,  the 

ENErs, in  the present  research, hedged their propositions approximately  twice more often than 

their  Arab  counterparts.  The  overall  ENEs  and  ANEs  contained  1247  instances  of  hedging 

occurring in 41.92 tokens PTW in the whole two corpora. Moreover, the most frequent category 

of hedging in the English corpus was modal verbs recording 4.39 tokens PTW, whereas the most 

frequent category in the Arabic corpus was epistemic lexical verbs recording 6.12 tokens PTW. 

Similarly, the least category of hedging used in the English corpus was  modal nouns recording 

0.25  tokens  PTW,  while  the  least  frequent  category  in  the  Arabic  corpus  was  metalinguistic 

comments recording 0.14 tokens PTW. Table 4.3 illustrates that the ENErs used totally 166 tokens 

of  boosting.  On  average,  the  ENEs  contained  10.57  tokens  of  the  feature  PTW.  However,  the 

ANErs used 182 tokens of boosting, i.e. ANEs contained 12.95 tokens of the feature PTW. In other 

words,  the  ANErs  boosted  their  propositions  more  often  than  their  English  counterparts  did. 

Moreover, the most frequent category of boosting in the English corpus was modal verbs recording 

4.39  tokens  PTW,  whereas  the  most  frequent  category  of  boosting  in  the  Arabic  corpus  was 

adverbs of degree, time, and frequency recording 4.34 tokens PTW. Similarly, the least category 

of boosting used in the English corpus was metalinguistic comments recording 0.19 tokens PTW, 

while  the  least  frequent  category  in  the  Arabic  corpus  was  indefinite/impersonal  pronouns 

recording 0.49 tokens PTW.   The  overall  ENEs  and  ANEs  contained  351  tokens  of  boosting 

recording 11.80 tokens PTW in the whole two corpora.

Similarly, as table 4.4 shows, on average, every ENE contained 38.4 hedges per NE, and on 

average, every ANE contained 23.85 hedges per NE. Moreover, one ENE consisted of 69 hedges 

maximally,  while  one  ANE  contained  maximally  45  hedges.  On  the  other  hand,  one  ENE 

contained minimally 16 hedges, and one ANE contained minimally only 10 hedges. Similarly, as 

table 4.4 showed, on average, every ENE contained 8.3 boosters per NE, and on average, every 

ANE  contained  9.1  boosters  per  NE.  Moreover,  one  ENE  consisted  of  16  boosters  maximally, 

while  one  ANE  contained  maximally  23  boosters.  On  the  other  hand,  one  ENE  contained 

minimally 2 boosters per NE, and one ANE contained minimally 3 boosters per NE. It is clear 

from the minimum values that all the NEs in the two corpora contained hedging and boosting. 

Moreover, the difference in the two mean frequencies of hedging (48.94 PTW for ENEs & 33.94 

PTW for ANEs) found in the two corpora under research can be explained in terms of the differing 

lengths of NEs included in the two corpora. It is reasonable to argue that the longer the text the 

greater the chances that it contains such features at a greater frequency rate. Obviously, it seems 

that the ENErs used more hedging features in their texts. This may suggest that compared to their 

Arabic colleagues, the ENErs were much more cautious and tentative in presenting their claims or 

journalistic information as they hedged their claims more frequently. However, the ANErs boosted 

their claims or propositions more frequently than the ENErs (10.57 PTW for ENEs & 12.95 PTW 

for ANEs) despite the fact that the ENEs were longer than the ANEs. This is clear evidence that 

the length of text is not the only factor that affect the use of two features.

the overall frequency of occurrence of hedging and boosting in the ENEs (English corpus)? And 

what are the linguistic forms or categories of hedging and boosting used in the English and Arabic 

corpus?  The analysis of the English corpus shows that the ENErs used 19 categories of hedging 

to mitigate their propositions. Table 4.5 presents the overall raw frequency and the mean frequency

Modal Adverbs

4.3.1.1 Modal Verbs   

As table 4.5 and  figure  4.1 illustrate, modal verbs, which are associated with  epistemic 

meanings and possibility, were the most frequent category of hedging in the English corpus. These 

verbs occurred in 149 instances and recorded 9.50 tokens PTW in the overall English corpus. This 

finding  matches  other  findings  where  modal  verbs  were  the  most  prototypical  realization  of 

hedging and the most occurring category (e.g., Tahririan & Shahzamani, 2009; Al-Ghoweri & Al-

Kayed, 2019). For instance, Tahririan and Shahzamani (2009), who studied the use of hedging 

features in ENEs and Persian newspaper editorials (PNEs), found that modal verbs occurred in 

292 instances in the whole English corpus and in 121 instances in the Persian corpus. Modal verbs 

are generally used to indicate various degrees of the writer/speaker’s certainty, epistemic meaning, 

possibility, tentativeness, and probability. In the English corpus, 8 subcategories of modal verbs 

occurring at different rates were observed as table 4.5 showed. As shown in table 4.5, the most 

frequently used modal verbs in the English corpus were would, could, can, may should, and need 

to occurring respectively as follows:   

4.3.1.1.1 Would                                                                                          

Hardjanto (2016) argues that would can be used as a hypothetical expression to indicate a 

higher degree of tentativeness. In the English corpus as shown in figure 4.1, would was the most 

 
          
             
 
 
                    
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
        
 
 
 
 
  
 
   
 
              
 
 
 
             
 
             
 
 
    
 
71 

frequently used modal verbs occurring in 43 instances and recording 2.74 tokens PTW in the whole 

English corpus. This finding supports the findings of Sedaghat et al. (2015) of the use of hedging 

in  the ENEs and PNEs. Sedaghat  et  al.,  (2015) found that would was the most frequent  modal 

verbs in the English corpus occurring in 114 instances and accounting for 38.04% out of the total 

number of hedging in the English corpus. The following example is extracted from the English 

corpus shows the use of would: 

(1) Spain’s prime minister, Pedro Sánchez, would be doing the right thing…  

4.3.1.1.2 Could  

The second most frequently used modal verb in the English corpus was could. Hardjanto 

(2016)  explains  that  could  is  used  to  indicate  epistemic  or  tentative  possibility.  In  the  English 

corpus, could occurred in 39 instances and recorded 2.49 tokens PTW in the whole corpus. The 

following example shows the use of could in the English corpus:  

(2) But the White House could easily do far more to intervene to stop the slaughter. 

4.3.1.1.3 Can     

Can  indicate  various  degrees  of  future  possibility,  probability,  likelihood,  tentative 

assumption, or assessment. Both can and could can indicate possibility in the future, but the use of 

can denotes slightly a more definite possibility. The modal can ranked third among English modal 

verbs occurring in 24 instances and recording 1.53 tokens PTW in the entire corpus. However, 

Rabab’ah (2013) found that can recorded the highest frequency (371 instances) among modal verbs 

in English education and nursing articles. The following example taken from the corpus shows the 

use of can:  

(3) Unless some way can be found to capture the emissions from power plants…                        

4.3.1.1.4 May   

The  fourth  most  frequent  modal  verb  used  in  the  English  corpus  was  may.  May  in  its 

epistemic function can be used to indicate future possibility and probability. This modal occurred 

in 17 instances in the English corpus and recorded 1.08 tokens PTW in the corpus. However, this 

finding does not support Sanjaya’s (2013) findings of use of hedging in English and Indonesian 

research  articles.  Sanjaya  found  that  may  was  the  most  frequently  used  modal  in  the  English 

corpus. The following example from the corpus shows the use of may:  

(4) It may well be that the Israeli military has advised the cabinet accordingly. 

4.3.1.1.5 Should   

 
                                                                                                                                 
                       
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                                                          
               
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                   
 
 
 
 
 
                                                 
                    
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                          
 
                                                                                                                                  
                         
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
            
72 

The modal verb should in its epistemic or hedging use can indicate speculation and reduce 

the strength of the claim. Should ranked fifth among modal verbs in the English corpus occurring 

in 11 instances and recorded 0.70 tokens PTW in the whole English corpus. The following example 

taken from the corpus shows the use of should: 

(5) Afghanistan’s future: withdrawal should not mean abandonment.  

4.3.1.1.6 Other Modal Verbs  

The third least frequently used modal verbs identified in the English corpus were need to, 

might,  and  ought  to.  Might  is  usually  used  to  indicate  probability  and  epistemic  meaning.  It 

occurred in 5 instances and recorded 0.32 tokens PTW in the entire English corpus. Need to and 

ought to occurred very rarely in their epistemic or hedging functions. They occurred in the English 

corpus as follows: Need to (RF =5, MF = 0.10) and ought to (RF=7, MF = 0.14). The following 

example illustrates the use of the modal verb ought to in the English corpus: 

(6) That ought to worry Europeans…                                                  

4.3.1.2 Agentless Passives      

Agentless passives are used by speakers or writers to mitigate a proposition and avoid any 

categorical commitment to a claim. They ranked second among hedging categories in the English 

corpus occurring in 106 instances and recording 6.76 tokens PTW in the overall English corpus. 

This category can indicate an indirect reporting of speech acts (Fraser, 2010). The use of agentless 

passives in the English corpus is illustrated in the following example:  

(7) Hundreds of Palestinians were wounded while more than 20 Israeli police officers suffered injuries. 

4.3.1.3 Adverbs of Frequency, Degree, Time, or Quantity            

The third most frequent category in the English corpus was adverbs of frequency, degree, 

or time. This category consists of all adverbs that indicate an approximate degree of verbal actions 

to  avoid  providing  exact  figures  or  entities.  Salager-Meyer  (1994)  labels  this  category  as 

approximators since it can replace exact numbers and give approximate information to the data. 

Salager-Meyer (2011) argues that approximators are used when we are not sure about the exactness 

of certain figures or when the speaker does not have enough knowledge in order to be precise and 

accurate. As table 4.5 and figure 4.1, these adverbs occurred in 88 instances and recorded 5.61 

tokens PTW in the entire English corpus. This supports the findings of Rabab’ah and Abu Rumman 

(2015), who found that such adverbs were the second most frequently used hedging category in 

the speech of King of Jordan, King Abdullah in English. Table 4.6 showed the most frequently

One of the most heavily used adverb was almost occurring in 13 instances in the entire 

English corpus and recording 0.82 tokens PTW. The adverb at least ranked second occurring in 

11 instances and recording 0.70 tokens PTW. About ranked in the third position occurring in 10 

instances and recording 0.63 tokens PTW in the whole English corpus. The adverb often ranked 

fourth occurring in 9 instances and recording 0.57tokens PTW. Similarly, Rabab’ah (2013) found 

that often occurred in 92 instances recording the highest frequency among adverbs of frequency in 

English  nursing  and  education  academic  articles.  As  table  4.6  showed,  other  more  adverbs 

occurred at approximately comparable rates as follows: Roughly (RF = 8, MF = 0.51), always (RF 

= 6, MF = 0.38), and rather (RF =5, MF = 0.31). Less frequently used adverbs were sometimes 

(RF = 3, MF = 0.19), usually (RF = 3, MF = 0.19), and mostly (RF = 2, MF = 0.12). In addition, 

there was a subcategory of adverbs labeled as other adverbs containing various subcategories of 

adverbs such as particularly, quite, partly, barely, generally and others. This subcategory occurred 

in 18 instances and recording 1.14 tokens PTW in the English corpus. The following example from 

the English corpus shows the use of these adverbs:                                                                                                                                 

(10) The ink on the coalition agreement was barely dry when Mr. Netanyahu showed that he intends to go 

down fighting.

The  fourth  most  frequent  hedging  category  in  the  English  corpus  was  quantifiers  or 

determiners occurring in 86 instances and recording 5.48 tokens PTW in the English corpus. In

the use of these quantifiers: 

(11) Most peace initiatives run into the sand… 

4.3.1.5 Reference Structures   

The  fifth  most  frequently  used  hedging  category  in  the  English  corpus  was  reference 

structures. This category occurred in 70 instances and recorded 4.46 tokens PTW in the whole 

English corpus. This finding supports Villanueva’s (2014) findings of the use of hedging in English 

and Philippine NEs. Villanueva (2014) found that reference structures accounted for 50 % out of 

the total number of hedging features used in the ENEs and Pilipino NEs. In the present research, 

most occurrences of English reference structures were in the form of verbs reporting or referencing 

an official figure, person, experts, or sources. Several other instances of reference structures were 

 
                                                                                                                                              
                                                                                                                                  
 
                                                                                                                       
                          
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
                            
 
75 

introduced by the phrase according to, which was very frequent in the English corpus as shown in 

the following example: 

(12) Last month, according to the Wall Street Journal, Mr. Biden decided against slowing the withdrawal 

from the main U.S. air base in the country.  

4.3.1.6 Epistemic Lexical Verbs    

The sixth most frequently used English category of hedging was epistemic lexical verbs. 

Salager-Meyer  (2011)  demonstrates  that  epistemic  lexical  verbs  are  speech  act  verbs  used  to 

perform  acts  such  as  doubting  and  evaluating.  They  are  tentative  strategies  used  by  the 

writer/speaker to report the other researchers’ studies or represent tentative reports of his/her own 

work (Varttala, 2001). They indicate that speakers or writers’ state of knowledge is not absolute, 

but it is based on assessments or beliefs. These verbs occurred in 65 instances and recorded 4.14 

tokens PTW in the whole English corpus. This does not support Rabab’ah’s (2013) findings, who 

found that epistemic lexical verbs recorded the highest mean scores (24.06 %) in English education 

and nursing articles. In the English corpus, 10 major subcategories of epistemic lexical verbs were 

identified as the following table shows:

MF = 0.38), and suppose (RF =4, MF = 0.25). However, epistemic lexical verbs such as believe,

use of these verbs in the English corpus:    

(13) But the winds seem favorable, at least for some type of revived agreement.                                                                                           

4.3.1.7 Conditional Clauses       

The use of conditional clauses in their epistemic or hedging meaning usually might denote 

conditional  probability  of  a  particular  action  in  the  future  or  a  hypothetical  conditionality  in  a 

proposition. Similarly, only conditional clauses indicating future conditionality could function as 

hedging features. According to Zarza (2018), NErs use if clauses to show a lack of definiteness of 

future prediction. According to Jabbar (2019), conditional clauses might indicate a hypothetical 

situation and denote the writer’s epistemic stance. Conditional clauses were a frequent category in 

the English corpus occurring in 41 instances and recording 2.61 tokens PTW in the English corpus. 

The following example shows the use of conditional clauses in the English corpus: 

(14) The Israelis, in particular, will respond to political pressure if it is exerted with sufficient skill and 

firmness. 

4.3.1.8 Concessive Conjunctions   

Concessive conjunctions  ranked eighth  among hedging categories in  the English corpus 

occurring in 28 instances and recording 1.78 tokens PTW in the entire English corpus. Concessive 

conjunctions are words or phrases that indicate that the speaker/writer presents his/her opinions 

tentatively  and  cohesively.  Although  and  though/even  though  were  the  most  frequently  used 

concessive conjunctions in the English corpus occurring as follows: Although (RF = 14, MF = 

0.89) and though/even though (RF = 10, MF = 0.63). Despite of/ despite that, nevertheless, and 

albeit  were  less  frequently  used  occurring  in  4  instances  and  recording  0.25  tokens  PTW.  The 

following example shows the use of concessive conjunctions in the English corpus: 

15) Despite evidence that Iran was complying with its obligations, Trump withdrew from the agreement…                                                                                                                                             

4.3.1.9 Modal Adverbs   

 Modal adverbs functioning as hedging features ranked ninth occurring in 20 instances and 

recording  1.27  tokens  PTW  in  the  whole  English  corpus.  Nine  major  subcategories  of  modal 

adverbs were observed in the English corpus as table 4.9 showed:

Table  4.9  shows  that  adverbs  such  as  likely/unlikely  and  maybe/perhaps  were  the  most 

frequently used modal adverbs in the English corpus occurring as follows: Likely/unlikely (RF =5, 

MF =0.31), and maybe/perhaps (RF = 4, MF = 0.25). Modal adverbs such as probably and possibly 

ranked second occurring as follows: Probably (RF=3, MF = 0.19), possibly (RF = 3, MF = 0.19). 

Similarly, Rabab’ah (2013) found that probably and possibly occurred in 292 instances recording 

the highest frequency among modal adverbs in English nursing and education academic articles. 

However, adverbs such as ostensibly/apparently, unexpectedly/expectedly, and supposedly were 

the least frequent modal adverbs occurring as follows: Ostensibly/apparently (RF =2, MF = 0.12), 

unexpectedly /expectedly (RF = 2, MF = 0.12), and supposedly (RF =1, MF = 0.06). The following 

example from the English corpus illustrates the use of such adverbs: 

(16)  Mr.  Johnson  to  make  the  Northern  Ireland  protocol  work  and  is  likely  to  repeat  that  message  in 

Cornwall. 

4.3.1.10 Rhetorical Questions  

Rhetorical  questions  as  hedging  features  typically  function  to  establish  a  more  dialogic 

interaction between the writer and reader in newspaper discourse, thus the writer’s arguments or 

views approved by his/her reader (Khabbazi-Oskouei, 2012). The writer/speaker does not seek an 

answer  for  his/her  rhetorical  question,  but  rather  he/she  actually  tries  to  present  his/her 

views/claims  tentatively  and  indirectly.  Rhetorical  questions  were  less  frequently  used  in  the 

English corpus than other previous categories occurring in 19 instances and recording 0.21 tokens 

PTW in the whole English corpus. The following example shows the use of rhetorical questions 

in the English corpus:                                         

(17) Why Cuba’s youth are revolting now against the island’s dictators?                                        

4.3.1.11 Indefinite /Impersonal Pronouns   

One less frequently used hedging feature in the English corpus was indefinite/impersonal

pronouns occurring in 18 instances and recording 1.15 tokens PTW in the whole corpus. As shown 

in  table  4.10,  8  major  subcategories  of  indefinite/impersonal  pronouns  were  observed  in  the 

English corpus:

following example from the English corpus illustrates the use of indefinite/impersonal pronouns:            

(17) Mr. Trump has long sought — a grander bargain than President Barack Obama got, one that would 

address... 

4.3.1.12 Adjectives     

Some  adjectives  are  used  in  English  to  indicate  the  writer/speaker’s  uncertainty  or 

tentativeness.  Hedging  adjectives  were  less  frequently  used  features  in  the  English  corpus 

occurring  in  15  instances  and  recording  0.96  tokens  PTW  in  the  whole  corpus.  In  the  English 

corpus, 4 subcategories of hedging adjectives were observed as shown in table 4.12:     

Table 4.11 Frequency of Adjectives in the English Corpus

As  table  4.11  shows,  uncertain/particular  were  the  most  frequently  used  adjectives

occurring in 5 instances and recording 0.31 tokens PTW. However, obvious ranked second among 

hedging adjectives occurring in 4 instances and recording 0.25 tokens PTW. Adjectives such as 

approximate/pending/incipient  were  less  frequently  used  occurring  as  follows:  Approximate/ 

pending/incipient  (RF=3,  MF=0.19).  Adjectives,  labeled  as  other  adjectives,  occurred  in  3 

instances and recorded 0.19 tokens PTW. Example (18) from the English corpus shows the use of 

such adjectives:       

(18) The obvious explanation is that this is a recovery from an unprecedented recession… 

4.3.1.13 Metalinguistic Comments    

Metalinguistic  comments  are  words,  phrases,  or  interjections,  irrelevant  to  the  main 

proposition, used by the writer/speaker to hedge his/her views or claims in a discourse. In fact, this 

category is used to guide the listener/reader to better interpret the views presented (Fraser, 2010). 

Metalinguistic comments occurred in 12 instances recording 0.76 tokens PTW in the whole English 

corpus. Hamood (2015) found that metalinguistic comments were the most frequent category in 

WhatsApp  group  discussions  between  Arab  and  Malaysian  English  learners.  Metalinguistic 

comments such as by the way, to be fair, if at all, in any case, in one sense, and most importantly 

were very frequent in the English corpus. The following example shows the use of metalinguistic 

comments in the English corpus: 

(19)  By  the  way,  the  Biden  administration  has  no  interest  right  now  in  being  forced  to  react  to  those 

headlines.   

4.3.1.11 Other Categories of Hedging in English Corpus: 

4.3.1.11.1 Nouns         

Nouns functioning as hedging features were less frequently used occurring in 12 instances 

and recording 1.76 tokens PTW in the whole English corpus. Assumption occurred in 5 instances 

and recorded 0.31 tokens PTW, and claim occurred in 4 instances and recorded 0.25 tokens PTW. 

Indication, uncertainty, and assessment were less frequently used, each one of them, occurring in 

1 instance and recording 0.06 tokens PTW. The following example clarifies the use of such nouns 

as hedging features in the English corpus:      

(20) But they do show that the assumption everything has already failed is exaggerated.                     

4.3.1.11.2 Conditional Subordinators   

Conditional subordinators are words such as as long as, so long as, assuming that, given 

that, and others, which connect two sentences and indicate that one proposition is conditioned by 

 
                                                                                                                         
               
 
 
 
 
                
 
                                                                                                                                                             
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                                   
 
 
                                                 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
               
 
 
 
 
 
 
            
 
80 

another to avoid any absolute responsibility. Conditional subordinators occurred in 10 instances 

recoding 0.64 tokens PTW in the whole English corpus. Among conditional subordinators, as long 

as recorded the highest frequency occurring in 5 instances and recording 0.31 tokens PTW. The 

following example from the English corpus shows the use of conditional subordinators: 

(21) But as long as Israeli politics remains on a knife-edge, the opportunity will be hard to seize.                               

4.3.1.11.3 Introductory Phrases   

Introductory phrases are adjective, noun, or verb phrases positioned at the beginning of a 

sentence to modify or mitigate another sentence proposition. According to Salager-Meyer (2011), 

introductory phrases, such as we know, we believe, to our knowledge, and others can indicate “the 

authors’ personal doubt and direct involvement” (p.7). According to Rabab’ah and Abu Rumman 

(2015), introductory phrases are used by the speaker/writer to indicate that his/her statements are 

his/her  personal  points  of  view  rather  than  facts.  Introductory  phrases  can  indicate  that  the 

writer/speaker wants to engage the reader/listener in his/her views and thus show awareness to the 

reader/listener’s  opinions  (Rabab’ah  &  Abu  Rumman,  2015).  Introductory  phrases  were  less 

frequently  used  occurring  in  9  instances  and  recording  0.57  tokens  PTW  in  the  whole  English 

corpus. The following example illustrates the use of  introductory phrases in the English corpus:               

(22)  There's  some  talk  in  the  Pentagon  of  seeking  authorization  to  launch  airstrikes  from  outside 

Afghanistan…                           

4.3.1.11.4 Compound Hedging 

Combining  two  or  more  hedging  features,  known  as  compound  hedging,  is  common  in 

English language. Compound hedging was a less frequently used category than other categories 

corpus  occurring  in  8  instances  and  recording  0.50  tokens  PTW  in  the  entire  English  corpus. 

Double hedging and treble hedging were frequently used in the English corpus. Double hedging 

occurring in 5 instances and recording 0.31 tokens PTW. However, treble hedging was rarely used 

occurring in only 3 instances in the entire English corpus and recording 0.19 tokens PTW. The 

following example from the English corpus illustrates the use of compound hedging:      

(23) An agreement to revive the nuclear deal with Iran appears possible as the United States… 

4.3.1.11.5 Modal Adjectives and Modal Nouns   

Modal adjectives (e.g., possible, impossible, plausible, potential, predictable, apparent…) 

are hedging features used to mitigate a proposition and indicate that the information presented is 

possible  or  probable  rather  than  absolute.  Varttala  (2001)  argues  that  modal  adjectives  are 

 
                                                                                                                                             
 
 
                 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
                                 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
         
              
 
 
 
 
 
 
81 

adjectives  that  reduce  the  strength  of  the  claim  and  are  used  to  indicate  the  writer/speaker’s 

possibility, probability, or uncertainty of a claim. Modal adjective were very rarely used occurring 

in 8 instances and recording 0.50 tokens PTW in the whole English corpus. This finding supports 

Khoshsima, Talati-Baghsiahi, and Moafian’s  (2016) findings, who  found that modal adjectives 

were the least frequent hedging category in all three corpora of linguistics research articles (i.e. 

Anglo-American writers, Iranian writers in English, and Iranian writers in Persian). The following 

example shows the use of modal adjectives as hedging features in the corpus: 

(24) The evictions have been described by a UN rights body as a possible war crime.                     

The least frequently used hedging category in the English corpus was modal nouns.  Modal 

nouns  are  nouns  that  indicate  a  probable  or  possible  state  of  the  proposition  presented  (e.g., 

possibility,  impossibility,  likelihood,  unlikelihood,  predictability,  unpredictability,  expectation). 

Modal nouns occurred in only 4 instances and recorded 0.21 tokens PTW in the entire English 

corpus. The following example clarifies the use of these nouns in the English corpus:     

(25) Mr. Trump  raised just  that  possibility last  month,  at  a signing  ceremony  for  yet  another  round  of 

sanctions against Iran’s leaders.    

4.3.2 Frequency and Categories of Boosting in the English Corpus    

By providing table 4.12, the researcher attempts to answer research questions: What is the 

frequency of occurrence of hedging and boosting in the ENEs (English corpus)? Moreover, what 

are  the  linguistic  forms  or  categories  of  hedging  and  boosting  used  in  the  English  and  Arabic 

corpus? The analysis of the English corpus shows that the ENErs used 10 categories of boosting 

to boost their propositions or claims. Table 4.12 presents the raw and mean frequency PTW of 

each category of boosting in the entire English corpus:

Modal  verbs  were  the  first  most  frequent  category  of  boosting  in  the  English  corpus 

occurring in 69 instances and recording 4.40 tokens of boosting PTW in the whole corpus. Modal 

verbs such as will, must, shall, and cannot function to indicate necessity, certainty, or obligation. 

According to Palmer (1990), they assert the speaker's confidence in the truth of a proposition and 

often indicate authority or conviction. Speakers or writers use modal verbs as boosting features to 

assert their authority or expertise (Hyland, 1998a). Will was a very frequently used boosting feature 

occurring in  41 instances  and recording 2.61 tokens PTW in  the English corpus. Must  was the 

second most frequently used boosting feature. It occurred in 18 instances and recorded 1.15 tokens 

PTW.  Cannot, have to, and should not were less frequently used boosting features. They occurred 

as follows: Cannot (RF=5, MF=0.32), have to (RF=3, MF=0.19), and should not (RF= 2, MF= 

0.13).  The  following  example  from  the  English  corpus  illustrates  the  use  of  modal  verbs  as  a 

boosting feature: 

(26) It will take time for the gentle diplomatic proddings from the United Nations…to be taken seriously.                  

4.3.2.2 Adverbs of Degree, Time, and Frequency   

Adverbs of degree, time, and frequency were the second most frequently occurring boosting 

category in the English corpus. They occurred in 49 instances and recorded 3.12 tokens PTW in 

the  whole  corpus.  These  averbs  amplify  the  force  of  a  statement.  Hyland  (2005b)  labels  them 

intensifiers. According to him, they increase the strength of a claim, making it more emphatic and 

 
                  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
           
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                                                                                                                  
 
 
 
 
 
83 

convincing. As shown in Table 4.13, 6 subcategories of these adverbs were observed in the corpus:

Table 4.13 shows that adverbs such as indeed /at all/of course functioning as boosting features 

were frequently used occurring in 16 instances and recording 1.02 tokens PTW. Other adverbs 

were less frequently used occurring as follows: Surely/certainly (RF=10, MF=0.64), really/exactly 

(RF=8, MF= 0.51), never/ever  (RF= 7, MF= 0.45), largely/strongly/clearly/deeply/fully (RF=5, 

MF= 0.32), and definitely/indefinitely/decisively (RF= 3, MF= 0.19). The following example from 

the English shows the use of these adverbs:    

(27) But America is certainly leaving. “Afghans are going to have to decide their future,” Biden said on 

June 25… 

4.3.2.3 Quantifiers/Determiners 

Quantifiers or determiners functioning as boosting features ranked third among boosting 

categories  used in  the English corpus. They occurred in 15 instances and recorded  0.96  tokens 

PTW.  These  boosting  features  are  used  to  indicate  a  complete  modification  of  the  category  of 

nouns. The quantifiers any and all were the most frequently used boosting subcategories in the 

English corpus. The following example from the English corpus illustrates the use of this category: 

(28) His rise is bad news for all Iranians, especially members of religious minorities.  

4.3.2.4 Adjectives 

  Adjectives were the fourth most frequent category of boosting occurring in 9 instances 

and  recording  0.57  adjectives  PTW  in  the  whole  English  corpus.  Hyland  (2005b)  states  that 

adjectives, similar to verbs, function as intensifiers to increase the strength of a claim making it 

more emphatic and convincing. Obvious, considerable, significant, large, and remarkable were 

the only boosting adjectives used in the English corpus. The following example from the English 

corpus illustrates the use of such adjectives:   

 
 
 
 
                                     
 
                                                 
 
 
 
 
                      
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                       
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
      
84 

(29) The most obvious and important point is that a dangerously naïve consensus has emerged in Israel…   

4.3.2.5 Indefinite/Impersonal Pronouns   

Indefinite  or  impersonal  pronouns  were  the  fifth  most  used  category  of  boosting.  They 

occurred  in  7  instances  and  recorded  0.45  pronouns  PTW  in  the  entire  English  corpus.  Much, 

anything, anyone, everything, and anymore were the only indefinite boosting pronouns used in the 

whole  English  corpus.  The  following  example  shows  the  use  of  such  pronouns  in  the  English 

corpus:                                                                                                                                                     

(30) Much also depends on what Hamas chooses to do.   

4.3.2.6 Epistemic Lexical Verbs 

The  sixth  most  frequently  used  boosting  feature  was  epistemic  lexical  verbs,  which  are 

used to amplify or boost a statement or claim. They occurred in 5 instances and recorded 0.32 

verbs PTW in the whole English corpus. Prove, assert, and confirm were the only epistemic lexical 

verbs used as boosting features in the English corpus as shown in the following example:               

(31) As President Biden has asserted, the Afghan government bears the major responsibility.   

4.3.2.7 Other Categories of Boosting  

There  were  other  boosting  categories  less  frequently  used  such  as  introductory 

phrases/verbs,  compound  boosting,  nouns,  and  metalinguistic  comments. 

Introductory 

phrases/verbs occurred in only 4 instances and recorded 0.25 tokens PTW in the whole English 

corpus.  The  following  example  illustrates  the  use  of  introductory  phrases/verbs  as  boosting 

features in the English corpus:   

(30)…it’s precisely that geniality that raises the ire of Raisi and his spiteful Islamist worldview.          

Moreover, in the English corpus, compound boosting was a less frequently used category. 

It  refers  to  combining  two  boosting  to  amplify  a  claim  or  proposition.  Compound  boosting 

occurred in 4 instances and recorded 0.25 tokens PTW. Two types of compound boosting were 

used in the English corpus: Double and treble boosting. The following example shows the use of 

compound boosting in the English corpus: 

(31) That future will almost certainly not include an offer from Madrid… 

Nouns  functioning  as  boosting  features  were  scarcely  used  in  the  English  corpus.  They 

occurred in 4 instances and recorded 0.25 nouns PTW in the whole corpus. No doubt and fact were 

the only boosting subcategories of nouns occurring in the English corpus. Example (32) below 

shows the use of such nouns in the English corpus:                                                                                                         

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                                  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
           
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
                                      
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
85 

(32)…Mr Biden is happy to pass the role of mediator passively to China, which seems keen to assert its 

power in the interests of peace (and, no doubt, of China). 

Metalinguistic Comments were the least frequently used category of boosting occurring in 

only  3  instances  and  recording  0.19  tokens  PTW  in  the  whole  corpus.  The  following  example 

shows one instance of use of metalinguistic comments in the English corpus:     

(33) Unless, of course, López Obrador is not really looking ahead…  

4.3.3 Frequency and Categories of Hedging Features in the Arabic Corpus

English and Arabic corpora?  Unlike the ENErs, the analysis of the Arabic corpus showed that the 

ANErs used 20 categories of hedging to mitigate their claims. However, the ANErs used limitedly 

less varied subcategories of hedging. Similarly, Samaie et al. (2014) found that the PNEs used less 

varied subcategories of hedging than the ENEs. In the present research, the ENErs employed 143 

subcategories  of  hedging  features,  whereas  the  ANErs  used  102  subcategories.  Table  4.14  and 

figure 4.2 present the overall raw and mean frequency for every category of hedging in the entire

4.3.3.1 Epistemic Lexical Verbs

occurring  in  86  instances  and  recording  6.12  tokens  PTW.  Abbadi  (2014),  in  a  study  of

most frequently  used category in  the ANEs with  a percentage of 19.6%. As illustrated in table

4.3.3.2 Adverbs of Frequency, Time, or Degree (Propositional Phrases) 

Adverbs of frequency, time, or degree, (also prepositional phrases or approximators) ranked 

second among categories of hedging in the Arabic corpus occurring in 70 instances and recording 

4.98 tokens PTW in the whole Arabic corpus. Similarly, Al-Ghoweri and Al-Kayed (2019) found 

that  approximators  of  degree,  quantity,  and  frequency  were  the  most  frequently  used  hedging 

category in the economic ANEs. To indicate imprecision or limitation on the applicability of their 

arguments,  Arabic  writers  mostly  used  the  adverbs  of  frequency  such  as  ‘ādatan  (usally/    ةداع), 

aḥāynan  (somtimes/    انايحأ),  and  others  (Andrusenko,  2015).  Moreover,  Al-Zarieni  et  al.  (2019) 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                         
 
 
 
 
 
                                                    
 
88 

found  that  approximators  were  one  of  the  most  commonly  used  hedging  categories  in  Arabic 

abstracts written by Arab native drafters. As shown in table 4.16, 7 subcategories of adverbs of 

frequency, time, or degree were observed in the Arabic corpus:

Among adverbs of frequency, time, or degree in the Arabic corpus, ‘aḥyanan/fī- bā’aḍi al- 

‘aḥyān  (sometimes)  ranked  first  occurring  in  13  instances  and  recording  0.92  tokens  PTW. 

However, adverbs such as nādīran/minn-al-nadir, bil-kād (rarely/barely), ghaliban/‘ala ‘āghlab 

(often), and ‘ūmūman (generally) ranked second occurring as follows: Nādīran/minn-al-nadir/bil-

kād (rarely/barely)(RF=12 , MF= 0.85 ), ghaliban/‘ala ‘āghlab (often) (RF= 10 , MF= 0.71), and 

‘ūmūman  (generally)  (RF=  10  ,  MF=  0.71).  Takrīban  (almost)/nahwa  (about),  and  nisbīyan 

(relatively) /tadrījayan (gradually) were the second most frequently  used adverbs in the Arabic 

corpus occurring as follows: Taqrīban (almost)/naḥwa (about) (RF =9, MF = 0.64), and nisbīyan 

(relatively)/tadrījayan (gradually) (RF =6, MF =0.42). Adverbs such as mīrāran wa tikrīran (every 

now and then)/tāratan  (sometimes), ila  ḥaden mā  (to some extent),  ila  ḥaden mū’ain (to some 

degree), and  bī- naḥwin/bī- ḥawali  (about/almost) were  a  less frequent  subcategory of hedging 

occurring as  follows: Mīrāran wa tikrīran (every now and then)/tāratan (sometimes) (RF = 5, MF 

=0.35), ila ḥaden mā (to some extent)/ila ḥaden mū’ain ( to some degree/to some degree) (RF= 4, 

 
                                                                                                            
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
89 

MF= 0.28 ), and bī- naḥwin/bī- ḥawali (about/almost) (RF=4, MF=0.28). However, adverbs such 

as ’ala ‘āqa/’ala māda (at least/on level), min-al- mānṭīqi (logically), li- or ila naḥwin(to some 

extent/almost), and li- dārāja were the least used subcategory occurring as follows: ’ala ‘āqa/’ala 

māda (at least/on level) (RF=3, MF=0.21), min-al- mānṭīqi (logically) (RF= 2, MF=0.12), li- or ila 

naḥwin(to some extent/ almost)/li- dārāja (to a particular degree) (RF=2, MF=012).The following 

example shows the use of such adverbs in the Arabic corpus:   

   By completely constructing the financial structure 
in  its  all  pillars,  economy  gradually  moves  from 
stagnancy...

4.3.3.4 Modal Verbs    

According  to  Obeidat  (1998),  Arabic  modal  verbs  mitigate  the  content  of  the  whole 

sentence, which they introduce and function as sentence modifiers. Modal verbs functioning as 

hedging features were the fourth most frequent category occurring in 48 instances and recording 

3.42 tokens PTW in the entire Arabic corpus. It was clearly observed that the ENErs tended to use 

modal  verbs  more  frequently  than  the  ANErs.  This  finding  does  not  support  the  findings  of 

Sedaghat et al. (2015) of the use of modal verbs in the PNEs and ENEs. These researchers found 

that the PNErs tended to use modal verbs more frequently than the ENErs as hedging features. As 

shown in table 4.17, 4 subcategories of modal verbs were observed in the Arabic corpus:

4.3.3.6 Rhetorical Questions  

In MSA, rhetorical questions usually contain a question words such as mann (who/نم), li-

mādha (why/اذامل), hal (do/does/is…/له), aina (where/نيأ), and others. Rhetorical questions were 

used at comparable rates with conditional clauses. They ranked fifth among hedging category in 

the  Arabic  corpus  occurring  in  29  instances  and  recording  2.06  tokens  PTW.  The  following 

example from the corpus shows the use of rhetorical questions in the Arabic corpus:      

Is  it  real  to  declare  that  everybody  should

4.3.3.9 Concessive Conjunctions                                                                                 

Concessive  conjunctions  were  the  eighth  most  frequently  used  hedging  feature  in  the 

Arabic corpus occurring in 20 instances and recording 1.42 tokens PTW in the whole corpus. Most 

structures of Arabic concessive conjunctions in the corpus included sentences containing rūghma 

‘an or ala rūghmi ‘an (although/نأ مغرلا ىلع/نأ مغر).This supports Abbadi’s (2014) findings, who 

found that  ala  rūghma  ‘an and  ‘bil-  rūghmi min (although)  were the most frequent concessive 

expressions employed in the ANEs. The following example from the corpus shows the use of such 

category:   

Although  this  seems  to  break  the  stalemate 
between all parties, nothing is really hopeful…

corpus  occurring  in  14  instances  and  recording  1.00  tokens  PTW  in  the  whole  corpus.  The 

following example from the Arabic corpus shows the use of rubbama as a hedging feature:  

The Israeli might think to exploit limited time as

4.3.3.15 Qad Structure   

Qad structure functioning as a hedging feature usually consists of the imperfective present 

form of verb al-mūḍara’ (present/عراضملا) preceded by the particle qad (e.g. qad yakūn/it would 

be/نوكي دق). According to Abdalla (2018, p.2004), when qad precedes the present form of the verb 

(imperfect); its meaning or function indicates taqrīb or taqlīl (approximation/reduced possibility/ 

diminution/بيرقت/ليلقت); and it functions to reduce the force of the verb. Qad structure was the tenth 

most  frequently  used  hedging  category  in  the  Arabic  corpus  occurring  in  12  instances  and 

recording  0.85  tokens  PTW.  The  following  example  from  the  corpus  shows  the  use  of  qad 

structure:   

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
      
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                      
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
93 

Washington  sees  that  the  pressure  it  has  put  on 
Russia  for  many  years  would  enforce  the  latter  to  do 
some resignation…

4.3.3.20 Similar-to-Verb Particles    

   To  reiterate,  Arab  linguists  labeled  some  particles  as  similar-to-verb  particles.  These 

particles according to them have a function similar to verbs. Some of these particles are employed 

to  mitigate  a  particular  proposition  thus  function  as  hedging  features  in  Arabic  language  (AL-

Semeiri, Shunmugam, & Kadhim, 2018). Similar-to-verb particles ranked fifteenth among hedging 

categories occurring in 7 instances and recording 0.50 tokens PTW in the whole Arabic corpus. In 

the Arabic corpus, 2 subcategories of similar-to-verb particles were observed. First, ka’anna (as 

if/  نأكو), which according to Arab semanticists, is used as a tashbīh particle (likening particle/ ةادأ

هيبشت);  was  frequent  in  the  Arabic  corpus  occurring  as  follows:  (RF=5,  MF=  0.35).  Lā’alla 

(  لعل/hopefully may) is semantically used as a tārajji particle (a particle of wishing desirability or 

wishfulness/ي جرت)  occurring  as  follows:  (RF=2,  MF=0.14).  The  following  example  from  the 

corpus shows the use of similar-to-verb particles:   

The USA seemed  as if it has been surprised by 
the  event,  or as if it  did  not  prepare itself,  as  it 
should be… 

 مل 

اهنأك

 وأ

،ثدحلاب

اه 
                       ...

)

in 59 instances and recording 4.20 tokens PTW. Modal verbs like other boosting features enhance 

the  forcefulness  of  statements.  One  most  frequently  used  structure  to  amplify  verbs,  and  thus 

function as a boosting feature is the use of particles such as sa (will/اس), sawfa (going to/فوس), and 

lann (will not/نل) preceded by a present tense verb. This structure was very frequent in the Arabic 

corpus occurring in  32 instances and recording  2.27 tokens PTW. However, the modal verb  la

4.4 Functional Analysis of Hedging and Boosting in both the English and Arabic Corpora

The following section attempts to answer the research question: What are the pragmatic 

functions of hedging and boosting in the ENEs and ANEs? As previously mentioned, linguists and 

pragmatists (e.g., Skelton, 1988; Hyland, 1994, 1998a; Salager-Meyer, 1994, 2011; Varttala, 2001; 

Fraser, 2010) demonstrated that hedging and boosting can accomplish, in addition to their semantic 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                  
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                   
                           
 
       
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
        
 
101 

functions, several pragmatic functions according to the socio-cultural contexts or communicative 

situations where they occur. In other words, hedging and boosting can serve different functions, 

which may change according to the context of propositional situation itself. 

4.4.1 Functions of hedging in the English and Arabic Corpora:   

The pragmatic functions of hedging can be summarized and clarified in relation to both the 

Arabic and English categories of hedging in the two sets of corpora as follows: 

4.4.1.1 Modifying Claims and Showing Imprecision 

Obviously, the use of various categories of hedging in both the ENEs and ANEs such as 

modal verbs, adverbs of frequency, degree, or time, agentless passives , epistemic lexical verbs, 

and  others  was  mainly  to  mitigate  claims  and  to  show  some  kind  of  uncertainty  or  confident 

imprecision when the precise information was not available or intentionally avoided. It seems that 

both the ENErs and ANErs used such hedging features to modify their propositions, present their 

claims  as  opinions  rather  than  facts,  and  thus  avoid  being  fully  committed  to  the  claims  they 

presented  (Hyland,  1994,  1998a;  Salager-Meyer,  1994,  2011;  Skelton,  1988;  Varttala,  2001; 

Fraser, 2010). This can be illustrated in the following examples: 

(60) Stability in the Middle East might be a pipe dream… 
(61) Most peace initiatives run into the sand…   
The  contest  between  Russia  and  China  over  a 
number of files could lead into a conflict… 

ينكم 
) 

 تافلملا نم ددع يف نيصلاو ايسور نيب سفانتلا نأب
yumkin/could/would

(62)  
ىلإ  ايجيردت لوحتي نأ

 عارص... (

Example (60) shows that the ENEr used might as a hedging feature to express some degree 

of doubt and uncertainty about the political situation in the Arab countries and to mitigate his/her 

claim when he/she predicted the future and stability in the Middle East. Moreover, some hedging 

features can be used with numerical expressions or countable entities in order to show imprecision, 

uncertainty,  indefiniteness,  or  lack  of  commitment  on  the  part  of  NErs  to  the  exactness  of  the 

expressed proposition as shown in example (61). In example (61), the ENEr could mention exactly 

the number of the initiatives that failed to achieve their purposes, but it seems that he or she wanted 

to  mitigate  his/her  claim  through  using  the  quantifiers  most  allowing  the  reader  to  be  free  to 

estimate the number of initiatives that run into the sand. In other words, most was used in example 

(61)  to  indicate  a  kind  of  indefiniteness  or  imprecision  when  the  precise  information  was  not 

available or purposely avoided. Similarly, in example (62), it can be noted that the ANEr used the 

modal verb yumkin (could/نكمي) to add a sense of uncertainty; to soften the claim expressed; and 

 
                                                
 
 
 
 
                                             
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                                                                                           
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
102 

to  reduce  the  strength  of  claim  of  what  might  happen  concerning  the  future  of  relationships 

between Russia and China. Without the use of  might in the English corpus and yumkin (could/ 

نكمي) in the Arabic one, the propositions would be too direct or too assertive.    

4.4.1.2 Avoiding Categorical Commitment  

Hedging might be very effective means used by the writer to avoid complete responsibility 

and lessen the reader’s misinterpretation or rejection. In addition to modifying a proposition, it 

seems that both the ENErs and ANErs used several hedging features such as modal verbs, adverbs, 

agentless passive, reference structures, and qad structure to indicate a lack of full commitment to 

their claims and thus avoid categorical responsibility as shown in examples (63) and (64):   

Obviously,  since  the  beginning,  this  submariner 
حضاولا
min-al-
 (
would be the greatest and fastest deterrence…             
(64) If you’re under 50, you probably don’t remember when Japan was going to take over the world. 

 نأ
 يف
waḍiḥ/obviously

ةصاوغلا
) 

لكشتس

ةوق

(63)  نمو
