As  example  (63)  shows,  by  using  the  adverb  (prepositional  phrase)  min-al-waḍiḥ  (نم 

حضاولا/obviously),  the  ANEr  obviously  avoided  being  fully  committed  to  his/her  claim  the 

submariner  would  be  the  greatest  and  fastest  deterrence,  in  case  he/she  might  provide  untrue 

analysis about future events in the world. Similarly, as shown in (64), by incorporating the modal 

adverb  probably  to  hedge  his/her  proposition,  the  ENEr  seemed  to  avoid  being  categorically 

committed or responsible for his/her claim or state of knowledge when he/she discussed Japan’s 

domination of the world with his/her reader attempting to be objective or distanced himself/herself 

from the claim (Buitkienė, 2008). 

4.4.1.3 Achieving Politeness and Showing Modesty     

As mentioned earlier, hedging, as a rhetorical and metadiscursive strategy, is used to create 

an interpersonal interaction between the writer and the reader in a discourse (Hyland, 2005a, 2017). 

According to Holmes (1988, p.22), hedging indicates “deference rather than uncertainty.” To put 

it  differently,  hedging  is  a  politeness  strategy,  which  the  writer/speaker  uses  to  save  the 

reader/listener’s negative face or minimize the face-threating acts implicitly by avoiding imposing 

views on the reader/listener. In other words, when the writer/speaker gives the reader/listener the 

freedom to judge for himself/herself; and whenever the writer/speaker does not impose facts or 

claims on the reader/listener by using hedging; he/she supposedly saves the negative or positive 

face of his/her readers/listeners (i.e. avoids FTAs) and shows his/her modesty toward the reader or 

the state of knowledge (Brown and Levinson, 1978, 1987). Apparently, it seems that ENErs and 

 
                          
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
      
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
                                             
 
 
 
 
 
 
103 

ANErs  in  the  present  research  employed  hedging  in  their  texts  to  demonstrate  that  what  they 

presented was  limited or subjective, to  avoid  any direct  imposition on their readers, to  achieve 

linguistic  politeness,  and  thus  to  show  modesty  towards  the  reader  or  the  claim  presented  as 

illustrated in the following examples:   

(65) One effect of this rule would be to hasten the transition from fossil fuels. 
يرشبلا
People’s  Protection  and  their  human  capital  are 
نوكيس
everybody’s responsibility; if we do not admit that, 
the effect of consequences will be global... 
Finally, the world needs to exert much more efforts 
and more research in the domain of investment…

ديزم
/
mazīd

As example (65) shows, the ENEr apparently used the modal verb would to mitigate his/her 

proposition, indicate the probability of his/her claims, strengthen his/her interpersonal relation with 

the reader, and thus avoid any confrontation with the reader in case that his/her claim one effect of 

this rule would be to hasten the transition from fossil fuels would be false. The ENEr seemed to 

use  would  to  suggest  a  hypothetical  statement  while  presenting  a  less  definite  claim  about  the 

future changes in the use of fossil fuels. Consequently, the ENEr mitigated his/her proposition to 

be polite and to be accepted because this claim might contradict his/her readers’ views. Similarly, 

it also seems that the ANEr in example (66) was very considerate and tentative in presenting his/her 

claims;  and  therefore,  he/she  hedged  (i.e.  by  a  conditional  clause)  his/her  proposition.  In  other 

words, the ANEr used the conditional clause to present his/her position tentatively and to present 

the  information  about  the  issue  of  water  cautiously,  avoiding  any  confrontation  with  his/her 

readers. Moreover, the ANEr wanted to present his/her claim the effect of consequences will be 

global in a more polite and diplomatic way by using the conditional clause in order to persuade 

his/her  readers  that  the  effect  of  these  consequences  were  serious,  so  he  or  she  should  do 

something. Moreover, example (77) shows that the ANEr used the quantifier mazīd (more/ديزم) to 

approximate  the  expected  or  probable  number  of  efforts  and  researches  in  the  domain  of 

investment and thus avoid being too direct or impolite. To put  it differently, it appears that the 

ANEr wanted to lessen the threat of being rejected and save his or her reader’s face when he asked 

politely the world governments to exert more efforts in the domain of investment.                                   

4.4.1.4 Establishing Reputation and Avoiding Direct Criticism               

In the present research, it seems that both the ENErs and ANErs incorporated hedging in 

their  texts  to  protect  themselves  against  being  criticized  by  their  readership  especially  when 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
      
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
104 

predicting future events or consequences in the world or to reduce the risk of opposition of readers 

(Carrio-Pastor,  2017).  Obviously,  these  editors  employed  hedging  to  build  a  national, 

international,  and  social  reputation  for  their  newspapers  and  most  importantly  to  achieve 

journalistic  or  business  acceptance  and  success.  According  to  Jensen  (2008),  “journalists  were 

viewed as more trustworthy (a) when news coverage of cancer research was hedged” (p. 347). In 

other  words,  when  an  editor  uses  hedging,  he/she  will  be  more  truthful,  precise,  and  reputable 

(Rabab’ah & Abu Rumman, 2015) as shown in the following examples:   

(68) But the winds seem favorable, at least for some type of revived agreement.                                                                                                                                                     
(69) The jump in US annual consumer price inflation to 4.2 per cent reported…was a shock. 
نيصلا
“China  would  be  17  percent  out  of  the  world  gross 
domestic product”, according to Leen Chirenge…

By inserting the epistemic lexical verb seem, the determiner some, and the adverb of degree 

at least in his/her sentence in examples (68), (69), and (70), the ENEr or ANEr appeared to be 

more precise or truthful in presenting his/her arguments or claims, and thus he/she avoided being 

criticized  or  proven  deceptive  in  case  there  would  be  no  chances  for  some  type  of  revived 

agreement between the USA and Iran. Similarly, example (69) demonstrates that the ENEr showed 

some  degree  of  imprecision  about  future  circumstances  and  wanted  to  avoid  being  fully 

responsible or disreputable; so he/she employed the agentless passive reported in case the reported 

statement the jump in US annual consumer price inflation to 4.2 per cent last week was a shock 

would be untrue in the future. Furthermore, as shown in example (70), the ANEr presented his/her 

claim  cautiously  by  using  the  reference  structure  wifqan  li  (يل  اقفو/according  to)  to  avoid  any 

probable criticism or refutation  against his/her newspaper if  China  will not  be  17 % out  of  the 

world gross domestic product in the future.                                                                                                                                        

4.4.1.5 Engaging and Interacting with the Reader    

As interpersonal metadiscursive feature, hedging plays an important role in strengthening 

an  interpersonal  relationship  between  the  writer  and  the  reader  when  they  are  employed  in  a 

discourse. In fact, whenever the writer gives the reader the freedom to judge for himself/herself, 

negotiate,  and  interpret  the  state  of  knowledge  or  claims  the  writer  presents,  he/she  is  very 

concerned  to  establish  a  better  interaction  with  the  reader  (Hyland,  2005a;  Jabbar,  2019). 

According to Khabbazi-Oskouei (2012), writers may use rhetorical questions as hedging features 

to  establish  a  more  dialogic  interaction  with  their  readers  and  thus  gain  acceptance  for  their 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                  
  
 
                                                              
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
105 

arguments or claims. Some hedging features such as introductory phrases, epistemic lexical verbs, 

rhetorical questions, metalinguistic comments and others were typically used by both the ENErs 

and ANErs in the present research to involve the reader in a dialogic interaction and share with 

him/her their opinions about issues in the world as shown in the following examples:           

(71) So how does its innovative power look today? 
We should not mistake supposing that Biden-
Ardoghal’s summit would completely mean a 
Turkish-American conciliation.   

 ناغودرأ

ندياب ة مق   نأ

ضارتفا يف ئطخن  ّلاأ انيلع نكل

 (72)
ة يكرت ةحلاصم ةيادب  امتح ينعتس
-

. 

ةيكيرمأ

As example (71) shows, when posing the rhetorical question, the ENEr did not want his/her 

reader  to  answer  his/her  question,  but  he/she  wanted  to  request  the  reader’s  involvement  and 

interaction in order for his/her reader to participate in decision making and to share with him/her 

his/her views regarding the innovative power, international policies, and political relationships. 

Similarly, as example (72) shows, by modifying his/her proposition using the introductory phrase 

alyyana anl la nukhta’ fi- iftirāḍ (we must not be mistaken to suppose/ضارتفا يف ئطخن لاأ انيلع), the 

ANEr intended to strengthen his/her relationship and interaction with his/her reader in order for 

him/her to share with him/her the view that Biden-Ardoghal’s summit would completely mean a 

Turkish-American conciliation.    

4.4.1.6 Presenting Claims as Opinions        

The presence of hedging in a discourse might indicate that the information or claim the 

writer presents is his/her subjective or personal opinion rather than a fact (Hyland, 2005a; Carrio-

Pastor, 2017). Moreover, since the genre of NEs is usually labeled as opinion articles, it seems that 

the use of hedging in an NE is very necessary to persuade and interact with the reader as they are 

used to enforce an opinion rather than a fact. Apparently, both the ENErs and ANErs in the present 

research employed hedging to present their claims as their personal views or their newspaper’s 

opinions rather than factual information as shown in the following examples from the corpora:  

(73) It would be fair to conclude that inflation expectations are moving up.      
Washington  sees  that  the  pressure  it  has  put  on 
Russia for many years would enforce it to do some 
resignation… 

 ايسور ىلع هتسرام يذلا طغضلا نأ نطنشاو ىرتو
تلازانت ميدقت ىلإ ةريخلأا

 (74)  
عفدي دق
 تاونس لاوط
   (qad yadfa’/ would enforce)

… 

As  example  (73)  illustrates,  the  ENEr  modified  his/her  proposition  by  using  the 

introductory phrase it would be fair to conclude that to declare that what he/she argued was his 

 
                                                                                                                                        
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
         
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                                                   
 
 
 
         
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
106 

personal  views  regarding  expectations  of  rising  in  inflation  rather  than  a  factual  piece  of 

information. Similarly, as shown in example (74), the ANEr hedged his/her proposition by using 

the structure of qad (may/would/دق) to present his/her statement Washington’s pressure on Russia 

would  enforce  it  to  do  some  resignation  as  his  subjective  and  personal  interpretation  of 

Washington-Moscow relationships; so the reader was free to accept or  refuse this statement. In 

other words, hedging a proposition in both the English and Arabic corpora allowed the reader to 

judge for himself and to give him/her the freedom to decide the validity of information, the truth 

of claim, or the exact number of an entity. 

4.4.1.7 Developing Argumentation and Achieving Persuasion      

To reiterate, hedging features, if they are appropriately used, can be effective features in 

producing persuasive writing, supporting the writer’s opinion, and building a strong writer-reader 

relationship  (Hyland,  2004,  2005a).  Moreover,  hedging  as  a  rhetorical  strategy  can  play  an 

important role as it might affect how strongly readers accept the truth of the information presented 

in  a  discourse.  Taking  precautions  against  presenting  categorical  statements  (i.e.  hedging  a 

proposition)  allows  writers  to  refute  possible  counter-arguments,  and  thus,  strengthens  their 

arguments  (Hyland,  1998b).  Hedging,  as  a  rhetorical  linguistic  feature,  can  be  powerful  in 

persuading the readers and influencing their attitudes towards the subject matter covered in the 

text (Vande Kopple & Crismore, 1997; Dafouz-Milne, 2008; Zarza, 2016). To put it differently, 

whenever writers hedge their state of knowledge, they intend to develop a better interaction with 

their readers for the purpose of affecting or persuading them to do some actions. In other words, 

writers use hedging not only for mitigation, but also for convincing and influencing their readers 

to do further actions as shown in the following examples from the corpora:  

(75) I’m a bit surprised, by the way, to learn that Japan and Singapore have so much of the market... 
Maybe  the  Israeli  also  thinks  of  exploiting  the  short 
time until the end of the Iranian presidential elections...

As example (75) shows, the ENEr seemed to use the metalinguistic comments by the way 

not only to modify his claim or avoid direct involvement but also to convince his/her readers to 

believe  that  his/her  discussion  and  expectation  regarding  Japan  and  Singapore’s  dominance  of 

global economies and markets were reliable and true; and thus he achieved persuasion. Similarly, 

in example (76), it appears that the ANEr hedged his/her proposition using the particle rubbama 

(maybe/امبر),  in  addition  to  other  objectives,  to  persuade  his/her  readers  to  adopt  his  views

specifically  when  he/she  presented  claims  or  facts  that  might  contradict  the  reader’s  interests 

concerning the exploitation of Israelis the time until the end of the Iranian presidential elections.                        

4.4.2 Functions of Boosting the ENEs and ANEs       

4.4.2.1 Developing Argumentation and Achieving Persuasion 

It seems that both ENErs and ANErs used boosting category to present claims as definitive 

and incontrovertible and to make his/her arguments more persuasive and his/her position more 

powerful. Apparently, the ENErs and ANErs, by inserting boosting features in their texts, appealed 

to their readers’ emotions, and consequently influence their readers’ opinions.  In other words, 

boosting is an effective feature used to create a persuasive tone by emphasizing the strength and 

validity of the writer’s claims (Crismore et al., 1993; Hyland, 2005)  as shown in the following 

example:  

(77) Iran will hold a presidential election in June to replace incumbent President Hassan Rouhani, who 
cannot run for a third consecutive term. 

In example (77), the ENEr apparently used the modal verbs will and cannot to appeal to 

his/her reader’s sentimentality,  to reinforce his/her claim, and thus to achieve persuasion. It seems 

that the ENEr wanted to persuade their readers emotionally and emphatically that his/her claim 

that Iran will hold a presidential election in June and the current president Hassan Rouhani cannot 

run for a third consecutive term is extremely true.      

4.4.2.2 Establishing Authority and Expertise 

By using boosting, writers can establish their expertise, emphasize their certainty, assert 

their confidence, and enhance the validity of their claim and credibility (Hyland, 2005b). In other 

words,  boosting  functions  to  position  the  writer  as  an  authoritative  figure  and  truthful  person 

(Varttala, 2001) as shown in following example:  

It  seems  clearly  that  US  President  Joseph  Biden  is 
publicly  determined  to  resolve  the  Turkish  issue, 
which he considers urgent... 

 ندياب

مزاع
ةحلم… 

فيزوج
اهربتعي

يكريملأا
 يتلا

ايكرت

 نأ

 سيئرلا

(clearly/

wāḍiḥann
(

In  example  (78),  it  seems  the  ANEr  used  the  adverbs  wāḍiḥann  (clearly)  as  a  boosting 

feature to enhance his/her position, to establish his/her expertise, and enhance the validity of his/her 

claim and credibility. He/she wanted to say that the claim that the US President Biden is publicly 

determined to resolve the Turkish issue is undoubtedly true and thus position himself/herself as an 

 
 
 
                                       
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
             
 
 
 
             
              
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
    
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
108 

authoritative and truthful person. 

4.4.2.3 Engaging the Reader 

One more important function boosting achieves is to engage the reader with the writer in a 

dialogic interaction. Boosting use helps the writer draw the reader's attention by making statements 

more emphatic and compelling (Brown & Levinson, 1987). In other words, boosting function to 

create a sense that  the information  and  claim  the writer presents  are important to  consider and 

believe,  therefore,  the  reader  should  participate  with  the  writer  in  his/her  claim  and  decision 

making as shown in the following example: 

(79) As the deputy assistant secretary for Israeli and Palestinian affairs at the US State Department, he 
no doubt knows his stuff but, reaching for a historical parallel, he is no Henry Kissinger. 

As example (79) shows, the ENEr seems that he/she used the boosting noun no doubt to 

catch  his/her  reader’s  attention,  so  he/she  will  be  engaged  with  the  writer  in  claiming  that  the 

deputy secretary for Israeli and Palestinian affairs no doubt knows his stuff but he is no Henry 

Kissinger is true and important to consider and believe.  

4.4.2.4 Indicating Conviction 

Boosting  is  a  rhetorical  feature  that  strengthens  a  claim  by  using  emphatic  language  or 

intensifiers,  thereby  conveying  the  speaker’s  conviction  and  strong  belief  in  their  statement 

(Hyland, 2005b). In other words, boosting helps the writer to express his/her strong belief in his/her 

argument, which can enhance the reader’s confidence. Moreover, boosting features indicate the 

writer's  full  commitment  to  his/her  stance  or  claim  (Brown  &  Levinson,  1987).  The  following 

example clarifies this point: 

The  warning  of  danger  came  from  the  Secretary-
General  of  the  Tunisian  General  Labour  Union 
himself, whose statements are beyond doubt...

It seems that the ANErs used the boosting epistemic lexical verb la taktanef al-shūkūk (it 

goes without doubt) to express his/her conviction fully, and thus he/she wanted to engage his/her 

reader  in  these  beliefs  and  convictions.  In  other  words,  the  ANErs  wanted  to  persuade  his/her 

reader  that  the  belief  that  the  Tunisian  Secretary-General’s  warning  of  danger  was  completely 

doubtless.  

4.4.2.5 Obeying Cultural Norms and Models: 

 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                    
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                                                                                                                       
 
 
 
 
 
 
109 

One important factor, which affects the use of hedging and boosting, is the cultural norms 

and models that prevail in a particular discourse community. According to Al-Otaibi (2018), in 

some cultures like Arabic, using boosting is necessary, as these cultures prefer assertive styles and 

confident expressions. In other words, the frequent use of boosting by the ANErs reflects cultural 

values of assertiveness and moral certainty, and the more frequent use of hedging by the ENErs 

reflects cultural values of tentativeness and moral uncertainty as shown in the following example: 

In this equation, China and Russia will constitute 
the two major powers that will limit the expansion 
of the influence of old colonialism.

As shown in example (81), the ANEr tended to use two modal verbs in the same sentence 

to amplify or boost his/her claim and position. The frequent use of boosting features by the ANEr 

is associated with his/her cultural models and values to which he/she belongs. The ANEr’s cultural 

models  seem  to  prefer  the  direct  and  emphatic  presentation  of  claims  or  positions  (Gee,  2011, 

2012). It seems that the frequent use of modal verbs by the ANErs conveys his/her obedience of 

assertive cultural values.  

4.4 Inferential Findings   

 The findings of the present research revealed that, in the English and Arabic corpora, there 

were some similarities and differences in terms of form, frequency, and function of hedging and 

boosting.  The  similarities  in  terms  of  form  and  function  may  suggest  that  these  rhetorical  and 

metadiscursive resources are an integral part of persuasive or argumentative genres such as NEs 

and common to all languages of the world. However, differences in the distribution or preference 

of  these  features  can  be  explained  in  terms  of  cross-linguistic,  cross-cultural,  and  pragmatic 

peculiarities  of  these  languages  (Al-Rickaby,  2016).  To  reiterate,  according  to  the  descriptive 

findings mentioned earlier in this chapter, the overall ENEs contained 768 instances of hedging, 

while the overall ANEs contained 479 instances of this feature. On average, the ENErs used 48.94 

tokens of hedging PTW, whereas  the ANErs used  33.94 tokens PTW. In other words, hedging 

features were used more frequently in the ENEs compared to the ANEs. Similarly, the ENErs used 

totally 166 instances of boosting. On average, the ENEs contained 10.57 tokens of boosting PTW.  

However, the ANErs used 182 instances of boosting, i.e., on average, the ANEs contained 12.95 

tokens of boosting PTW. In other words, the ANErs boosted their propositions more often than 

their English counterparts did. Similarly, the ENErs hedged their propositions approximately twice 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
               
    
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
          
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
110 

more often than their Arab counterparts. In other words, the ENErs were more tentative or cautious 

when they presented their opinions or claims in their texts than the ANErs. However, the ANErs 

were more assertive and direct in presenting their claims as they used a more frequent number of 

boosting. 

Moreover, the most frequent category of hedging in the English corpus was modal verbs 

recording 9.50 tokens PTW, whereas the most frequent category of hedging in the Arabic corpus 

was  epistemic  lexical  verbs  recording  6.12  tokens  PTW.  Similarly,  the  least  frequently  used 

category of hedging in the English corpus was modal nouns recording 0.21 tokens PTW, while the 

least frequent category of hedging in the Arabic corpus was metalinguistic comments recording 

0.14 tokens PTW. Moreover, the most frequent category of boosting in the English corpus was 

modal verbs recording 4.40 tokens PTW, whereas the most frequent category of boosting in the 

Arabic corpus was adverbs of frequency, degree, or time recording 4.34 tokens PTW. Similarly, 

the least frequently used category of boosting in the English corpus was metalinguistic comments 

recording 0.19 tokens PTW, while the least frequent category of boosting in the Arabic corpus was 

impersonal  or  indefinite  pronouns  recording  0.50  tokens  PTW.    Similarly,  the  ENErs  used  19 

categories and 143 subcategories of hedging to mitigate their claims; and they used 10 categories 

and  66  subcategories  to  boost  their  claims.  However,  the  ANErs  used  20  categories  and  126 

subcategories of hedging to mitigate their propositions or claims; and they used 7 categories and 

43 subcategories of boosting to boost their claims. These findings indicate that the ENErs showed 

greater  variability  in  terms  of  usage  of  hedging  and  boosting  as  they  used  more  varied 

subcategories of those two features than their Arab colleagues did. This was similar to Al-Ghoweri 

& Al-Kayed’s (2019) findings, who found, in a comparative study of ENEs and ANEs in terms of 

use of hedging and boosting, that the ENErs hedged their texts more heavily and with a variety of 

features than the ANErs did, who used a more frequent number of boosting. According to these 

researchers’ findings, the ANEs in general were less frequently hedged than the English ones and 

the types of subcategories used in them were fewer compared to their English counterparts.   

Moreover, on average, every ENE contained 38.4 hedges per NE, and on average, every 

ANE  contained  23.85  hedges  per  NE.  Moreover,  one  ENE  consisted  of  69  hedges  maximally, 

while one ANE contained 45 hedges maximally. On the other hand, one ENE contained 16 hedges 

minimally, and one ANE contained only 10 hedges minimally. Similarly, on average, every ENE 

contained  8.3  boosters  per  NE,  and  on  average,  every  ANE  contained  9.1  boosters  per  NE. 

 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
111 

Moreover, one ENE consisted of 16 boosters maximally, while one ANE contained 23 boosters 

maximally. On the other hand, one ENE contained 2 boosters per NE minimally, and one ANE 

contained 3 boosters per NE minimally. It is clear from the minimum values that all the NEs in the 

two corpora contained hedging and boosting. Moreover, the difference in the two mean frequencies 

of  hedging  (48.94  PTW  for  ENEs  &  33.94  PTW  for  ANEs)  found  in  the  two  corpora  under 

research can be explained in terms of the differing lengths of NEs included in the two corpora. It 

is reasonable to argue that the longer the text the greater the chances that it contains such features 

at a greater frequency rate. This seems that the ENErs used more hedging features in their texts, 

as they were longer than their Arabic counterparts. Apparently, this may suggest that compared to 

their Arabic colleagues, the ENErs were much more cautious in presenting their claims as they 

hedged their claims more frequently; and therefore, the ENErs were more cautious or tentative in 

presenting their claims or journalistic information. However, the ANErs boosted their propositions 

more frequently than the ENErs (10.57 PTW for ENEs & 12.95 PTW for ANEs) despite the fact 

that ENEs were longer than ANEs. This is clear evidence that the length of text is not the only 

factor that affect the use of two features. Next section, the researcher will clarify what factors that 

influence the use of the two features.   

 As observed earlier, the NErs with different socio-cultural backgrounds preferred to use 

several  different  lexico-grammatical  categories  of  hedging  and  boosting  in  their  texts.  In  the 

English corpus, the six most frequently used hedging categories were modal verbs (RF = 149, MF 

= 9.50), agentless passives (RF = 106, MF =6.76), adverbs of degree, time, or frequency (RF = 88, 

MF  =5.61),  quantifiers/determiners  (RF  =86,  MF  =  5.48),  reference  structures  (RF  =  70,  MF 

=4.46), and epistemic lexical verbs (RF = 65, MF =4.14) occurring respectively. In the English 

corpus, the six most frequently used boosting categories were modal verbs (RF=69, MF=4.40), 

adverbs  of  degree,  time,  and  frequency  (RF=49,  MF=  3.12),  quantifiers/  determiners  (RF=  15, 

MF= 0.96), adjectives (RF= 9, MF= 0.57), indefinite/impersonal pronouns (RF= 7, MF= 0.45), 

epistemic  lexical  verbs  (RF=  5,  MF=  0.32)  occurring  respectively.  However,  the  rates  and 

distribution of categories and subcategories of hedging and boosting in the Arabic corpus were 

different from the English one. To put it differently, the six most frequent categories of hedging in 

the Arabic corpus were epistemic lexical verbs (RF = 86, MF = 6.12), adverbs of degree, time, or 

frequency (RF = 70, MF = 4.98), reference structures (RF = 56 , MF = 3.99), modal verbs (RF = 

48, MF = 3), conditional clauses (RF = 29 , MF =2.06), and rhetorical questions (RF =29, MF = 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
112 

2.06) occurring respectively. The six most frequent categories of boosting in the Arabic corpus 

were adverbs of degree, time, or frequency (RF = 61, MF =4.34), modal verbs (RF=59, MF=4.20), 

adjectives  (RF=  27,  MF=1.92),  quantifiers/determiners  (RF=12,  MF=0.85),  epistemic  lexical 

verbs (RF =8, MF=0.57), and nouns  (RF=8, MF=0.57) occurring respectively. Moreover, several 

categories of hedging and boosting occurring in the Arabic corpus were non-existent in the English 

corpus and vice versa as shown in table 4.23:
