As table 4.23 illustrates, adjectives and compound hedging as major categories of boosting 

in the English corpus, were not observed in the Arabic corpus altogether. Similarly, as prominent 

categories of hedging in the Arabic corpus, qad structure, similar-to-verb particles, and rubbama 

particle  were  non-existent  categories  in  the  English  corpus.  Similarly,  while  introductory 

phrases/verbs and metalinguistic comments exist as major categories of boosting in the English 

 
 
 
 
 
                       
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
113 

corpus, they were not observed in the Arabic one. Moreover, through comparing the use of hedging 

in  the  two  subcorpora  of  NEs  (ENEs  &  ANEs),  we  can  observe  that  there  are  comparatively 

significant differences in terms of use of such feature. To put it simply, ENErs used 48.94 tokens 

of hedging PTW, whereas ANErs used 34.08 tokens PTW. This suggests that ENErs, in the present 

research were more tentative and cautious when they presented their claims or state of knowledge 

than  their  Arab  counterparts.  However,  there  are  no  relatively  significant  differences  between 

ENErs and ANErs in terms of use of boosting, i.e. ENErs used 10.56 tokens of boosting and ANErs 

used 12.95 tokens of the feature. This suggests that both groups of NErs follow a similar cultural 

model and cultural norms when they use boosting, whereas their cultural model or behavior differ 

when they use boosting (Gee, 2011, 2012).  Furthermore,  the  findings  of  qualitative  analysis  of 

function  of  hedging  demonstrated  that  both  the  ENErs  and  ANErs  employed  these  features  to 

accomplish several pragmatic, rhetorical, and metadiscursive purposes in the present two corpora. 

The functional analysis of hedging categories showed that the ENErs and ANErs utilized different 

categories of hedging to modify their claims, indicate imprecision, avoid categorical commitment 

or complete responsibility, achieve politeness, and indicate modesty. Similarly, both the ENErs 

and  ANErs  seemed  to  use  hedging  to  establish  journalistic  reputation,  avoid  direct  criticism, 

involve and interact with the reader, present their claims as opinions rather than facts, and finally, 

develop argumentation and achieve persuasion. Moreover, both the ENErs and ANErs seemed to 

use boosting to develop argumentation and achieving Persuasion, establishing authority, engaging 

the reader, stablishing conviction, and obeying cultural norms and models. Factors and reasons of 

similarities  and  differences  between  the  two  linguistic  groups  in  terms  of  use  of  hedging  and 

boosting will be discuss in the discussion section. 

4.5 Discussion   

This section proceeds to discuss the descriptive and inferential findings of the research in 

the light of the theoretical framework adopted in the research, the relation of quantitative findings 

to qualitative analysis, and the relation of the findings of the present research to the findings of 

previous research. One main objective of the present research was to examine or compare the use 

(i.e., frequency, form, and function) of hedging and boosting features in two major sets of corpora 

(i.e.  English  and  Arabic  corpora)  in  the  genre  of  NEs  in  their  socio-cultural  contexts.  Having 

provided some points of similarities and differences in terms of usage of  such two features, we 

should answer the last research question: What  are the reasons of differences or preferences in 

 
 
               
 
 
               
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
114 

terms of usage of hedging and boosting features if there were any between the ENErs and ANErs? 

These differences and similarities regarding the use of such two features, which were observed in 

the previous section, can be reviewed through providing the following sub-questions and explicitly 

or implicitly attempting to answer them:  

Why did the ENErs hedge their propositions or claims twice more often than their Arab 

counterparts? On the other hand, why are there significance differences in terms of frequency of 

hedging between the two major English and Arabic corpora or between the three sub-genres of 

NEs?  Moreover,  why  ANErs  amplify  (boost)  their  claims  more  often  than  their  English 

counterparts?  To  reiterate,  the  theoretical  framework  of  the  current  research  is  based  on  Gee’s 

theory of socio-cultural model of linguistic features (1999, 2008, 2011, & 2012). One important 

aspect of Gee’s linguistic theory is the notion of situated meaning, cultural model, and discourse. 

Briefly, the situated meaning of an expression is that meaning determined by the socio-cultural 

specific  context  where  the  expression  is  used  or  uttered;  and  this  context  is  associated  with  a 

specific  cultural  models  followed  by  language  writers  or  speakers.  As  previously  illustrated, 

hedging  features  were  comparatively  very  frequently  used  by  both  the  ENErs  and  ANErs  to 

mitigate  their  arguments  or  claims  in  the  present  data.  The  overall  English  corpus  contained 

hedging features at a much higher frequency (RF = 768, MF = 48.94) than the Arabic one (RF= 

479, MF=34.08). However, the ANEs contained a relatively more frequent number of boosting 

features  (i.e.  occurring  in  182  instances  and  recording  12.95  tokens  PTW)  than  their  English 

counterparts (i.e. occurring in169 instances and recording 10.56 tokens PTW). In other words, the 

statements or claims presented by the AENrs were much more categorical or assertive compared 

to the statements presented by their English counterparts. These findings were particularly similar 

to Al-Ghoweri and Al-Kayed’s (2019) findings of the use of hedging and boosting in ENEs and 

ANEs.  According  to  these  researchers’  findings,  the  ENErs  used  hedging  significantly  more 

frequently  than  their  Arab  counterparts  did.  Similarly,  through  exploring  the  similarities  and 

differences between English and Indonesian research articles, Sanjaya (2013) found that overall 

English research articles were more tentative than Indonesian articles as English articles contained 

a more frequent number of hedging and Indonesian articles contained a more frequent number of 

boosting.  

The findings of the present research can be justified in the light of different socio-cultural 

contexts of the two groups of NEs. The noticeable contrast between the frequent use of hedging in 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
115 

the  ENEs  and  its  rarity  in  the  ANEs  can  strongly  imply  that  ANErs,  unlike  their  English 

counterparts, tend to view the world in binary terms, interpreting reality as strictly divided into 

two  absolute  scales.  The  findings  of  the  present  research  suggest  that  there  were  two  different 

socio-cultural models, which affected the use of hedging and boosting by the native ENErs and 

ANErs  in  their  texts.  As  previously  mentioned  in  chapter  one,  according  to  several  linguists, 

writing is a cultural or social form. “It is a social product whose shape and influence depend upon 

prior political  and ideological  factors” (Rabab’ah & Abu Rumman, 2015, p. 40). According to 

Connor (2005), “writing  is  increasingly regarded as being socially situated;  each situation may 

entail special consideration to audience, purposes, level of perfection, and correspondingly may 

require  varying  amounts  of  revision,  collaboration,  and  attention  to  detail”  (p.  293).  To  put  it 

differently,  the  rhetorical  patterns,  rhetorical  strategies,  and  writing  conventions  are  likely  to 

change  according  to  the  socio-cultural  contexts  where  a  piece  of  writing  occurs.  Similarly,  the 

distinct rhetorical patterns (such as hedging and boosting) observed across the two groups of NErs 

likely  stemmed  from  contrasting  ideological  frameworks  including  political,  socio-cultural 

ideologies, belief systems, or values that shaped their respective sociocultural contexts (Gee, 2012; 

Sanjaya, 2013). In Arab sociocultural contexts (ANErs), dominant belief systems may prioritize 

presenting claims or knowledge with assertive certainty, reflected in a high frequency of boosting. 

Conversely,  Anglo-American  sociocultural  contexts  often  emphasize  cautious  or  tentative 

expression of ideas, characterized by the frequent use of hedging (Sanjay, 2013).   

In other words, the findings of the present research suggest that ENErs and ANErs appeared 

to employ distinct cognitive frameworks and rhetorical strategies for processing, interpreting, and 

structuring knowledge, particularly through divergent use of hedging and boosting conventions 

(Al-Rickaby, 2016; Rabab’ah & Abu Rumman, 2015; Al-Ghoweri & Al-Kayed, 2019). However, 

the similarities between the ENEs and ANEs in terms of form and function might suggest that 

these rhetorical  and metadiscursive features are an integral part of persuasive or  argumentative 

genres such as NEs and common to all cultures or languages of the world. The differences in the 

frequency, distribution, or preferences of these features seemed to  be  determined by the  cross-

linguistic and cross-cultural peculiarities of these languages (Al-Rickaby, 2016; Rabab’ah & Abu 

Rumman’s,  2015;  &  Al-Ghoweri  &  Al-Kayed,  2019).  To  put  differently,  the  present  research 

supports the claim that different cultural norms (cultural models), values, and belief systems of 

both ENErs and ANErs are very important factors in constructing the patterns of use of hedging 

 
 
116 

and boosting. In other words, every cultural or linguistic system employs hedging and boosting 

differently  in  terms  of  distribution,  preferences,  and  frequencies  of  these  features.  To  put  it 

differently, the socio-cultural models adopted was an important factor that the ANErs preferred a 

more assertive, categorical, or direct style of writing and that the ENErs preferred a more tentative 

or indirect style of writing. This context required that the ANErs should use more assertive patterns 

of writing and that the ENErs should use more tentative patterns of writing.   

According to  several  linguists (e.g., Connor, 1996;  Sa’adeddin,  1989;  El-Arousy, 2004; 

and others), Arab writers and speakers prefer direct and assertive structures, therefore, they used a 

less  frequent  number  of  hedging  and  a  more  frequent  number  of  boosting.  However,  English 

writers  and  speakers  preferred  tentative  structures  and  tended  to  use  hedging  features  more 

frequently  and  boosting  features  less  frequently.  To  reiterate,  Connor  (1996)  and  Sa’adeddin 

(1989)  cited  a  great  deal  of  research  that  describes  Arabic  rhetorical  styles  and  structures  as 

amplified and overassertive. This means that Arabic texts are supposed to contain a less frequent 

number  of  hedging  and  more  frequent  number  of  boosting.  According  to  Wierzbicka  (1999), 

Oriental cultures or languages are more assertive than the Western ones, which the findings of the 

present research support indicating that the ANErs (as oriental writers) seemed to be more assertive 

than their English counterparts in journalistic discourse. 

Therefore,  the  use  of  these  features,  which  are  supposed  to  make  the  text  persuasive, 

comprehensible,  and  natural,  may  differ  from  one  language  to  another.  For  instance,  what  is 

expected and accepted in terms of use of metadiscursive markers such as hedging and boosting in 

a certain language may not be so in other languages. The prevalent use of the discourse marker wa 

(و/and) in Arabic, which is a linguistic feature used to maintain continuity and cohesion, is not 

merely optional but essential to achieve naturalness and coherence in written texts. However, in 

English  translations,  this  marker  is  frequently  deleted,  as  keeping  it  would  make  the  text 

stylistically or structurally unnatural (AL-Semeiri et al., 2018). Similarly, it can be said that the 

frequent use of hedging  in  English  discourse  is  linguistically  and socio-culturally very  natural, 

whereas  it  is  socio-culturally  unnatural  or  inappropriate  in  Arabic  discourse.  Moreover,  the 

differences concerning the frequency of hedging and boosting in the two corpora can be explained 

in terms of the differing lengths of NEs in both corpora. It can be said that the longer the text is 

the more likely to contain such features with a high frequency. According to Villanueva (2014), 

the number of hedging  features  used in  newspaper discourse  can be affected by the number of 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
117 

words  the  NEs  have.  However,  this  justification,  although  it  is  factual  to  some  extent,  is  not 

applicable to the present corpora. One empirical evidence that refutes this justification is that some 

shorter-text ENEs or ANEs contained much more numbers of hedging or boosting than the longer-

text ENEs or ANEs.  Moreover, the ANErs boosted their claims more frequently than the ENErs 

(10.57 PTW for ENEs & 12.95 PTW for ANEs) despite the fact that the ENEs were longer than 

the ANEs. This is clear evidence that the length of text is not the only factor that affect the use of 

two features. 

According to Mauranen (2001, p. 45), “in order to arrive at an explanation of why texts the 

way they are, it is necessary that researchers resort to social factors such as the size of the expected 

readership,  degree  of  homogeneity  of  readership,  and  cultural  characteristics  as  the  sole 

determinants of rhetorical difference observed to draw on the social contexts where they occur” 

That is to say, the rhetorical patterns of writing conventions can be mostly determined by the size 

and degree of variability of readership and the cultural characteristics of the target community. In 

other words, when the writer produces a particular text; he/she is very concerned that he satisfies 

the requirements of his/her target community. Therefore, he/she shapes his/her rhetorical structures 

of  the  text  according  to  these  requirements.  These  requirements,  which  constitute  the  cultural 

models of writers and their readers, can affect the use of such features (Gee, 2011, 2012; Sanajaya, 

2013, 2015). Similarly, Martín-Martín and Pérez (2014) argue that “in order to get their papers 

accepted,  researchers  need  to  meet  the  expectations  of  the  members  of  their  particular 

communities,  especially  those  of  the  editors  and  reviewers  of  international  English  language 

journals” (p.1). Salager-Meyer (1994) argues that the use of hedging is determined by the general 

structure  of  the  discourse  community.  In  other  words,  obeying  the  rules  of  the  discourse 

community  might  require  the  use  of  hedging  and  boosting  in  a  particular  pattern.  Similarly,  it 

seems that when the ENErs or ANErs wanted to publish their NEs in their newspapers; they should 

be able to satisfy the requirements of their target journalistic community or readership through 

obeying  the  rules  of  writing  conventions  of  this  community.  For  instance,  the  journalistic 

community of ENEs may have required that the ENErs should present their claims cautiously and 

indirectly  (i.e.  through  using  various  hedging  categories).  However,  it  seems  that  the  target 

journalistic  community  of  ANEs  required  that  ANErs  should  present  claims  or  argumentation 

more precisely or categorically (i.e. through using a more frequent number of boosting).  

The requirements of target community, which constitute an important part of the writer’s 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
118 

cultural models include: The writer/speaker’s intrapersonal factors, low or high power distance 

between the writer/speaker and the reader/listener, the speaker/writer’s degree of individualism vs. 

collectivism, and the speaker/writer’s degree of high or low uncertainty avoidance. Intrapersonal 

factor,  which  refers  to  the  writer’s  feelings,  experiences,  attitudes,  and  ideologies,  are  cultural 

models associated with the use of such rhetorical features by a particular individual in a particular 

context (Gee, 2011, 2012; Sanajaya, 2013). Intrapersonal factor include the writer’s educational 

backgrounds and ideologies. Some discourse analysts argue that the use of hedging and boosting 

can  be  affected  by  the  writers’  educational  backgrounds  and  ideologies  (Vassileva,  2001; 

Khoshsima et al., 2016), or journalistic experiences (Zarza, 2018). In other words, another factor, 

which constructs the cultural thought patterns, seems to be the background and educational system 

of  the  writer.  According  to  Khuwaileh’s  (2006,  pp.41-42),  one  important  justification  for  the 

frequent use of hedging in the English texts was that the English authors could be more experienced 

or  socio-culturally  cautious,  as  opposed  to  the  Arab  ones,  who  tended  to  present  their  claims 

assertively  or  incautiously.  Similarly,  writers  might  also  have  limited  knowledge  about  the 

common features or rhetorical structures of journalistic writing of NEs as a specific genre, or they 

are  unaware  of  the  cultural  pattern  to  use  hedging  and  boosting,  thus  they  failed  to  meet  the 

discourse community requirements. Jalilifar (2011) suggests that the differences between Iranian 

and English patterns of use of hedging might stem from the insufficient awareness of the Iranian 

writers  of  the  function  of  hedging  in  a  discourse.  Due  to  their  distinct  cultural  backgrounds 

compared to English writers, Iranian writers may develop unique interpretations of norms related 

to  formality, directness,  politeness,  and other pragmatic conventions  (Jalilifar, 2011). To put it 

differently, the rhetorical patterns of a writer can be determined by his or her previous journalistic 

experiences. For instance, ENErs with regular exposure to ENEs may adopt rhetorical strategies 

in their NEs (such as hedging and boosting use) that closely resemble those used by their peers 

(Sanjaya, 2013, 2015).  

Another important factor that constitutes a significant aspect of the cultural models of the 

writer is power distance. Power distance refers to the degree of distance between the writer and 

the reader in terms of their social statuses. For instance, the ANErs seemed in the present research 

to  see  themselves  as  more  qualified  and  powerful  than  their  readers;  and  hence  they  were  not 

concerned to build a better relationship with them (i.e. thus they used a less frequent number of 

hedging). However, the ENErs seemed to consider themselves as qualified as their readers; and 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
119 

thus they were concerned to build a strong relationship with their readers, so they tended to use 

more hedging and less boosting. Another factor constituting the cultural models of the writer is the 

factor  of  the  writer’s  individualism  and  collectivism.  Individualism  refers  the  degree  of 

individualistic freedom of the writer when he/she shapes his/her linguistic or rhetorical features in 

the  text,  whereas  collectivism  refers  to  the  degree  of  the  writer’s  obedience  of  the  writing 

conventions of his/her target community. In the present research, the ENErs seemed to be more 

individualistic in constructing their rhetorical patterns than their Arab colleagues, who seemed to 

be  more  collectivistic.  Being  individualistic,  the  ENErs  considered  that  their  claims  were 

subjective, so they were responsible for presenting these claims cautiously and tentatively in case 

these claims are true or false. However, as being more collectivistic, the ANErs considered that 

their claims objective or factual, so they were more direct and assertive in presenting their claims 

as they considered their reader as a participant in the state of knowledge. Uncertainty avoidance 

refers to how far the writer avoids being uncertain or vague, or “the extent to which the members 

of a culture feel threatened by ambiguous or unknown situations” (Sanjaya, 2013, p.191). In other 

words, in the present research, while the ENErs’ degree of uncertainty avoidance was very low, 

the ANErs’ degree of uncertainty avoidance was comparatively very high. In other words, since 

hedging is categorized as uncertainty markers, it seems that the socio-cultural prevalent beliefs or 

cultural models among the ANErs can be that hedging is a sign of one’s lack of knowledge.  

According to Alonso, Alonso, and Mariñas (2012), native speakers of Spanish language 

negatively consider that hedging is a sign of lack of knowledge or commitment to the truth-value 

of the claims presented. In other words, it seems that the ANErs viewed hedging as a sign of lack 

of  knowledge;  and  thus  they  avoided  to  use  such  feature  in  order  not  to  be  considered 

inexperienced. Moreover, according to Moghadam (2017), hedging can “weaken the expression”, 

and thus, they are often perceived as undermining an argument rather than serving as a tool to 

reinforce it. According to Moghadam (2017), this can be one reason why the Iranian journalists 

did  not  use  hedging  features  in  their  texts  as  frequently  as  Anglo-American  ones.  Moghadam 

(2017) states that the Iranian journalists probably did not view expressions such as perhaps, maybe, 

likely, and others as “constructive to their persuasive goals.” To put it differently, the differences 

between the two groups of ENErs and ANErs can be justified as the ANErs might have negative 

perception  towards  imprecision  or  uncertainty  (i.e.  use  of  hedging),  whereas  the  ENErs  had  a 

positive perception towards uncertainty. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
120 

All these aspects are a part of cultural models or belief systems proposed by Gee (2011, 

2012)  which  are  determined  by  the  socio-cultural  circumstances  of  the  writer  (Sanjaya,  2013, 

2015). In other words, the fact that the ANErs were more assertive than the ENErs can thus be 

attributed  to  the  different  cultural  models  followed  by  the  two  groups  of  NErs.  To  reiterate, 

cultural models are “informal belief systems held by an individual used to help the individual to 

carry  on  the  business  of  his  or  her  living”  (Gee,  2012,  p.43,  as  cited  in  Sanjaya,  2013).  Belief 

system is  supposed to  play  an important  role in  constructing the individual language use (Gee, 

2011; Sanjaya, 2013, 2015). In the Arab context, the ANEs adopted an assertive or authoritative 

tone, reflecting the writer’s unquestionable authority that readers were expected to accept without 

challenge. In contrast, the ENErs in this research did not perceive themselves as holding absolute 

textual authority, leading them to diverge from the cultural norms observed in the ANEs. While 

the ENErs were  recognized as experts within their journalistic communities, their role differed 

markedly  from  that  of  ANErs,  who  were  regarded  as  ultimate  authorities.  Consequently,  the 

ANErs articulated claims with greater certainty, employing a more frequent number of boosting 

features and a less frequent number of hedging features. This implies that the ANErs, positioned 

as  authoritative  knowledge  sources  in  Arab  journalism,  presented  their  assertions,  as 

incontrovertible truths not open to reader’s rejection (cf. Sanjaya, 2015; Al-Rickaby, 2016). 

Similarly, Jalilifar (2011) states that the PNErs’ direct structures and style in Farsi language 

as their texts contained a frequent number of emphatics (i.e. boosting features) can be attributed to 

Iranian convention of valuing and respecting the rules of those in power without questioning them 

or without refuting their claims. Moreover, Jalilifar (2011) attributes the ENErs’ frequent use of 

hedging to their being more considerate and polite to their readers. Moreover, Bonyadi et al. (2012) 

reports that academic English writers used more hedging features and fewer boosting features than 

their Iranian counterparts; and according to these researchers, English writers were more tactful 

and cautious in presenting their claims; so they tended to interact with their readers indirectly and 

politely. However, the Iranian writers seemed to be less conservative and instead addressed their 

readers directly (Bonyadi et al., 2012). Mashhady, Fatollahi, & Shahraki (2015), in their research, 

attribute the frequent use of hedging by ENErs to the fact that the ENErs were more conservative 

and respectful to their readers than their Persian counterparts. Moreover, according to Al-Ghoweri 

and Al-Kayed’s (2019) findings, the ENErs seemed to be very concerned about the interpersonal 

reader-writer interaction performed by the use of hedging. The ANErs, in contrast with the ENErs, 

 
 
 
121 

tended  to  use  more  boosters  or  emphatics  such  as  ṫab’an  (certainly/  اعبط),  bi-tā’kīd  (definitely/ 

ديكأتلاب), and others in their texts in order to present their claims and views concerning a particular 

issue (Al-Ghoweri and Al-Kayed’s, 2019). This is similar to Noorian and Biria’s (2010) findings, 

who report that while the Persian writers preferred the use of emphatics in their texts, the American 

English  writers  tended  frequently  to  use  more  hedges.  According  to  Yang  (2013),  the  use  of 

hedging by Chinese scholars was considerably affected by Chinese national culture (i.e. Chinese 

cultural models), which is supported by findings of the present research. 

Moreover,  why  did  the  ENErs  use  19  categories  and  143  subcategories  of  hedging  to 

mitigate their claims? Why did the ENErs use 10 categories and 66 subcategories to boost their 

claims? Why did the ANErs use 20 categories and 126 subcategories of hedging to mitigate their 

propositions or claims? Why did the ANErs used 7 categories and 43 subcategories of boosting to 

boost their claims. Why did the ENErs prefer to use certain categories more than other and why 

did the ANErs use certain categories more than other? It seems that both the ENErs and ANErs’ 

rhetorical strategies or use of hedging and boosting were constrained by the structural limitations 

and properties of languages within which they write. It seems that the ENErs and ANErs’ usage 

of subcategories of hedging and boosting in the present research was constrained by the availability 

of linguistic choices in their languages (Khoshsima et al., 2016; Aldhulaee, 2011). Berg (2009) 

categorizes  structural-linguistic  variations  between  Arabic  and  English  into  two  types.  The 

first, qualitative  variation,  describes  structural  elements  (e.g.,  morphemes,  words,  or  syntactic 

patterns)  present  in  one  language  but  entirely  absent  in  the  other.  The  second, quantitative 

variation, refers to differences in the frequency or prominence of shared structural features across 

the two languages. These variations critically shape the divergences between English and Arabic, 

serving as defining characteristics of their respective linguistic systems. For instance, while non-

existent in the English linguistic system, qad structure (might/دق) occurred very frequently in the 

Arabic discourse as a hedging feature. This might justify why the ANErs used different categories 

and  subcategories  of  hedging  and  boosting  or  why  the  ENErs  employed  significantly  more 

subcategories of hedging than the ANErs. Obviously, it seems that the ENErs had more lexico-

grammatical, structural, and linguistic choices to use more various subcategories of hedging and 

boosting;  whereas  the  ANErs  had  less  lexico-grammatical  options;  and  thus  they  hedged  or 

boosted their propositions with a comparatively less various number of subcategories of such two 

features. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
122 

The  two  groups  of  NErs,  as  shown  in  the  findings,  to  some  extent,  used  different 

subcategories  of  hedging  and  boosting,  since  each  language  has  its  own  linguistic  system  and 

structures. As previously mentioned, the linguistic system of Arabic language is quite different 

from the English linguistic system as they belong to two different linguistic families, therefore, we 

can find many hedging and boosting features and structures exist in Arabic language but do not 

exist in English language or vice versa. For instance, the Arabic linguistic system does not have a 

modal verbal system equivalent to the English one; and similarly, the English linguistic system 

does not have hedging features such as qad structure (may/would/دق), similar- to-verb particles 

(seem  as  if/لعل/  نأك),  and  rubbama  particle  (may/maybe/ام بر).  Research  suggests  that  Arabic 

speakers or writers adapt to the lack of a modal verb system by relying on particles and lexico-

grammatical  strategies,  whereas  English  speakers  use  modal  verb  subcategories  to  address 

structural  gaps.  Arabic,  similar  to  Persian  but  distinct  from  English,  has  fewer  modal  verb 

subcategories,  as  noted  by  Tahririan  and  Shahzamani  (2009).  In  contrast,  English’s  complex 

system  of  modal  verb  subcategories  (such  as could, might,  or must)  offers  the  ENErs  greater 

flexibility in hedging or boosting assertions (AL-Semeiri et al., 2018). 

Additionally,  why  did  both  the  ENErs  and  ANErs  employ  similar  seventeen  major 

categories of hedging and seven major categories of boosting? Similarly, why was it supposed that 

the different categories of hedging and boosting functioned similarly in both corpora? This can be 

justified  in  the  light  of  linguistic  universals.  A linguistic  universal is  a  pattern  that  occurs 

systematically  across natural  languages  (Song,  2011). For  instance,  all  languages  have nouns 

and verbs, and thus in the present research, both the ENErs and ANErs shared a similar pattern of 

using major categories of hedging and boosting such as modal verbs, agentless passives, rhetorical 

questions, conditional clauses, and others. In fact, the similarities between the ENErs and ANErs 

in terms of using major categories of such features can be also justified in light of similar generic 

writing conventions of NEs between the two groups of NErs. In other words, although there were 

differences  between  the  ENErs  and  ANErs  in  terms  of  usage  of  such  features,  there  were  also 

similarities,  which  are  explained  in  terms  of  the  similar  linguistic  universals  and  generic 

conventions  prevailing  similarly  in  the  two  languages  or  discourse  communities.  Generic 

conventions,  which  are  the  rhetorical  structures  and  moves  common  in  a  particular  discourse 

community, seemed to be relatively similar in English and Arabic newspaper discourse in terms 

of using such features.      

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
123 

Then, what are the reasons of differences or preferences in terms of usage of hedging and 

boosting  between  the  ENErs  and  ANErs?  One  major  reason  of  the  similarities  and  differences 

between the ENErs and ANErs in terms of usage of such two features is the pattern of cultural 

models,  which  each  group  of  NErs  adopted  in  their  socio-cultural  contexts  to  achieve  various 

rhetorical,  persuasive,  social,  or  ideological  purposes.  Accordingly,  the  cultural  models,  which 

constitute  various  aspects  of  socio-cultural  belief  systems  such  as  the  size  and  degree  of 

homogeneity of readership, the cultural characteristics  of the target  community, linguistic and 

lexico-grammatical properties of language, the writer/reader’s power distance, the writer’s degree 

of  individualism  vs.  collectivism,  the  writer’s  degree  of  uncertainty  avoidance,  intrapersonal 

factor, generic conventions, and educational backgrounds or journalistic experiences, seemed to 

play an integral role in constructing rhetorical patterns of NEs in the present research specifically 

the use of  hedging and boosting features.  

4.6 Summary              

In this chapter, the descriptive, inferential, and qualitative findings of the present research 

were  presented  and  compared  with  other  researchers’  findings.  Moreover,  the  most  important 

socio-cultural, rhetorical, and metadiscursive functions of hedging and boosting were identified. 

The  factors  determining  the  findings  of  the  present  research  were  also  provided.  One  such 

important factor was the cultural models, which may constitute various aspects of socio-cultural 

belief systems. Belief systems or cultural models seemed to play an integral role in constructing 

the rhetorical patterns of NEs in the present research. Next chapter presents the conclusion, which 

includes  the  limitations,  the  recommendations,  the  contribution,  and  implications  of  present 

research.              

 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
                
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
124 

Conclusion 

  After presenting and discussing the findings of this research, this chapter aims to provide 

some  limitations  the  research  encountered  and  recommendations  that  provide  some  pieces  of 

advice for future researchers who are interested to conduct research in this area. Moreover, this 

chapter  provides  some  theoretical  and  empirical  implications  for  the  present  findings,  and  it 

concludes with a summary of the most important points the present research tackled. Finally, a list 

of references and appendixes were also provided in the last section of this research.  

5.1 Limitations  

It  can  be  said  that  this  research  achieved  its  main  objectives,  and  it  answered  its  main 

research  questions  clearly  and  sufficiently.  Although,  the  findings  of  present  research  provide 

some significant implications and recommendations for other researchers; and the current corpora, 

can be said, were representative of the genre of NEs, the research faced certain limitations that 

rendered the findings, to a particular extent, inconclusive. First, this research was limited to only 

40 NEs (20 ENEs and 20 ANEs); hence, this amount of data was relatively small or not enough to 

generalize  the  results  to  other  ENEs  or  ANEs  regarding  the  use  of  hedging  and  boosting. 

Additionally,  as  initially  proposed,  the  use  of  hedging  and  boosting  in  discourse  may  also  be 

influenced by other significant factors, such as the writers' age, their level of proficiency, years of 

experience,  and  their  gender  and  nativeness,  which  the  present  research  did  not  tackle  due  to 

feasibility  issues.  For  instance,  some  ENEs  or  ANEs  were  unsigned  by  their  editors;  so  it  was 

impossible to control the factor of their editors’ nativeness (i.e. whether this editor was a native 

speaker  of  English  or  not).  Likewise,  in  the  present  research,  the  rhetorical  sections  or  moves 

within  each  ENE  or  ANE  were  not  examined,  which  may  be  a  relevant  factor  influencing  the 

research findings. 

 Another limitation in the present research was the coding and identification of linguistic 

realizations  of hedging and boosting, which were done by the researcher alone,  because it was 

difficult for the researcher to find experienced English and Arab native-speaker volunteers in order 

to do the coding or identification job in the two corpora. In other words, in the present research, 

the researcher was not able to achieve the inter-rater reliability as there were not experts of English 

and Arabic available in order to compare the researcher's results with those of an independent rater. 

 
 
                  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
125 

In other words, the validity of a research's statistical analysis or data coding is partly dependent on 

the  reliability  of  the  coding  system  applied,  which  is  typically  measured  through  inter-rater 

reliability test. Moreover, it was not a simple task to decide whether a certain linguistic feature 

should be considered as a hedging feature or not or a boosting feature or not. In other words, during 

the data analysis, the researcher was not completely certain about whether a particular linguistic 

feature  should  be  classified  as  a  hedge  or  a  booster.  The  determination  of  whether  a  linguistic 

expression was a hedging or boosting  feature or  not  was based on the semantic and pragmatic 

meaning, which this expression had in a certain context. For example, the English modal verbs 

will and must can serve as either hedging or boosting features depending on the context. It was 

challenging to decide whether these modals expressed capability or probability (i.e., whether they 

functioned as hedges, boosters, or neither), as they can serve multiple functions in a given context. 

In fact, this methodological issue (i.e., determining whether a feature is a hedge or a booster) was 

faced  by  all  researchers  (Crompton,  1997).  Moreover,  applying  a  careful  manual  analysis  and 

reading of each NE were very exhausting and time-consuming. Therefore, some expressions might 

have escaped the researcher’s attention and some others would wrongly have been considered as 

hedging or boosting features. Accordingly, in the present research, if it was possible to involve at 

least one native speaker for each language or an assistant researcher to help the researcher in the 

coding process, the findings and conclusions derived from these findings might differ from those 

presented in the current research. 

Another issue encountered in this research was the taxonomy of hedging and boosting used, 

which could also influence the findings. As Hyland (2017) points out, any taxonomy is inherently 

problematic because it is impossible to create an exhaustive classification of all the features present 

in a language. The various taxonomies of hedging and boosting proposed by different researchers, 

therefore, can make any attempt to provide a classification of such feature inherently problematic. 

In  other  words,  Fraser’s  (2010)  taxonomy  of  English  hedging,  which  the  present  research  was 

based on, was relatively inapplicable (specifically for the ANEs). Identifying the two features in 

the ANEs depending upon Fraser’s (2010) taxonomies of English hedging was a difficult and time-

consuming process. In other words, one challenge the research faced was applying an analytical 

model originally developed for English data to the Arabic corpus, since there was limited research 

on hedging and boosting in Arabic. 

5.2 Recommendations   

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
                                  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
126 

Considering the findings and limitations of the present research, further research needs to 

be  conducted  to  investigate  the  validity  of  such  insights  and  findings  and  overcome  these 

limitations. In the present research, there was one main factor that affected the use of hedging and 

boosting features namely the cultural model factor that each group of NErs adopted within their 

socio-cultural contexts. It can be said this explanation was inconclusive, and thus further research 

should  be  conducted  to  validate  it.  Moreover,  although  hedging  and  boosting  are  important 

rhetorical,  communicative,  and  metadiscursive  features  that  have  been  studied  across  different 

types of discourse, languages, and genres (Clyne, 1991; Crismore et al., 1993; Vassileva, 2001; 

Hyland,  2005a,  2005b;  Salager-Meyer,  1994,  2011),  research  on  hedging  and  boosting  in 

newspaper  discourse  is  relatively  limited.  To  the  best  of  the  researcher’s  knowledge,  there  has 

been no research that investigated hedging and boosting in ANEs except for Al-Ghoweri and Al-

Kayed (2019) (cf. Abbadi, 2014); and very few studies have been conducted to explore the two 

features  in  English  newspaper  discourse  and  newspaper  discourse  in  other  languages.  More 

research needs to be conducted to investigate such two features in Arabic newspaper discourse and 

specifically in ANEs. Similarly, given the limited scope of the current research’s corpus, future 

research should expand the size of the corpus in comparative or contrastive analyses to examine 

the occurrence of these features in ENEs and ANEs and determine whether comparable results 

emerge. Furthermore, since the present research is restricted to analyze hedging and boosting in 

only NEs, future researchers might also conduct research to analyze the use of such features in 

other genres of newspaper discourse such as advertisements, news reports, business letters, and so 

on. 

Similarly, future research needs to be done to compare not only two sets of languages with 

different  sociocultural  characteristics  but  also  more  sets  of  languages  with  different  or  similar 

characteristics. For instance, future researchers, in assistance with other native researchers in these 

languages,  can  include  such  languages  as  Arabic  and  Hebrew  vs.  German  and  English  in  a 

comparative analysis of hedging and boosting in NEs although this task is difficult to do. Similarly, 

the  current  research  employed  methodologies  of  corpus  linguistics  to  address  the  research 

questions. However, as previously highlighted, the findings in the two corpora and conclusions 

derived from them were inconclusive. Therefore, it will be more beneficial if this corpus-based 

quantitative analysis is supported by the data obtained from qualitative analysis such as interviews 

or questionnaires to investigate the validity of these hypotheses. In other words, future research 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
127 

should investigate to what degree these differences in terms of use of hedging and boosting have 

effects on readers. For instance, instead of claiming that hedging functions to mitigate statements 

or indicate imprecision; future researchers might identify the various meanings or functions of the 

feature depending the readers’ perceptions of these meanings such as to avoid responsibility, to 

affect readers’ views, and so on. 

Future research should investigate additional socio-cultural and pragmatic dimensions of 

hedging  and  boosting,  as  well  as  conduct  comparative  analyses  of  their  functional  variations 

between ENEs and ANEs. Moreover, as mentioned above, the usage of hedging and boosting in a 

discourse can be determined by other important factors such as the age of the writers, their level 

of proficiency, years of experience, and gender, which the present research did not tackle due to 

availability  or  feasibility  issues.  Accordingly,  other  researchers  are  advised  to  conduct  further 

research while considering such factors. Similarly, the present research investigated two types of 

interactional metadiscourse namely hedging and boosting in NEs; further research  can analyze 

other  interactional  metadiscursive  features  such  as  attitude  and  engagement  markers.  Future 

research can also examine how ENErs and ANErs employ hedging and boosting across distinct 

rhetorical sections (e.g., introductions, problem statements, methodologies, or discussions) of their 

NEs, analyzing functional differences within these structural components.                                                     

5.3 Contributions and Implications   

As  has  been  mentioned  earlier,  to  the  best  of  the  researcher’s  knowledge,  the  present 

research has been the second one that investigated hedging and boosting features in the genre of 

ANEs (the first was Al-Ghoweri & Al-Kayed, 2019). Most previous research conducted in this 

area has compared and focused on the use of such features in English and other languages such as 

English,  Albanian, and Italian (Dheskali, 2017) and English vs. Malaysian (Zarza, 2018). Some 

research analyzed the use of the two features in other genres or discourses such as RAs, theses and 

dissertations, and so on (e.g., Vassileva, 2001; Hyland, 2005a, 2005b). Moreover, limited research 

has shown that these features played an important role in achieving persuasion or strengthening 

argumentation in newspaper discourse (Zarza, 2018; Amirrostami, 2019).   

Furthermore, the findings  of the present  research  have both  theoretical  and  pedagogical 

implications: Theoretical implications can be useful in modifying the existing models of pattern 

of use of such features in ENEs and ANEs. The present findings can extend our knowledge and 

understanding of the metadiscursive, rhetorical, argumentative and persuasive properties of such 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
128 

features in both English and Arabic languages specifically in newspaper discourse. The findings 

can also add to the existing literature of comparative linguistics and contrastive rhetoric and show 

how such rhetorical features are used differently in different languages, discourses, and genres. 

The  pedagogical  insights  from  the  findings  of  the  current  research  could  enhance  practices  in 

curriculum  design,  instructional  material  development,  and  specialized  language  training 

programs, such as English for Specific/Academic Purposes (ESP/EAP) and Arabic for Academic 

Purposes (AAP). The findings may increase the awareness of press and media students, translators, 

and instructors of the socio-cultural, rhetorical, communicative, and metadiscursive functions and 

characteristics of such features in ENEs or ANEs. Moreover, the pedagogical implications of the 

findings, which showed that the ENErs were more tentative or cautious than the ANErs, can be 

very implicational. That is to say, ESP or EAP instructors in the Arab world should raise students’ 

awareness of the fact that the Arabic newspaper discourse is quite different from English one in 

terms of patterns of use of these rhetorical features.   

The findings of the research can also be beneficial to professional or beginner translators, 

instructors, and students in translation courses, as they clearly showed how two languages differed 

rhetorically  in  terms  of  use  of  such  features.  For  instance,  when  Arab  translators,  researchers, 

readers, journalists, or students read English newspapers, they should take these cross-linguistic 

or cross-cultural differences into consideration and avoid unjustifiable or strong assertions when 

they write in English (i.e. boost their claims)(Sultan, 2011). Similarly, when English translators, 

researchers, readers, or journalists read Arabic newspapers or any discourse, they should take in 

account  the assertive nature of the Arabic discourse (Jabbar, 2019).  John Swales and Christine 

Feak (2012) argue that there are “pedagogical values in sensitizing students to the rhetorical effects 

and the rhetorical structures that tend to recur in genre-specific texts” (p.15). The findings of the 

present research can offer valuable guidance for cross-cultural translators, EFL/AFL learners, and 

EAP/AAP  instructors  by  demonstrating  how  ENErs  and  ANErs  employ  hedging  and  boosting 

features, structure claims, and employ persuasive techniques within their written discourse. Thus, 

in  light  of  the  present  findings,  ESP  or  ASP  students  can  be  able  to  write  persuasive  and 

argumentative NEs that are appropriately and effectively hedged or boosted. Teachers, students, 

novice  readers,  and  writers  need  to  take  into  account  how  such  factors  as  genre,  discourse 

community, cultural values, belief norms, cultural models, and writing conventions of ENErs and 

ANErs play an important role in the use and distribution of these features in a particular discourse. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
129 

5.4 Summary   

Hedging  and  boosting,  as  two  rhetorical,  communicative,  and  metadiscursive  features, 

have been claimed to play an effective persuasive and argumentative role in the genre of NEs. The 

purpose of this contrastive corpus-based research was to investigate the usage of such features in 

ENEs and ANEs in their two different socio-cultural contexts. It aimed to explore how ENErs and 

ANErs structured their hedging and boosting features in their texts in the two languages and the 

effect of socio-cultural contexts on the realization of these features. To achieve this purpose, 40 

NEs (20 ENEs and 20 ANEs) were randomly selected and downloaded from the electronic version 

of influential, national, and international Anglo-American and Arabic newspapers available on the 

internet  and  were  analyzed  in  terms  of  their  usage  of  such  two  features.  The  raw  and  mean 

frequency of each category or subcategory of hedging and boosting in each language and were 

quantified and classified based upon Gee’s theoretical cultural model of linguistic features (1999, 

2008,  2011,  2012),  Fraser’s  (2010)  taxonomy,  and  Hyland’s  (1996)  definition  of  hedging  and 

Hyland’s  (2005b)  definition  of  boosting.  In  the  present  research,  there  was  one  independent 

variable and one dependent variable. The independent variable was the linguistic or sociocultural 

context where the NEs were written, and the dependent variable was the use of such two features. 

The findings of the present research revealed that, in the English and Arabic corpora, there were 

some similarities and differences in terms of use and occurrence of hedging and boosting. The 

similarities in terms of form and function was justified that these rhetorical and metadiscursive 

resources were an integral part of persuasive or argumentative genres such as NEs and common to 

all languages of the world. However, differences in the distribution or preference of these features 

were  explained  in  terms  of  cross-linguistic,  cross-cultural,  and  pragmatic  peculiarities  of  these 

languages (Al-Rickaby, 2016).  

The findings of descriptive statistics showed the ENErs used totally 768 hedging features, 

while the ANErs used 479 hedging features. On average, the ENErs used 48.94 tokens of hedging 

PTW,  whereas  the  ANErs  used  34.08  tokens  of  the  feature  PTW.  Apparently,  these  findings 

demonstrated that hedging was used more frequently in the ENEs compared to the ANEs. These 

findings  were  similar  to  Al-Ghoweri  and  Al-Kayed’s  (2019)  findings  of  the  use  of  hedging  in 

ENEs  and  ANEs.  These  researchers  found  that  the  ENEs  were  more  heavily  hedged  than  the 

Arabic ones. In fact, the ENErs, in the present research, hedged their propositions approximately 

twice more often than their Arab counterparts. The ENErs were more tentative or cautious when 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
130 

they presented their opinions or facts in their texts than the ANErs. Moreover, the ENErs were 

very concerned about the reader-writer interaction than their Arab counterparts; so they tended to 

use more hedging features in their texts than the ANErs to establish a strong relationship with their 

readers.  The  overall  ENEs  and  ANEs  contained  1247  instances  of  hedging  occurring  in  41.92 

tokens PTW in the whole two corpora. Moreover, the most frequent category of hedging in the 

English corpus was modal verbs recording 4.39 tokens PTW, whereas the most frequent category 

in the Arabic corpus was epistemic lexical verbs recording tokens 6.12 PTW. Similarly, the least 

category  of hedging used in  the English corpus  was modal nouns recording 0.25 tokens PTW, 

while the least frequent category in the Arabic corpus was metalinguistic comments recording 0.14 

tokens PTW. Moreover, the ENErs used totally 169 tokens of boosting. On average, the ENEs 

contained 10.57 tokens of boosting PTW. However, the ANErs used 182 tokens of boosting, i.e. 

ANEs  contained  12.95  tokens  of  boosting  PTW.  In  other  words,  the  ANErs  boosted  their 

propositions more often than their English counterparts. Moreover, the most frequent category of 

boosting in the English corpus was modal verbs recording 4.39 tokens PTW, whereas the most 

frequent  category  of  boosting  in  the  Arabic  corpus  was  adverbs  of  Degree,  time,  frequency 

recording 4.34 tokens PTW. Similarly, the least category of boosting used in the English corpus 

was metalinguistic comments recording 0.19 tokens PTW, while the least frequent category in the 

Arabic corpus was indefinite /impersonal pronouns recording 0.49 tokens PTW. The overall ENEs 

and  ANEs  contained  351  tokens  of  boosting  recording  11.80  tokens  PTW  in  the  whole  two 

corpora. 

Similarly, the ENErs used 19 categories and 143 subcategories of hedging to mitigate their 

claims.  However,  the  ENErs  used  10  categories  and  66  subcategories  to  boost  their  claims. 

Furthermore, the ANErs used 20 categories  and  126 subcategories  of hedging to  mitigate their 

propositions or claims. Moreover, the ANErs used 7 categories and 43 subcategories of boosting 

to boost their claims. These findings indicated that the ENErs showed greater variability in terms 

of usage of hedging and boosting by using more varied subcategories of those two features than 

their Arab colleagues. This was similar to Al-Ghoweri & Al-Kayed’s (2019) findings, who found 

that the ENErs hedged their texts more heavily and with a variety of features than the ANErs did, 

who used a more frequent number of boosting. As observed earlier, the NErs with different socio-

cultural backgrounds preferred to use several different lexico-grammatical categories of hedging 

and boosting in their texts. In the English corpus, the six most frequently used hedging categories 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
131 

were modal verbs (RF = 149, MF = 9.50), agentless passives (RF = 106, MF =6.76), adverbs of 

degree, time, or frequency (RF = 88, MF =5.61), quantifiers/determiners (RF =86, MF = 5.48), 

reference  structures  (RF  =  70,  MF  =4.46),  and  epistemic  lexical  verbs  (RF  =  65,  MF  =4.14) 

occurring  respectively.  In  the  English  corpus,  the  six most  frequently  used  boosting  categories 

were modal verbs (RF=69, MF=4.40), adverbs of degree, time, and frequency (RF=49, MF= 3.12), 

quantifiers/ determiners (RF= 15, MF= 0.96), adjectives (RF= 9, MF= 0.57), indefinite/impersonal 

pronouns (RF= 7, MF= 0.45), epistemic lexical verbs (RF= 5, MF= 0.32) occurring respectively. 

However, the rates and distribution of categories and subcategories of such features in the Arabic 

corpus were different from the English one. To put it differently, the six most frequent categories 

of hedging in the Arabic corpus were epistemic lexical verbs (RF = 86, MF = 6.12), adverbs of 

degree, time, or frequency (RF = 70, MF = 4.98), reference structures (RF = 56 , MF = 3.99), 

modal verbs (RF = 48, MF = 3), conditional clauses (RF = 29 , MF =2.06), and rhetorical questions 

(RF =29, MF = 2.06)  occurring respectively. The six most frequent categories of boosting in the 

Arabic  corpus  were  adverbs  of  degree,  time,  or  frequency  (RF  =  61,  MF  =4.34),  modal  verbs 

(RF=59, MF=4.20), adjectives (RF= 27, MF=1.92), quantifiers/determiners (RF=12, MF=0.85), 

epistemic lexical verbs (RF =8, MF= 0.57), and nouns (RF=8, MF=0.57) occurring respectively.

The findings also showed that adjectives, and compound hedging as major categories of 

hedging in the English corpus, were not observed in the Arabic corpus altogether. Similarly, as 

prominent categories of hedging in the Arabic corpus, qad structure, similar-to-verb particles, and 

rubbama particle were non-existent categories in the English corpus. Similarly, while introductory 

phrases/verbs and metalinguistic comments exist as major categories of boosting in the English 

corpus, they were not observed in the Arabic one. Through comparing the use of hedging in the 

two subcorpora of NEs (ENEs & ANEs), it was observed that there were comparatively significant 

differences  in  terms of use of such  features. To  put  it simply,  the  ENErs used 48.94 tokens of 

hedging PTW, whereas ANErs used 34.08 tokens PTW. This suggested that ENErs, in the present 

research were more tentative and cautious when they presented their claims or state of knowledge 

than their Arab counterparts. However, there were no relatively significant differences between 

ENErs and ANErs in terms of use of boosting, i.e. the ENErs used 10.56 tokens of boosting and 

the ANErs used 12.95 tokens of the feature. This suggested that both groups of NErs followed a 

similar cultural model or cultural norms when they used boosting, whereas their cultural model or 

behavior differed when they used hedging (Gee, 2011, 2012). The findings of qualitative analysis 

 
 
132 

of functions of hedging demonstrated that both the ENErs and ANErs employed these features to 

accomplish several pragmatic, rhetorical, and metadiscursive purposes in the present two corpora. 

The functional analysis of hedging categories showed that the ENErs and ANErs utilized different 

categories of hedging to modify their claims, indicate imprecision, avoid categorical commitment 

or complete responsibility, achieve politeness, and indicate modesty. Similarly, both  the ENErs 

and ANErs seemed to use hedging to establish journalistic reputation, to avoid direct criticism, to 

interact with the reader, to present their claims as opinions rather than facts, and finally, to develop 

argumentation  and  achieve  persuasion.  Moreover,  both  the  ENErs  and  ANErs  seemed  to  use 

boosting to develop argumentation and achieving persuasion, to establish authority and power, to 

engage the reader, to establish conviction, and to obey cultural norms or models.    

The differences in the distribution or preferences of these features were attributed to the 

cross-linguistic, cross-cultural, and pragmatic peculiarities of these languages (Al-Rickaby, 2016). 

In other words, these differences regarding the use of hedging and boosting between the ENErs 

and ANErs, which were observed in the findings, were explained in terms of difference of cultural 

models that each groups adopted in their linguistic or socio-cultural contexts. To put it differently, 

according to several scholars (e.g., Gee, 2012; Sanjaya, 2015), the rhetorical patterns, rhetorical 

features, and writing conventions were likely to change according to the change of cultural models 

determined by socio-cultural context where a piece of writing occurred. It was also suggested that 

the  pattern  of  use  of  hedging  and  boosting  by  the  ENErs  and  ANErs  was  determined  by  the 

availability  of  linguistic  choices  in  their  languages  (Al-Rickaby,  2016;  Rabab'ah  &  Abu 

Rumman’s, 2015; & Al-Ghoweri & Al-Kaye, 2019). However, the similarities in terms of form 

and function were justified as these rhetorical and metadiscursive features were an integral part of 

persuasive or argumentative genres such as NEs and common to all cultures or languages of the 

world (Al-Rickaby, 2016; Rabab'ah & Abu Rumman, 2015; & Al-Ghoweri & Al-Kayed, 2019). 

In other words, these similarities were justified in light of similar linguistic universals and generic 

conventions, which were supposed to be similar in the two languages (Gee, 2012; Song, 2011). 

According  to  other  views  (Gee,  2012;  Sanjaya,  2015),  cultural  models  affecting  the 

rhetorical patterns of writing conventions could be determined by the size and degree of variability 

of readership, the cultural characteristics of the target  community including low or high power 

distance between the writer and the reader, the writer’s degree of individualism vs. collectivism, 

the writer’s degree of high or low uncertainty avoidance, and the intrapersonal factor. These socio-

 
 
 
 
133 

cultural characteristics were very likely to affect the cultural models or belief systems, which each 

group  of  NErs  adopted  in  the  present  research.  Accordingly,  the  cultural  models,  which  may 

constitute  various  aspects  of  socio-cultural  belief  systems  such  as  the  size  and  degree  of 

homogeneity  of  readership,  the  cultural  characteristics  of  the  target  community,  linguistic  and 

lexico-grammatical properties of language, power distance between readers and writers, and so on, 

seemed  to  play  an  integral  role  in  constructing  the  rhetorical  patterns  of  NEs  in  the  present 

research.   

Finally,  the  present  research  faced  some  limitations,  which  made  the  present  findings 

inconclusive.  One  such  limitation  was  the  relatively  small  size  of  the  data.  Moreover,  due  to 

feasibility issues, the present research did not consider such important factors as the age of the 

writers, their level of proficiency, years of experience, and gender, which were supposed to affect 

the usage of hedging and boosting in the present two corpora. Similarly, in the present research, 

the rhetorical sections or moves of each ENE or ANE were not investigated, thus this factor can 

have  been  important  in  determining  the  findings  of  present  research.  Another  limitation  in  the 

present  research  was  the  coding  and  identification  of  linguistic  realizations  of  hedging  and 

boosting  which  the  researcher  did  alone.  One  more  limitation  was  the  difficulty  the  researcher 

faced in determining whether a certain linguistic features should be considered as a hedging or 

boosting or not. Another problem the current research  faced was the taxonomy of hedging and 

boosting adopted in this research, which might have some effects on the findings.  

Considering  the  findings  and  limitations  of  the  present  research,  it  was  suggested  that 

further research should be conducted to overcome these limitations and to check the validity of 

such  insights  and  findings.  Future  research  was  strongly  suggested  to  analyze  a  larger  corpus 

containing more numbers of NEs, to include more than two languages in the analysis, to analyze 

or compare the use of hedging and boosting in other genres of newspaper discourse, and to explore 

more socio-cultural functions of such features in both ENEs and ANEs. It was also suggested that 

future  researchers  should  also  consider  such  factors  as  the  age  of  the  writers,  their  level  of 

proficiency, years of journalistic experiences, and their gender. Moreover, it was shown that the 

findings of the present research had some theoretical and pedagogical implications. The present 

findings were argued to extend our knowledge and understanding of the metadiscursive, rhetorical, 

persuasive,  and  argumentative  properties  of  hedging  and  boosting  in  both  English  and  Arabic 

languages specifically in newspaper discourse. It was also suggested that the findings could add to 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
134 

the existing literature of comparative linguistics and contrastive rhetoric that rhetorical  features 

such as hedging and boosting were used differently in different discourses, languages, and genres. 

The present findings also suggested that its pedagogical implications contribute to such areas as 

designing syllabi, materials development, and ESP, EAP, or AAP instruction. The present findings 

were said to increase the awareness of press and media students, translators, and instructors about 

the socio-cultural, rhetorical, communicative, and metadiscursive functions and characteristics of 

hedging and boosting in newspaper discourse in general and in ENEs or ANEs in particular. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
135

linguistic strategies employed in editorials. Argumentum, 10, 724-746.

July 1/ 2021 
The Editorial Board 
USA TODAY  
This July Fourth, America will leave Afghanistan independence in its death throes                          With predictions  of  a  Taliban  victory within  months 
of an American exit from Afghanistan, White House critics are already declaring that Joe Biden will own the result. 
That isn't fair, of course, since the war was never decisively waged by four successive presidents, the Kabul regime was chronically corrupt and the American public 
has long since lost patience with 20 years of spent blood and treasure.  
But it isn't entirely wrong, either. 
Exit President Donald Trump 
In Biden's race for the exit, any chance of pressuring opposing factions into a peace settlement was forfeited.  
The Trump administration last year brought the Taliban to the table to begin negotiating an end to the war. And at first it looked promising. 
There was a seven-day reduction of violence (not quite a cease-fire). An agreement between the United States and Taliban that included an initial U.S. reduction in 
forces from 13,000 to 8,600, a Taliban promise to renounce terror organizations operating on Afghan soil, and initiation of final peace talks with the Kabul government. 
That last provision was key. 
But as months passed without any serious effort by Taliban leaders to talk peace with the democratically elected government of President Ashraf Ghani, President 
Donald Trump gave away leverage by continuing to withdraw U.S. troops and negotiating a May 1 exit deadline with the Taliban. He wanted to boast in an election 
year that he was "bringing our soldiers back home." 6 
Enter President Joe Biden 
Meanwhile, Taliban attacks across the country mounted. By the time Biden took office, U.S. troop levels were down to 2,500, supplemented by 7,000 NATO soldiers, 
devoted not to ground combat but to training Afghan forces, providing air support for Afghan operations or conducting counterterrorism missions. 
U.S. casualties were nonexistent. Biden might have slowed down the exodus of troops and made further drawdowns contingent on the Taliban at least making a good 
faith effort to reach a peace deal with the Kabul government.  
Instead, Biden did the opposite, announcing in April that all troops would leave. And soon. He first set a deadline of Sept. 11. Then moved it way up to July 4 (which 
is this Sunday) or shortly thereafter. Several hundred troops will remain to provide security for diplomats. 
Biden said it was "time to end the forever war." But, of course, that war wasn't ending. America was simply pulling up stakes, a move that only encouraged more war 
as the Taliban stepped up offensive operations. 
Taliban battlefield successes 
Militants have captured 50 district capitals since May 1, and they now control a third of the country and contest 42% more. Even their commanders are startled by 
their rapid battlefield successes. Meanwhile, battered and homeless Afghan women are fleeing shelters in the north to escape the brutally restrictive Taliban, and the 
rights of women across the country are in jeopardy.  
The latest U.S. intelligence assessment is that the government in Kabul could collapse in six months, according to The Wall Street Journal. And the top U.S. military 
commander in Afghanistan, Gen. Austin "Scott" Miller , told reporters Tuesday of his concern that the nation could devolve into civil war once NATO troops leave. 
Nor is there a guarantee terrorist organizations such as al-Qaida won't once again use Afghanistan as a base of operations. The Taliban and al-Qaida have never 
severed ties. With U.S. troops gone, America's ability to monitor terrorism activities will diminish.  
Evacuate Afghan interpreters 
The Biden administration is at least planning to evacuate thousands of Afghans who worked with U.S. forces and now risk retribution from the Taliban. They're to be 
taken to other countries while processing U.S. entry applications. The lives of 18,000 are at stake, along with about 53,000 family members.  
The task is daunting. Necessary. And the time to accomplish is quickly running out. 
But America is certainly leaving. "Afghans are going to have to decide their future," Biden said on June 25, as he met with Ghani and promised $266 million in 
humanitarian aid, $3.3 billion in security assistance and millions of COVID-19 shots. 
There's some talk in the Pentagon of seeking authorization to launch airstrikes from outside Afghanistan in support of beleaguered government forces. This would be 
important if it's approved by the White House, though logistically challenging. 
But Biden may say no. He seems hellbent to wash his hands of this forever war.  
So be it. But the reality is that should there be harrowing images of a democracy in its death throes beamed back into American mobile devices and living rooms in 
the months to come, the stain left on the Biden presidency will never go away.
